[[proof]]
name = "mathd_algebra_478"
iteration = 1
time = 23
proof = """
theorem mathd_algebra_478
(b h v : ℝ)
(h₀ : 0 < b ∧ 0 < h ∧ 0 < v)
(h₁ : v = 1 / 3 * (b * h))
(h₂ : b = 30)
(h₃ : h = 13 / 2) :
v = 65 := by
  have h_main : v = 65 := by
    have h₄ : v = 1 / 3 * (b * h) := h₁
    rw [h₄]
    rw [h₂, h₃]
    -- Substitute b = 30 and h = 13 / 2 into the equation
    norm_num
    <;>
    ring_nf
    <;>
    norm_num
    <;>
    linarith
  exact h_main
"""
outline = """
### Detailed Proof and Analysis

**Problem Breakdown:**
We are given:
1. \\( b > 0 \\), \\( h > 0 \\), \\( v > 0 \\).
2. \\( v = \\frac{1}{3} \\cdot (b \\cdot h) \\).
3. \\( b = 30 \\).
4. \\( h = \\frac{13}{2} \\).

We need to prove that \\( v = 65 \\).

**Approach:**
1. Substitute \\( b = 30 \\) and \\( h = \\frac{13}{2} \\) into the equation for \\( v \\).
2. Simplify the expression for \\( v \\):
   \\[
   v = \\frac{1}{3} \\cdot (30 \\cdot \\frac{13}{2}) = \\frac{1}{3} \\cdot 195 = 65.
   \\]
3. The result follows directly from the substitution and simplification.

**Detailed Steps:**
1. Substitute \\( b = 30 \\) and \\( h = \\frac{13}{2} \\) into \\( v = \\frac{1}{3} \\cdot (b \\cdot h) \\):
   \\[
   v = \\frac{1}{3} \\cdot (30 \\cdot \\frac{13}{2}).
   \\]
2. Simplify the multiplication inside the parentheses:
   \\[
   30 \\cdot \\frac{13}{2} = \\frac{30 \\cdot 13}{2} = \\frac{390}{2} = 195.
   \\]
3. Multiply by \\( \\frac{1}{3} \\):
   \\[
   v = \\frac{1}{3} \\cdot 195 = 65.
   \\]
4. The result is \\( v = 65 \\).

**Verification:**
- The given conditions are consistent with the problem statement.
- The arithmetic is straightforward and correct.

### Step 1: Abstract Plan

1. **Substitute the given values of \\( b \\) and \\( h \\) into the expression for \\( v \\).**
   - \\( v = \\frac{1}{3} \\cdot (30 \\cdot \\frac{13}{2}) \\).

2. **Simplify the expression inside the parentheses.**
   - \\( 30 \\cdot \\frac{13}{2} = \\frac{390}{2} = 195 \\).

3. **Multiply by \\( \\frac{1}{3} \\).**
   - \\( \\frac{1}{3} \\cdot 195 = 65 \\).

4. **Conclude that \\( v = 65 \\).**

### Step 2: Lean 4 `have` Statements

```lean4
theorem mathd_algebra_478
(b h v : ℝ)
(h₀ : 0 < b ∧ 0 < h ∧ 0 < v)
(h₁ : v = 1 / 3 * (b * h))
(h₂ : b = 30)
(h₃ : h = 13 / 2) :
v = 65 := by
  have h_main : v = 65 := by
    sorry
  sorry
```

### Explanation:
- The `have` statement `h_main` directly corresponds to the goal `v = 65`. The proof of `h_main` will use the given hypotheses to substitute and simplify the expression for `v`. The actual proof is straightforward arithmetic, but we mark it as `sorry` for now. The final goal is trivially satisfied by `h_main`, so we also `sorry` the final goal.

"""

[[proof]]
name = "numbertheory_4x3m7y3neq2003"
iteration = 2
time = 116
proof = """
theorem numbertheory_4x3m7y3neq2003
(x y : ℤ) :
4 * x^3 - 7 * y^3 ≠ 2003 := by
  have h_main : 4 * x^3 - 7 * y^3 ≠ 2003 := by
    intro h
    have h₁ := h
    rw [← Int.emod_add_ediv x 7] at h₁
    rw [← Int.emod_add_ediv y 4] at h₁
    ring_nf at h₁
    have h₂ := h₁
    have h₃ := h₁
    have h₄ : x % 7 = 0 ∨ x % 7 = 1 ∨ x % 7 = 2 ∨ x % 7 = 3 ∨ x % 7 = 4 ∨ x % 7 = 5 ∨ x % 7 = 6 := by
      omega
    have h₅ : y % 4 = 0 ∨ y % 4 = 1 ∨ y % 4 = 2 ∨ y % 4 = 3 := by
      omega
    rcases h₄ with (h₄ | h₄ | h₄ | h₄ | h₄ | h₄ | h₄) <;>
    rcases h₅ with (h₅ | h₅ | h₅ | h₅) <;>
    simp [h₄, h₅, pow_three, Int.mul_emod, Int.sub_emod, Int.add_emod, Int.emod_emod] at h₁ h₂ h₃ <;>
    (try omega) <;>
    (try {
      have h₆ : x % 7 = 0 ∨ x % 7 = 1 ∨ x % 7 = 2 ∨ x % 7 = 3 ∨ x % 7 = 4 ∨ x % 7 = 5 ∨ x % 7 = 6 := by omega
      rcases h₆ with (h₆ | h₆ | h₆ | h₆ | h₆ | h₆ | h₆) <;>
      simp [h₆, pow_three, Int.mul_emod, Int.sub_emod, Int.add_emod, Int.emod_emod] at h₁ h₂ h₃ <;>
      omega
    }) <;>
    (try {
      have h₆ : y % 4 = 0 ∨ y % 4 = 1 ∨ y % 4 = 2 ∨ y % 4 = 3 := by omega
      rcases h₆ with (h₆ | h₆ | h₆ | h₆) <;>
      simp [h₆, pow_three, Int.mul_emod, Int.sub_emod, Int.add_emod, Int.emod_emod] at h₁ h₂ h₃ <;>
      omega
    }) <;>
    omega
  exact h_main
"""
outline = """
### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, we need to prove that there are no integers \\( x, y \\) such that \\( 4x^3 - 7y^3 = 2003 \\).

#### Step 1: Understand the equation modulo 7
We can analyze the equation modulo 7 to find constraints on \\( x \\) and \\( y \\).

1. Compute \\( 4x^3 \\mod 7 \\):
   - The possible residues of \\( x \\mod 7 \\) are \\( 0, 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6 \\).
   - Compute \\( 4x^3 \\mod 7 \\):
     - \\( x \\equiv 0 \\mod 7 \\): \\( 4x^3 \\equiv 0 \\mod 7 \\).
     - \\( x \\equiv 1 \\mod 7 \\): \\( 4 \\cdot 1 \\equiv 4 \\mod 7 \\).
     - \\( x \\equiv 2 \\mod 7 \\): \\( 4 \\cdot 8 \\equiv 32 \\equiv 4 \\mod 7 \\) (since \\( 32 - 4 \\cdot 7 = 32 - 28 = 4 \\)).
     - \\( x \\equiv 3 \\mod 7 \\): \\( 4 \\cdot 27 \\equiv 108 \\equiv 108 - 15 \\cdot 7 = 108 - 105 = 3 \\mod 7 \\).
     - \\( x \\equiv 4 \\mod 7 \\): \\( 4 \\cdot 64 \\equiv 256 \\equiv 256 - 36 \\cdot 7 = 256 - 252 = 4 \\mod 7 \\).
     - \\( x \\equiv 5 \\mod 7 \\): \\( 4 \\cdot 125 \\equiv 500 \\equiv 500 - 71 \\cdot 7 = 500 - 497 = 3 \\mod 7 \\).
     - \\( x \\equiv 6 \\mod 7 \\): \\( 4 \\cdot 216 \\equiv 864 \\equiv 864 - 123 \\cdot 7 = 864 - 861 = 3 \\mod 7 \\).
   - Thus, \\( 4x^3 \\mod 7 \\) can be \\( 0, 3, 4 \\).

2. Compute \\( -7y^3 \\mod 7 \\):
   - \\( -7y^3 \\equiv 0 \\mod 7 \\), since \\( 7 \\equiv 0 \\mod 7 \\).

3. The equation \\( 4x^3 - 7y^3 \\equiv 2003 \\mod 7 \\) becomes:
   - \\( 4x^3 \\equiv 2003 \\mod 7 \\).
   - Compute \\( 2003 \\mod 7 \\):
     - \\( 7 \\times 286 = 2002 \\), so \\( 2003 \\equiv 1 \\mod 7 \\).
   - Thus, \\( 4x^3 \\equiv 1 \\mod 7 \\).

4. From the earlier computation of \\( 4x^3 \\mod 7 \\), the only possibility is \\( x \\equiv 2 \\mod 7 \\).

#### Step 2: Substitute \\( x = 7k + 2 \\)
Since \\( x \\equiv 2 \\mod 7 \\), we can write \\( x = 7k + 2 \\) for some integer \\( k \\). Substitute this into the original equation:
\\[ 4(7k + 2)^3 - 7y^3 = 2003. \\]

Expand \\( (7k + 2)^3 \\):
\\[ (7k + 2)^3 = 343k^3 + 441k^2 + 189k + 8. \\]
Multiply by 4:
\\[ 4(7k + 2)^3 = 1372k^3 + 1764k^2 + 756k + 32. \\]
Thus, the equation becomes:
\\[ 1372k^3 + 1764k^2 + 756k + 32 - 7y^3 = 2003. \\]
Simplify:
\\[ 1372k^3 + 1764k^2 + 756k - 7y^3 = 1971. \\]
Divide by 7:
\\[ 196k^3 + 252k^2 + 108k - y^3 = 281.571... \\]
This is not directly helpful, so we instead consider the equation modulo 8.

#### Step 3: Analyze modulo 8
We can also analyze the equation modulo 8 to find constraints.

1. Compute \\( 4x^3 \\mod 8 \\):
   - The possible residues of \\( x \\mod 8 \\) are \\( 0, 1, 2, \\dots, 7 \\).
   - \\( x^3 \\mod 8 \\) cycles as follows:
     - \\( x \\equiv 0 \\mod 8 \\): \\( x^3 \\equiv 0 \\mod 8 \\).
     - \\( x \\equiv 1 \\mod 8 \\): \\( x^3 \\equiv 1 \\mod 8 \\).
     - \\( x \\equiv 2 \\mod 8 \\): \\( x^3 \\equiv 8 \\equiv 0 \\mod 8 \\).
     - \\( x \\equiv 3 \\mod 8 \\): \\( x^3 \\equiv 27 \\equiv 3 \\mod 8 \\).
     - \\( x \\equiv 4 \\mod 8 \\): \\( x^3 \\equiv 64 \\equiv 0 \\mod 8 \\).
     - \\( x \\equiv 5 \\mod 8 \\): \\( x^3 \\equiv 125 \\equiv 5 \\mod 8 \\).
     - \\( x \\equiv 6 \\mod 8 \\): \\( x^3 \\equiv 216 \\equiv 4 \\mod 8 \\).
     - \\( x \\equiv 7 \\mod 8 \\): \\( x^3 \\equiv 343 \\equiv 7 \\mod 8 \\).
   - Thus, \\( 4x^3 \\mod 8 \\) can be:
     - \\( 0 \\), \\( 4 \\), or \\( 0 \\) (if \\( x \\equiv 0, 2, 4 \\mod 8 \\)).
     - \\( 4 \\), \\( 0 \\), or \\( 4 \\) (if \\( x \\equiv 1, 3, 5, 7 \\mod 8 \\)).

2. Compute \\( -7y^3 \\mod 8 \\):
   - \\( -7 \\equiv 1 \\mod 8 \\), so \\( -7y^3 \\equiv y^3 \\mod 8 \\).

3. The equation \\( 4x^3 - 7y^3 \\equiv 2003 \\mod 8 \\) becomes:
   - \\( 4x^3 + y^3 \\equiv 2003 \\mod 8 \\).
   - Compute \\( 2003 \\mod 8 \\):
     - \\( 8 \\times 250 = 2000 \\), so \\( 2003 \\equiv 3 \\mod 8 \\).
   - Thus, \\( 4x^3 + y^3 \\equiv 3 \\mod 8 \\).

4. We can enumerate all possible residues of \\( x^3 \\mod 8 \\) and \\( y^3 \\mod 8 \\):
   - \\( x^3 \\mod 8 \\): \\( 0, 1, 0, 3, 0, 5, 4, 7 \\).
   - \\( y^3 \\mod 8 \\): \\( 0, 1, 0, 3, 0, 1, 0, 7 \\).
   - The possible combinations of \\( 4x^3 + y^3 \\mod 8 \\) are:
     - \\( x^3 \\equiv 0 \\): \\( 0 + y^3 \\equiv 3 \\mod 8 \\), so \\( y^3 \\equiv 3 \\mod 8 \\).
       - \\( y^3 \\equiv 3 \\mod 8 \\) is impossible because \\( y^3 \\mod 8 \\) is \\( 0, 1, 0, 3, 0, 1, 0, 7 \\), and \\( 3 \\) is not in this list.
     - \\( x^3 \\equiv 1 \\): \\( 4 + y^3 \\equiv 3 \\mod 8 \\), so \\( y^3 \\equiv -1 \\equiv 7 \\mod 8 \\).
       - \\( y^3 \\equiv 7 \\mod 8 \\) is possible (e.g., \\( y \\equiv 7 \\mod 8 \\)).
     - \\( x^3 \\equiv 3 \\): \\( 12 + y^3 \\equiv 3 \\mod 8 \\), so \\( y^3 \\equiv -9 \\equiv -1 \\equiv 7 \\mod 8 \\).
       - Same as above.
     - \\( x^3 \\equiv 4 \\): \\( 16 + y^3 \\equiv 3 \\mod 8 \\), so \\( y^3 \\equiv -13 \\equiv -5 \\equiv 3 \\mod 8 \\).
       - \\( y^3 \\equiv 3 \\mod 8 \\) is impossible.
     - \\( x^3 \\equiv 5 \\): \\( 20 + y^3 \\equiv 3 \\mod 8 \\), so \\( y^3 \\equiv -17 \\equiv -1 \\equiv 7 \\mod 8 \\).
       - Same as above.
     - \\( x^3 \\equiv 6 \\): \\( 24 + y^3 \\equiv 3 \\mod 8 \\), so \\( y^3 \\equiv -21 \\equiv -5 \\equiv 3 \\mod 8 \\).
       - \\( y^3 \\equiv 3 \\mod 8 \\) is impossible.
     - \\( x^3 \\equiv 7 \\): \\( 28 + y^3 \\equiv 3 \\mod 8 \\), so \\( y^3 \\equiv -25 \\equiv -1 \\equiv 7 \\mod 8 \\).
       - Same as above.

   The only possible case is \\( x^3 \\equiv 1 \\mod 8 \\) and \\( y^3 \\equiv 7 \\mod 8 \\).

#### Step 4: Find \\( x \\mod 7 \\) and \\( y \\mod 4 \\)
From earlier, \\( x \\equiv 2 \\mod 7 \\).

Now, we can find \\( y \\mod 4 \\).

1. The equation is:
   \\[ 4x^3 - 7y^3 = 2003. \\]
   Since \\( x \\equiv 2 \\mod 7 \\), we can write \\( x = 7k + 2 \\).

2. Substitute \\( x = 7k + 2 \\):
   \\[ 4(7k + 2)^3 - 7y^3 = 2003. \\]
   Expand \\( (7k + 2)^3 \\):
   \\[ (7k + 2)^3 = 343k^3 + 441k^2 + 189k + 8. \\]
   Multiply by 4:
   \\[ 4(7k + 2)^3 = 1372k^3 + 1764k^2 + 756k + 32. \\]
   Thus:
   \\[ 1372k^3 + 1764k^2 + 756k + 32 - 7y^3 = 2003. \\]
   Simplify:
   \\[ 1372k^3 + 1764k^2 + 756k - 7y^3 = 1971. \\]
   Divide by 7:
   \\[ 196k^3 + 252k^2 + 108k - y^3 = 281.571... \\]
   This is not directly helpful, so we instead consider the equation modulo 4.

3. Compute \\( 4x^3 \\mod 4 \\):
   - \\( 4x^3 \\equiv 0 \\mod 4 \\).

4. Compute \\( -7y^3 \\mod 4 \\):
   - \\( -7 \\equiv -3 \\equiv 1 \\mod 4 \\), so \\( -7y^3 \\equiv y^3 \\mod 4 \\).

5. The equation \\( 4x^3 - 7y^3 \\equiv 2003 \\mod 4 \\) becomes:
   - \\( 0 - y^3 \\equiv 2003 \\mod 4 \\).
   - \\( 2003 \\mod 4 \\):
     - \\( 4 \\times 500 = 2000 \\), so \\( 2003 \\equiv 3 \\mod 4 \\).
   - Thus, \\( -y^3 \\equiv 3 \\mod 4 \\), or \\( y^3 \\equiv 1 \\mod 4 \\).

6. The possible residues of \\( y^3 \\mod 4 \\) are:
   - If \\( y \\) is even, \\( y \\equiv 0 \\mod 2 \\), then \\( y^3 \\equiv 0 \\mod 4 \\).
   - If \\( y \\) is odd, \\( y \\equiv 1 \\mod 2 \\), then \\( y^3 \\equiv 1 \\mod 4 \\).
   - Thus, \\( y^3 \\equiv 1 \\mod 4 \\) is possible only if \\( y \\) is odd.

#### Step 5: Bounding the possible values
We can bound the possible values of \\( x \\) and \\( y \\).

1. The equation is:
   \\[ 4x^3 - 7y^3 = 2003. \\]
   Since \\( x^3 \\) is divisible by 4 when \\( x \\) is even, and \\( y^3 \\) is divisible by 7 when \\( y \\) is divisible by 7, we can consider cases based on the parity of \\( x \\) and \\( y \\).

2. Assume \\( x \\) is even:
   - Let \\( x = 2k \\).
   - The equation becomes:
     \\[ 4(8k^3) - 7y^3 = 2003, \\]
     \\[ 32k^3 - 7y^3 = 2003. \\]
     - \\( 32k^3 \\geq 2003 \\) implies \\( k \\geq 2 \\) (since \\( 32 \\times 1^3 = 32 < 2003 \\), \\( 32 \\times 2^3 = 256 \\), \\( 32 \\times 3^3 = 864 \\), etc.).
     - For \\( k = 2 \\):
       \\[ 32 \\times 8 - 7y^3 = 2003, \\]
       \\[ 256 - 7y^3 = 2003, \\]
       \\[ 7y^3 = 256 - 2003 = -1747, \\]
       \\[ y^3 = -1747 / 7 \\approx -249.571 \\), which is not an integer.
     - For \\( k = 3 \\):
       \\[ 32 \\times 27 - 7y^3 = 2003, \\]
       \\[ 864 - 7y^3 = 2003, \\]
       \\[ 7y^3 = 864 - 2003 = -1139, \\]
       \\[ y^3 = -1139 / 7 \\approx -162.714 \\), not an integer.
     - For \\( k \\geq 3 \\), \\( 32k^3 \\) grows rapidly, and \\( 7y^3 = 32k^3 - 2003 \\) becomes negative or too large to be a perfect cube.

3. Assume \\( x \\) is odd:
   - Let \\( x = 2k + 1 \\).
   - The equation becomes:
     \\[ 4(8k^3 + 12k^2 + 6k + 1) - 7y^3 = 2003, \\]
     \\[ 32k^3 + 48k^2 + 24k + 4 - 7y^3 = 2003, \\]
     \\[ 32k^3 + 48k^2 + 24k - 7y^3 = 1999. \\]
     - For \\( k = 0 \\):
       \\[ 0 + 0 + 0 - 7y^3 = 1999, \\]
       \\[ y^3 = -1999 / 7 \\approx -285.571 \\), not an integer.
     - For \\( k = 1 \\):
       \\[ 32 + 48 + 24 - 7y^3 = 1999, \\]
       \\[ 104 - 7y^3 = 1999, \\]
       \\[ 7y^3 = 104 - 1999 = -1895, \\]
       \\[ y^3 = -1895 / 7 \\approx -270.714 \\), not an integer.
     - For \\( k \\geq 1 \\), the left-hand side grows rapidly, and \\( 7y^3 = 32k^3 + 48k^2 + 24k - 1999 \\) becomes negative or too large to be a perfect cube.

#### Step 6: Conclusion
After checking small values and bounding, we find that there are no integer solutions to \\( 4x^3 - 7y^3 = 2003 \\).

### Step 7: Abstract Plan

1. **Modulo 7 Analysis**:
   - Compute \\( 4x^3 \\mod 7 \\) and find that \\( x \\equiv 2 \\mod 7 \\) is the only possibility.
   - Substitute \\( x = 7k + 2 \\) and analyze further.

2. **Modulo 4 Analysis**:
   - Compute \\( 4x^3 - 7y^3 \\mod 4 \\) to find \\( y^3 \\equiv 1 \\mod 4 \\), implying \\( y \\) is odd.

3. **Bounding and Case Analysis**:
   - Check small values of \\( x \\) and \\( y \\) to find no solutions.
   - For larger values, the left-hand side grows too rapidly to match the right-hand side.

### Lean 4 `have` Statements

```lean4
theorem numbertheory_4x3m7y3neq2003
(x y : ℤ) :
4 * x^3 - 7 * y^3 ≠ 2003 := by
  have h_main : 4 * x^3 - 7 * y^3 ≠ 2003 := by
    sorry
  sorry
```

"""

[[proof]]
name = "aime_1983_p1"
iteration = 3
time = 113
proof = """
theorem aime_1983_p1
(x y z w : ℕ)
(ht : 1 < x ∧ 1 < y ∧ 1 < z)
(hw : 0 ≤ w)
(h0 : Real.log w / Real.log x = 24)
(h1 : Real.log w / Real.log y = 40)
(h2 : Real.log w / Real.log (x * y * z) = 12):
Real.log w / Real.log z = 60 := by
  have hx : (x : ℝ) > 1 := by
    have hx' : (1 : ℝ) < x := by
      norm_cast
      <;> linarith
    exact hx'
  
  have hy : (y : ℝ) > 1 := by
    have hy' : (1 : ℝ) < y := by
      norm_cast
      <;> linarith
    exact hy'
  
  have hz : (z : ℝ) > 1 := by
    have hz' : (1 : ℝ) < z := by
      norm_cast
      <;> linarith
    exact hz'
  
  have hw_pos : (w : ℝ) > 0 := by
    by_contra h
    have h' : (w : ℝ) ≤ 0 := by linarith
    have h₁ : Real.log w = 0 := by
      have h₂ : w = 0 := by
        by_contra h₃
        have h₄ : w ≥ 1 := by
          omega
        have h₅ : (w : ℝ) ≥ 1 := by exact_mod_cast h₄
        have h₆ : (w : ℝ) > 0 := by linarith
        nlinarith
      simp [h₂]
      <;> simp_all [Real.log_zero]
      <;> norm_num
      <;> linarith
    have h₂ : Real.log w / Real.log x = 0 := by
      rw [h₁]
      simp
      <;> field_simp [Real.log_ne_zero_of_pos_of_ne_one (by positivity : (0 : ℝ) < x) (by
        norm_num
        <;> linarith [hx]
        )]
      <;> ring_nf
      <;> simp_all
      <;> linarith
    linarith
  
  have h3 : Real.log x ≠ 0 := by
    have h3 : Real.log x > 0 := Real.log_pos (by linarith)
    linarith
  
  have h4 : Real.log y ≠ 0 := by
    have h4 : Real.log y > 0 := Real.log_pos (by linarith)
    linarith
  
  have h5 : Real.log z ≠ 0 := by
    have h5 : Real.log z > 0 := Real.log_pos (by linarith)
    linarith
  
  have h6 : Real.log w = 24 * Real.log x := by
    have h6 : Real.log w / Real.log x = 24 := h0
    have h7 : Real.log w = 24 * Real.log x := by
      have h8 : Real.log x ≠ 0 := h3
      field_simp at h6 ⊢
      <;> nlinarith
    exact h7
  
  have h7 : Real.log w = 40 * Real.log y := by
    have h7 : Real.log w / Real.log y = 40 := h1
    have h8 : Real.log w = 40 * Real.log y := by
      have h9 : Real.log y ≠ 0 := h4
      field_simp at h7 ⊢
      <;> nlinarith
    exact h8
  
  have h8 : 3 * Real.log x = 5 * Real.log y := by
    have h8 : Real.log w = 24 * Real.log x := h6
    have h9 : Real.log w = 40 * Real.log y := h7
    have h10 : 24 * Real.log x = 40 * Real.log y := by linarith
    have h11 : 3 * Real.log x = 5 * Real.log y := by
      linarith
    exact h11
  
  have h9 : Real.log (x * y * z) = Real.log x + Real.log y + Real.log z := by
    have h9 : Real.log (x * y * z) = Real.log (x * y * z) := rfl
    rw [h9]
    have h10 : Real.log (x * y * z) = Real.log (x * y * z) := rfl
    rw [h10]
    have h11 : Real.log (x * y * z) = Real.log x + Real.log y + Real.log z := by
      have h12 : Real.log (x * y * z) = Real.log (x * y) + Real.log z := by
        rw [Real.log_mul (by positivity) (by positivity)]
        <;> ring_nf
      have h13 : Real.log (x * y) = Real.log x + Real.log y := by
        rw [Real.log_mul (by positivity) (by positivity)]
        <;> ring_nf
      rw [h12, h13]
      <;> ring_nf
    rw [h11]
    <;> ring_nf
  
  have h10 : 2 * Real.log x = Real.log x + Real.log y + Real.log z := by
    have h10 : Real.log w / Real.log (x * y * z) = 12 := h2
    have h11 : Real.log (x * y * z) = Real.log x + Real.log y + Real.log z := h9
    have h12 : Real.log w = 24 * Real.log x := h6
    rw [h11] at h10
    have h13 : (24 * Real.log x) / (Real.log x + Real.log y + Real.log z) = 12 := by simpa [h12] using h10
    have h14 : Real.log x + Real.log y + Real.log z ≠ 0 := by
      by_contra h
      rw [h] at h13
      norm_num at h13
      <;> simp_all [div_eq_mul_inv]
      <;> field_simp at *
      <;> nlinarith [Real.log_pos (by linarith : (1 : ℝ) < x), Real.log_pos (by linarith : (1 : ℝ) < y), Real.log_pos (by linarith : (1 : ℝ) < z)]
    have h15 : 24 * Real.log x = 12 * (Real.log x + Real.log y + Real.log z) := by
      field_simp at h13
      nlinarith
    nlinarith
  
  have h11 : Real.log x = Real.log y + Real.log z := by
    have h11 : 2 * Real.log x = Real.log x + Real.log y + Real.log z := h10
    linarith
  
  have h12 : 3 * Real.log y + 3 * Real.log z = 5 * Real.log y := by
    have h12 : 3 * Real.log x = 5 * Real.log y := h8
    have h13 : Real.log x = Real.log y + Real.log z := h11
    nlinarith
  
  have h13 : Real.log z = (2/3) * Real.log y := by
    have h13 : 3 * Real.log y + 3 * Real.log z = 5 * Real.log y := h12
    have h14 : 3 * Real.log z = 2 * Real.log y := by linarith
    have h15 : Real.log z = (2/3) * Real.log y := by
      ring_nf at h14 ⊢
      nlinarith
    exact h15
  
  have h14 : Real.log w = 40 * Real.log y := by
    have h14 : Real.log w = 40 * Real.log y := by
      linarith
    exact h14
  
  have h15 : Real.log w / Real.log z = 60 := by
    have h15 : Real.log w = 40 * Real.log y := h14
    have h16 : Real.log z = (2/3) * Real.log y := h13
    have h17 : Real.log w / Real.log z = 60 := by
      rw [h15, h16]
      have h18 : (40 * Real.log y : ℝ) / ((2 / 3) * Real.log y) = 60 := by
        have h19 : Real.log y ≠ 0 := h4
        field_simp [h19]
        <;> ring_nf
        <;> field_simp [h19]
        <;> nlinarith
      exact h18
    exact h17
  
  simpa using h15

### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, recall the given conditions:
1. \\( x, y, z > 1 \\) (as natural numbers)
2. \\( w \\geq 0 \\) (as a natural number)
3. \\(\\frac{\\log w}{\\log x} = 24\\)
4. \\(\\frac{\\log w}{\\log y} = 40\\)
5. \\(\\frac{\\log w}{\\log (x \\cdot y \\cdot z)} = 12\\)

We need to prove that \\(\\frac{\\log w}{\\log z} = 60\\).

#### Key Observations:
1. Since \\(x, y, z > 1\\) and \\(w \\geq 0\\), the logarithms are well-defined (as long as \\(w \\neq 0\\), but \\(w = 0\\) would make \\(\\log w\\) undefined in the real numbers. However, Lean's `Real.log` is defined for \\(w = 0\\) as `0` (but this is a special case). But in the problem, \\(w\\) is a natural number, and Lean's `Real.log` is defined for \\(w = 0\\) as `0` (but the hypothesis `h0` would imply `0 / Real.log x = 24`, i.e., `0 = 24`, which is false. So, we can assume \\(w > 0\\) because otherwise the hypotheses are contradictory. But Lean's `Real.log` is defined for `w = 0` as `0`, so we must be careful. However, the hypothesis `h0` would imply `0 = 24` if `w = 0`, which is false. Thus, we can safely assume `w > 0` because `w = 0` leads to a contradiction.

   But wait, Lean's `Real.log` is defined for `w = 0` as `0`, so `h0` becomes `0 / Real.log x = 24`, i.e., `0 = 24`, which is false. Hence, `w > 0` is implied by the hypotheses. Similarly, `x, y, z > 1` is given, and `w > 0` is also implied.

   But in Lean, `Real.log 0 = 0` is true, so `h0` would be `0 / Real.log x = 24` if `w = 0`, i.e., `0 = 24`, which is false. Hence, `w > 0` is implied by the hypotheses.

   But in the problem, `w` is a natural number, and `Real.log w` is `Real.log` of `(w : ℝ)`. The hypotheses are:
   - `h0 : Real.log w / Real.log x = 24`
   - `h1 : Real.log w / Real.log y = 40`
   - `h2 : Real.log w / Real.log (x * y * z) = 12`

   We can deduce that `w > 0` because if `w = 0`, then `Real.log w = 0`, and `h0` would become `0 = 24`, which is false. Similarly, `x, y, z > 1` is given, so `Real.log x, Real.log y, Real.log (x * y * z) > 0` (since `x, y, z > 1`).

   Therefore, all denominators are positive, and the hypotheses are well-defined.

2. From `h0` and `h1`, we can write:
   \\[
   \\log w = 24 \\log x \\quad \\text{and} \\quad \\log w = 40 \\log y.
   \\]
   This gives:
   \\[
   24 \\log x = 40 \\log y \\implies 6 \\log x = 10 \\log y \\implies 3 \\log x = 5 \\log y.
   \\]
   Simplifying further:
   \\[
   \\log x = \\frac{5}{3} \\log y.
   \\]

3. From `h2`, we have:
   \\[
   \\frac{\\log w}{\\log (x \\cdot y \\cdot z)} = 12.
   \\]
   Since \\(\\log (x \\cdot y \\cdot z) = \\log x + \\log y + \\log z\\), we get:
   \\[
   \\frac{\\log w}{\\log x + \\log y + \\log z} = 12.
   \\]
   Substitute \\(\\log w = 24 \\log x\\) (from `h0`):
   \\[
   \\frac{24 \\log x}{\\log x + \\log y + \\log z} = 12.
   \\]
   Simplify:
   \\[
   24 \\log x = 12 (\\log x + \\log y + \\log z) \\implies 2 \\log x = \\log x + \\log y + \\log z.
   \\]
   Rearrange:
   \\[
   \\log x - \\log y - \\log z = 0 \\implies \\log x = \\log y + \\log z.
   \\]
   But from earlier, \\(\\log x = \\frac{5}{3} \\log y\\). Substitute this into the new equation:
   \\[
   \\frac{5}{3} \\log y = \\log y + \\log z.
   \\]
   Rearrange:
   \\[
   \\frac{5}{3} \\log y - \\log y = \\log z \\implies \\frac{2}{3} \\log y = \\log z.
   \\]
   Thus:
   \\[
   \\log z = \\frac{2}{3} \\log y.
   \\]
   But from `h0`, \\(\\log w = 24 \\log x = 24 \\cdot \\frac{5}{3} \\log y = 40 \\log y\\). Therefore:
   \\[
   \\frac{\\log w}{\\log z} = \\frac{40 \\log y}{\\frac{2}{3} \\log y} = \\frac{40 \\log y}{\\frac{2}{3} \\log y} = 40 \\cdot \\frac{3}{2} = 60.
   \\]
   This is the desired result.

#### Step-by-Step Abstract Plan:
1. From `h0` and `h1`, derive `24 * Real.log x = 40 * Real.log y` and simplify to `3 * Real.log x = 5 * Real.log y`.
2. From `h2`, derive `24 * Real.log x = 12 * (Real.log x + Real.log y + Real.log z)`, and simplify to `2 * Real.log x = Real.log x + Real.log y + Real.log z`.
3. Substitute `3 * Real.log x = 5 * Real.log y` into the simplified `h2` equation to get `Real.log x = Real.log y + Real.log z`.
4. Substitute `Real.log x = Real.log y + Real.log z` into `3 * Real.log x = 5 * Real.log y` to get `3 * (Real.log y + Real.log z) = 5 * Real.log y`.
5. Simplify to `3 * Real.log y + 3 * Real.log z = 5 * Real.log y` and further to `3 * Real.log z = 2 * Real.log y`, i.e., `Real.log z = (2/3) * Real.log y`.
6. From `h0`, `Real.log w = 24 * Real.log x = 24 * (5/3) * Real.log y = 40 * Real.log y`.
7. Substitute `Real.log w` and `Real.log z` into the goal to get `(40 * Real.log y) / ((2/3) * Real.log y) = 60`.

### Lean 4 Proof Sketch with `have` Statements

```lean4
theorem aime_1983_p1
(x y z w : ℕ)
(ht : 1 < x ∧ 1 < y ∧ 1 < z)
(hw : 0 ≤ w)
(h0 : Real.log w / Real.log x = 24)
(h1 : Real.log w / Real.log y = 40)
(h2 : Real.log w / Real.log (x * y * z) = 12):
Real.log w / Real.log z = 60 := by
  have hx : (x : ℝ) > 1 := by sorry
  have hy : (y : ℝ) > 1 := by sorry
  have hz : (z : ℝ) > 1 := by sorry
  have hw_pos : (w : ℝ) > 0 := by sorry
  have h3 : Real.log x ≠ 0 := by sorry
  have h4 : Real.log y ≠ 0 := by sorry
  have h5 : Real.log z ≠ 0 := by sorry
  have h6 : Real.log w = 24 * Real.log x := by sorry
  have h7 : Real.log w = 40 * Real.log y := by sorry
  have h8 : 3 * Real.log x = 5 * Real.log y := by sorry
  have h9 : Real.log (x * y * z) = Real.log x + Real.log y + Real.log z := by sorry
  have h10 : 2 * Real.log x = Real.log x + Real.log y + Real.log z := by sorry
  have h11 : Real.log x = Real.log y + Real.log z := by sorry
  have h12 : 3 * Real.log y + 3 * Real.log z = 5 * Real.log y := by sorry
  have h13 : Real.log z = (2/3) * Real.log y := by sorry
  have h14 : Real.log w = 40 * Real.log y := by sorry
  have h15 : Real.log w / Real.log z = 60 := by sorry
  sorry
```

### Explanation:
1. `hx`, `hy`, `hz`: Convert the inequalities from `ℕ` to `ℝ` to work with logarithms.
2. `hw_pos`: Prove that `w > 0` (since `w` is a natural number and the hypotheses are contradictory otherwise).
3. `h3`, `h4`, `h5`: The logarithms of `x`, `y`, `z` are non-zero because `x, y, z > 1`.
4. `h6`, `h7`: Rewrite the hypotheses using the definition of division.
5. `h8`: Derive the relationship between `log x` and `log y` from `h6` and `h7`.
6. `h9`: The logarithm of a product is the sum of the logarithms.
7. `h10`: Substitute `h9` into `h2` and simplify to get `h10`.
8. `h11`: Rearrange `h10` to get `log x = log y + log z`.
9. `h12`: Substitute `h8` into `h11` to get `3 * log y + 3 * log z = 5 * log y`.
10. `h13`: Solve for `log z` in terms of `log y`.
11. `h14`: Substitute `h6` into `h14` to get `log w = 40 * log y`.
12. `h15`: Substitute `h13` and `h14` into the goal to get `60`.

[[proof]]
name = "mathd_algebra_141"
iteration = 5
time = 22
proof = """
theorem mathd_algebra_141
(a b : ℝ)
(h₁ : (a * b)=180)
(h₂ : 2 * (a + b)=54) :
(a^2 + b^2) = 369 := by
  have h_sum : a + b = 27 := by
    have h₃ : 2 * (a + b) = 54 := h₂
    -- Divide both sides by 2 to solve for a + b
    have h₄ : a + b = 27 := by
      linarith
    exact h₄
  
  have h_main : a^2 + b^2 = 369 := by
    have h₃ : a^2 + b^2 = 369 := by
      have h₄ : a^2 + b^2 = (a + b)^2 - 2 * (a * b) := by
        ring
      rw [h₄]
      rw [h_sum]
      rw [h₁]
      <;> norm_num
      <;> nlinarith
    exact h₃
  
  exact h_main
"""
outline = """
### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, we are given:
1. \\( a \\cdot b = 180 \\)
2. \\( 2(a + b) = 54 \\)

We need to prove that \\( a^2 + b^2 = 369 \\).

#### Step 1: Simplify the Second Equation
The second equation can be simplified by dividing both sides by 2:
\\[ a + b = 27 \\]

#### Step 2: Use the Identity for \\( a^2 + b^2 \\)
We know that:
\\[ a^2 + b^2 = (a + b)^2 - 2ab \\]

Substitute the known values:
\\[ a^2 + b^2 = (27)^2 - 2 \\cdot 180 \\]
\\[ a^2 + b^2 = 729 - 360 \\]
\\[ a^2 + b^2 = 369 \\]

This directly gives the desired result.

#### Verification
Alternatively, we can directly compute \\( a^2 + b^2 \\) using the quadratic formula. However, the above method is more straightforward.

### Step-by-Step Abstract Plan

1. **Simplify the Linear Equation**:
   - Divide both sides of \\( 2(a + b) = 54 \\) by 2 to get \\( a + b = 27 \\).

2. **Use the Identity for \\( a^2 + b^2 \\)**:
   - Recall that \\( a^2 + b^2 = (a + b)^2 - 2ab \\).
   - Substitute the known values \\( a + b = 27 \\) and \\( ab = 180 \\) to get \\( a^2 + b^2 = 27^2 - 2 \\cdot 180 \\).

3. **Calculate the Result**:
   - Compute \\( 27^2 = 729 \\).
   - Compute \\( 2 \\cdot 180 = 360 \\).
   - Subtract to get \\( 729 - 360 = 369 \\).

4. **Final Result**:
   - The result is \\( a^2 + b^2 = 369 \\).

### Lean 4 Proof Sketch with `have` Statements

```lean4
theorem mathd_algebra_141
(a b : ℝ)
(h₁ : (a * b)=180)
(h₂ : 2 * (a + b)=54) :
(a^2 + b^2) = 369 := by
  have h_sum : a + b = 27 := by sorry
  have h_main : a^2 + b^2 = 369 := by sorry
  sorry
```

### Explanation of `have` Statements:
1. `h_sum`: This directly corresponds to simplifying the linear equation \\( 2(a + b) = 54 \\) to \\( a + b = 27 \\).
2. `h_main`: This corresponds to the main goal \\( a^2 + b^2 = 369 \\), which is derived using the identity \\( a^2 + b^2 = (a + b)^2 - 2ab \\).

"""

name = "mathd_numbertheory_3"
iteration = 6
time = 23
proof = """
theorem mathd_numbertheory_3 :
(∑ x ∈ Finset.range 10, ((x + 1)^2)) % 10 = 5 := by
  have h_sum : (∑ x ∈ Finset.range 10, ((x + 1)^2)) = 385 := by
    decide
  
  have h_mod : (∑ x ∈ Finset.range 10, ((x + 1)^2)) % 10 = 5 := by
    rw [h_sum]
    <;> norm_num
    <;> rfl
  
  exact h_mod
"""
outline = """
### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, we need to compute the sum `∑ x ∈ Finset.range 10, ((x + 1)^2)` modulo 10. 

1. **Understand the Sum**:
   - `Finset.range 10` is the set `{0, 1, ..., 9}`.
   - The sum is `∑ x ∈ {0, ..., 9}, (x + 1)^2`.
   - For each `x` in `{0, ..., 9}`, `x + 1` is in `{1, ..., 10}`, and `(x + 1)^2` is `1, 4, 9, 16, 25, 36, 49, 64, 81, 100`.

2. **Compute the Sum**:
   - `∑ x ∈ {0, ..., 9}, (x + 1)^2 = 1 + 4 + 9 + 16 + 25 + 36 + 49 + 64 + 81 + 100 = 385`.

3. **Compute Modulo 10**:
   - `385 % 10 = 5` because `385 = 10 * 38 + 5`.

Alternatively, we can compute the sum directly modulo 10:
   - `1^2 = 1`
   - `2^2 = 4`
   - `3^2 = 9`
   - `4^2 = 16 ≡ 6 mod 10`
   - `5^2 = 25 ≡ 5 mod 10`
   - `6^2 = 36 ≡ 6 mod 10`
   - `7^2 = 49 ≡ 9 mod 10`
   - `8^2 = 64 ≡ 4 mod 10`
   - `9^2 = 81 ≡ 1 mod 10`
   - `10^2 = 100 ≡ 0 mod 10`
   - Summing them up: `1 + 4 + 9 + 6 + 5 + 6 + 9 + 4 + 1 + 0 = 45 ≡ 5 mod 10`.

But the first method is simpler, so we'll use it.

### Step 1: Abstract Plan

1. **Compute the Sum**:
   - Calculate `∑ x ∈ Finset.range 10, (x + 1)^2 = 385`.

2. **Compute Modulo 10**:
   - `385 % 10 = 5`.

### Step 2: Lean 4 `have` Statements

```lean4
theorem mathd_numbertheory_3 :
(∑ x ∈ Finset.range 10, ((x + 1)^2)) % 10 = 5 := by
  have h_sum : (∑ x ∈ Finset.range 10, ((x + 1)^2)) = 385 := by sorry
  have h_mod : (∑ x ∈ Finset.range 10, ((x + 1)^2)) % 10 = 5 := by sorry
  sorry
```

### Explanation:
1. `h_sum`: We first compute the exact sum of the squares of `(x + 1)` for `x` from `0` to `9` and find it is `385`.
2. `h_mod`: We then compute `385 % 10 = 5` to get the final result.

"""

[[proof]]
name = "mathd_algebra_209"
iteration = 8
time = 62
proof = """
theorem mathd_algebra_209
(σ : Equiv ℝ ℝ)
(h₀ : σ.2 2 = 10)
(h₁ : σ.2 10 = 1)
(h₂ : σ.2 1 = 2) :
σ.1 (σ.1 10) = 1 := by
  have h_σ1_10 : σ.1 10 = 2 := by
    have h₃ : σ.1 (σ.2 2) = 2 := by
      apply σ.apply_symm_apply
    have h₄ : σ.2 2 = 10 := h₀
    have h₅ : σ.1 10 = 2 := by
      calc
        σ.1 10 = σ.1 (σ.2 2) := by rw [h₄]
        _ = 2 := by rw [h₃]
    exact h₅
  
  have h_main : σ.1 (σ.1 10) = 1 := by
    have h₃ : σ.1 (σ.1 10) = σ.1 (σ.2 1) := by
      rw [h_σ1_10]
      <;> simp [h₂]
      <;> norm_num
    rw [h₃]
    have h₄ : σ.1 (σ.2 1) = 1 := by
      apply σ.apply_symm_apply
    rw [h₄]
    <;> simp [h₂]
    <;> norm_num
  
  exact h_main
"""
outline = """
### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, recall that `σ : Equiv ℝ ℝ` is an equivalence (bijection) from `ℝ` to `ℝ`. This means that `σ` is a function with a two-sided inverse, and its inverse is also a function. The notation `σ.1` and `σ.2` is a bit confusing, but in Lean, `Equiv ℝ ℝ` is a structure with two fields:
1. `toFun : ℝ → ℝ` (the function `σ.1`).
2. `invFun : ℝ → ℝ` (the inverse function `σ.2`).

The `invFun` is the two-sided inverse of `toFun`, and vice versa. The `Equiv` structure also has a `right_inv` and `left_inv` field, but we won't need them here.

Given the hypotheses:
1. `σ.2 2 = 10`
2. `σ.2 10 = 1`
3. `σ.2 1 = 2`

We need to prove that `σ.1 (σ.1 10) = 1`.

#### Step 1: Understand the Hypotheses and Goal

The hypotheses tell us that:
- `σ.2 2 = 10`
- `σ.2 10 = 1`
- `σ.2 1 = 2`

The goal is to prove that `σ.1 (σ.1 10) = 1`.

#### Step 2: Use the Inverse Properties

Since `σ` is an equivalence, `σ.1` is a bijection, and `σ.2` is its inverse. The inverse properties are:
1. `σ.1 (σ.2 x) = x` for all `x` in the domain.
2. `σ.2 (σ.1 x) = x` for all `x` in the domain.

We can use these properties to simplify the goal.

#### Step 3: Simplify the Goal

We need to prove `σ.1 (σ.1 10) = 1`.

First, apply `σ.2` to both sides of the goal:
`σ.2 (σ.1 (σ.1 10)) = σ.2 1`

But by the inverse property `σ.2 (σ.1 x) = x`, we have:
`σ.2 (σ.1 (σ.1 10)) = σ.1 10`

Thus, the goal becomes:
`σ.1 10 = σ.2 1`

But from the hypotheses, we know `σ.2 1 = 2`, so we need to prove `σ.1 10 = 2`.

#### Step 4: Prove `σ.1 10 = 2`

We can use the inverse property again:
`σ.1 (σ.2 10) = 10`

But `σ.2 10 = 1` by hypothesis, so:
`σ.1 1 = 10`

This is not directly helpful, but we can use the inverse property again:
`σ.1 (σ.2 2) = 2`

But `σ.2 2 = 10` by hypothesis, so:
`σ.1 10 = 2`

This is exactly what we need to prove `σ.1 10 = 2`.

#### Step 5: Finalize the Proof

Now, recall that we have `σ.1 10 = 2` and `σ.2 1 = 2`. Thus:
`σ.1 10 = σ.2 1`

But earlier, we derived that `σ.2 (σ.1 (σ.1 10)) = σ.1 10`, and we need to prove `σ.1 (σ.1 10) = 1`.

But `σ.1 10 = σ.2 1`, so:
`σ.1 (σ.1 10) = σ.1 (σ.2 1) = 1` by the inverse property `σ.1 (σ.2 x) = x`.

Thus, the goal is proved.

### Step 6: Abstract Plan

1. **Prove `σ.1 10 = 2`:**
   - Use the inverse property `σ.1 (σ.2 x) = x` with `x = 2` and the hypothesis `σ.2 2 = 10` to get `σ.1 10 = 2`.

2. **Prove `σ.1 (σ.1 10) = 1`:**
   - Use the inverse property `σ.1 (σ.2 x) = x` with `x = 1` to get `σ.1 (σ.2 1) = 1`.
   - But `σ.2 1 = 2` by hypothesis, and `σ.1 2 = 10` is not directly given. However, we can use the inverse property again to get `σ.1 2 = 10` from `σ.1 (σ.2 2) = 2` and `σ.2 2 = 10`.
   - Alternatively, we can directly use `σ.1 10 = 2` to get `σ.1 (σ.1 10) = σ.1 2 = 10`, but this seems incorrect. Wait, no:
     - `σ.1 (σ.1 10) = σ.1 2` because `σ.1 10 = 2`.
     - But `σ.1 2 = 10` is not given. Hmm, this seems problematic.
   - Actually, the correct path is to use `σ.1 (σ.1 10) = σ.1 (σ.2 1)` because `σ.1 10 = 2` and `σ.2 1 = 2`.
   - Then `σ.1 (σ.2 1) = 1` by the inverse property `σ.1 (σ.2 x) = x`.
   - Thus, `σ.1 (σ.1 10) = 1`.

But wait, the above is incorrect. The correct path is:
1. `σ.1 10 = 2` (from `σ.1 (σ.2 2) = 2` and `σ.2 2 = 10`).
2. `σ.1 (σ.1 10) = σ.1 2` because `σ.1 10 = 2`.
3. We don't know `σ.1 2` directly, but we can use `σ.1 (σ.2 1) = 1` to get `σ.1 2 = 10` (but this is not true, as `σ.1 2` is not directly constrained).

This seems circular. The correct approach is to use the inverse properties directly:
1. `σ.1 (σ.1 10) = σ.1 (σ.2 1)` because `σ.1 10 = 2` and `σ.2 1 = 2`.
2. `σ.1 (σ.2 1) = 1` by the inverse property.
Thus, `σ.1 (σ.1 10) = 1`.

But we need to ensure that `σ.1 10 = 2` is correct. Let's re-examine:
`σ.1 (σ.2 2) = 2` by the inverse property.
But `σ.2 2 = 10` by hypothesis, so `σ.1 10 = 2`.

Thus, the proof is correct.

### Step 7: Summarize the Abstract Plan

1. **Prove `σ.1 10 = 2`:**
   - Use the inverse property `σ.1 (σ.2 x) = x` with `x = 2` and the hypothesis `σ.2 2 = 10` to get `σ.1 10 = 2`.

2. **Prove `σ.1 (σ.1 10) = 1`:**
   - Since `σ.1 10 = 2` and `σ.2 1 = 2`, we have `σ.1 (σ.1 10) = σ.1 (σ.2 1) = 1` by the inverse property.

### Step 8: Lean 4 `have` Statements

```lean4
theorem mathd_algebra_209
(σ : Equiv ℝ ℝ)
(h₀ : σ.2 2 = 10)
(h₁ : σ.2 10 = 1)
(h₂ : σ.2 1 = 2) :
σ.1 (σ.1 10) = 1 := by
  have h_σ1_10 : σ.1 10 = 2 := by sorry
  have h_main : σ.1 (σ.1 10) = 1 := by sorry
  sorry
```

### Explanation

1. `h_σ1_10 : σ.1 10 = 2`: This is derived by applying the inverse property `σ.1 (σ.2 x) = x` to `x = 2` and using the hypothesis `h₀ : σ.2 2 = 10`.

2. `h_main : σ.1 (σ.1 10) = 1`: This is derived by using `h_σ1_10` to simplify `σ.1 (σ.1 10)` to `σ.1 2`, and then using the inverse property `σ.1 (σ.2 x) = x` with `x = 2` to get `σ.1 2 = 1` (but this is not directly true; instead, we use `σ.1 (σ.2 1) = 1` and `σ.2 1 = 2` to get `σ.1 (σ.1 10) = σ.1 (σ.2 1) = 1`).

"""

[[proof]]
name = "mathd_numbertheory_1124"
iteration = 9
time = 38
proof = """
theorem mathd_numbertheory_1124
(n : ℕ)
(h₀ : n ≤ 9)
(h₁ : 18∣374 * 10 + n) :
n = 4 := by
  have h_main : n = 4 := by
    have h₂ : n ≤ 9 := h₀
    interval_cases n <;> norm_num at h₁ ⊢ <;>
    (try omega) <;>
    (try {
      norm_num at h₁
      omega
    }) <;>
    (try {
      simp_all [Nat.dvd_iff_mod_eq_zero, Nat.mod_eq_of_lt]
      <;> omega
    })
    <;>
    (try {
      omega
    })
    <;>
    (try {
      norm_num at h₁ ⊢
      <;> omega
    })
  exact h_main
"""
outline = """
### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, we need to understand the problem:
- We have a natural number `n` such that `n ≤ 9`.
- The number `374 * 10 + n` is divisible by `18` (i.e., `18` divides `3740 + n`).
- We need to prove that `n = 4`.

#### Step 1: Understand the Divisibility Condition
The condition `18 ∣ 3740 + n` is equivalent to `3740 + n ≡ 0 mod 18`. 

First, we can simplify `3740 mod 18`:
- `18 * 207 = 3726`
- `3740 - 3726 = 14`
So, `3740 ≡ 14 mod 18` because `3740 = 18 * 207 + 14`.

Thus, the condition becomes:
`14 + n ≡ 0 mod 18`, or equivalently, `n ≡ -14 mod 18`, or `n ≡ 4 mod 18` (since `-14 ≡ 4 mod 18` because `-14 + 18 = 4`).

But since `n ≤ 9`, we can directly check the possible values of `n` from `0` to `9` to find the unique solution.

#### Step 2: Check Possible Values of `n`
We need `18 ∣ 3740 + n`, i.e., `3740 + n ≡ 0 mod 18`.

Compute `3740 mod 18`:
- `18 * 207 = 3726`
- `3740 - 3726 = 14`
So, `3740 ≡ 14 mod 18`.

Thus, the condition is `14 + n ≡ 0 mod 18`, or `n ≡ -14 ≡ 4 mod 18`.

But since `n ≤ 9`, the only possibility is `n = 4` (because `n ≡ 4 mod 18` and `n ≤ 9` implies `n = 4`).

Alternatively, we can directly check the condition for each `n` from `0` to `9`:
1. `3740 + 0 = 3740 ≡ 14 mod 18` → `14 ≢ 0 mod 18`
2. `3740 + 1 = 3741 ≡ 14 + 1 = 15 mod 18` → `15 ≢ 0 mod 18`
3. `3740 + 2 = 3742 ≡ 14 + 2 = 16 mod 18` → `16 ≢ 0 mod 18`
4. `3740 + 3 = 3743 ≡ 14 + 3 = 17 mod 18` → `17 ≢ 0 mod 18`
5. `3740 + 4 = 3744 ≡ 14 + 4 = 18 ≡ 0 mod 18` → This is the solution!
6. `3740 + 5 = 3745 ≡ 14 + 5 = 19 ≡ 1 mod 18` → `1 ≢ 0 mod 18`
7. `3740 + 6 = 3746 ≡ 14 + 6 = 20 ≡ 2 mod 18` → `2 ≢ 0 mod 18`
8. `3740 + 7 = 3747 ≡ 14 + 7 = 21 ≡ 3 mod 18` → `3 ≢ 0 mod 18`
9. `3740 + 8 = 3748 ≡ 14 + 8 = 22 ≡ 4 mod 18` → `4 ≢ 0 mod 18`
10. `3740 + 9 = 3749 ≡ 14 + 9 = 23 ≡ 5 mod 18` → `5 ≢ 0 mod 18`

Thus, the only solution is `n = 4`.

#### Step 3: Abstract Plan
1. Compute `3740 mod 18` to get `14`.
2. The condition `18 ∣ 3740 + n` is equivalent to `14 + n ≡ 0 mod 18`, i.e., `n ≡ 4 mod 18`.
3. Since `n ≤ 9`, the only solution is `n = 4`.

Alternatively, we can directly check all `n` from `0` to `9` to find that only `n = 4` satisfies `18 ∣ 3740 + n`.

### Step 4: Lean 4 `have` Statements

```lean4
theorem mathd_numbertheory_1124
(n : ℕ)
(h₀ : n ≤ 9)
(h₁ : 18∣374 * 10 + n) :
n = 4 := by
  have h_main : n = 4 := by sorry

name = "mathd_numbertheory_237"
iteration = 11
time = 17
proof = """
theorem mathd_numbertheory_237 :
(∑ k ∈ (Finset.range 101), k) % 6 = 4 := by
  have h_sum : (∑ k in Finset.range 101, k) = 5050 := by
    rfl
  
  have h_mod : (∑ k in Finset.range 101, k) % 6 = 4 := by
    rw [h_sum]
    <;> norm_num
    <;> rfl
  
  exact h_mod
"""
outline = """
### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, we need to find the sum of the first 100 natural numbers (since `Finset.range 101` is `{0, ..., 100}`) and then find the remainder when this sum is divided by 6.

The sum of the first `n` natural numbers is given by the formula:
\\[ S = \\frac{n(n + 1)}{2} \\]
For `n = 100`, the sum is:
\\[ S = \\frac{100 \\times 101}{2} = 50 \\times 101 = 5050 \\]

Now, we need to find `5050 % 6`.

First, compute `5050 ÷ 6`:
\\[ 6 \\times 841 = 5046 \\]
\\[ 5050 - 5046 = 4 \\]
Thus, `5050 % 6 = 4`.

Alternatively, we can compute `5050 % 6` directly:
\\[ 5050 \\div 6 = 841 \\text{ with a remainder of } 4 \\]
because:
\\[ 6 \\times 841 = 5046 \\]
\\[ 5050 - 5046 = 4 \\]

Therefore, the remainder is `4`.

### Step 1: Abstract Plan

1. **Calculate the sum of the first 100 natural numbers**:
   - The sum is `S = 100 * 101 / 2 = 5050`.

2. **Find `5050 % 6`**:
   - `6 * 841 = 5046` and `5050 - 5046 = 4`, so `5050 % 6 = 4`.

3. **Conclusion**:
   - The remainder is `4`.

### Step 2: Lean 4 `have` Statements

```lean4
theorem mathd_numbertheory_237 :
(∑ k ∈ (Finset.range 101), k) % 6 = 4 := by
  have h_sum : (∑ k in Finset.range 101, k) = 5050 := by sorry
  have h_mod : (∑ k in Finset.range 101, k) % 6 = 4 := by sorry
  sorry
```

### Explanation:
1. `h_sum`: The sum of the first 100 natural numbers is `5050`. This is a straightforward calculation using the formula for the sum of an arithmetic series.
2. `h_mod`: The remainder when `5050` is divided by `6` is `4`. This is verified by direct computation.

"""

[[proof]]
name = "mathd_algebra_33"
iteration = 12
time = 38
proof = """
theorem mathd_algebra_33
(x y z : ℝ)
(h₀ : x ≠ 0)
(h₁ : 2 * x = 5 * y)
(h₂ : 7 * y = 10 * z) :
z / x = 7 / 25 := by
  have h_y : y = (2 * x) / 5 := by
    have h₃ : y = (2 * x) / 5 := by
      -- Solve for y using the given equation 2 * x = 5 * y
      apply Eq.symm
      -- Use linear arithmetic to solve for y
      linarith
    exact h₃
  
  have h_z : z = (7 * x) / 25 := by
    have h₃ : z = (7 * x) / 25 := by
      -- Substitute y = (2 * x) / 5 into the equation 7 * y = 10 * z
      have h₄ : 7 * y = 10 * z := h₂
      rw [h_y] at h₄
      -- Simplify the equation to solve for z
      ring_nf at h₄ ⊢
      nlinarith
    exact h₃
  
  have h_main : z / x = 7 / 25 := by
    rw [h_z]
    have h₃ : x ≠ 0 := h₀
    field_simp [h₃]
    <;> ring_nf
    <;> field_simp [h₃]
    <;> nlinarith
  
  rw [h_main]
  <;> norm_num
"""
outline = """
### Detailed Proof

**Problem Analysis:**
We are given three real numbers \\( x, y, z \\) with \\( x \\neq 0 \\), and the following equations:
1. \\( 2x = 5y \\)
2. \\( 7y = 10z \\)

We need to prove that \\( \\frac{z}{x} = \\frac{7}{25} \\).

**Approach:**
First, we can express \\( y \\) in terms of \\( x \\) using the first equation:
\\[ y = \\frac{2x}{5} \\]

Next, we can express \\( z \\) in terms of \\( y \\) using the second equation:
\\[ z = \\frac{7y}{10} \\]

Substitute \\( y = \\frac{2x}{5} \\) into the expression for \\( z \\):
\\[ z = \\frac{7 \\cdot \\frac{2x}{5}}{10} = \\frac{14x}{50} = \\frac{7x}{25} \\]

Now, we can compute \\( \\frac{z}{x} \\):
\\[ \\frac{z}{x} = \\frac{\\frac{7x}{25}}{x} = \\frac{7x}{25x} = \\frac{7}{25} \\]
since \\( x \\neq 0 \\).

Alternatively, we can directly compute \\( \\frac{z}{x} \\) using the expressions for \\( y \\) and \\( z \\):
\\[ \\frac{z}{x} = \\frac{\\frac{7y}{10}}{x} = \\frac{7y}{10x} \\]
But from \\( 2x = 5y \\), we have \\( y = \\frac{2x}{5} \\), so:
\\[ \\frac{z}{x} = \\frac{7 \\cdot \\frac{2x}{5}}{10x} = \\frac{\\frac{14x}{5}}{10x} = \\frac{14x}{50x} = \\frac{14}{50} = \\frac{7}{25} \\]

This is the same as the previous approach.

**Verification:**
1. From \\( 2x = 5y \\), we get \\( y = \\frac{2x}{5} \\).
2. From \\( 7y = 10z \\), we get \\( z = \\frac{7y}{10} \\).
3. Substitute \\( y = \\frac{2x}{5} \\) into \\( z \\):
   \\[ z = \\frac{7 \\cdot \\frac{2x}{5}}{10} = \\frac{14x}{50} = \\frac{7x}{25} \\]
4. Now, \\( \\frac{z}{x} = \\frac{\\frac{7x}{25}}{x} = \\frac{7}{25} \\), since \\( x \\neq 0 \\).

Alternatively, we can directly compute \\( \\frac{z}{x} \\):
\\[ \\frac{z}{x} = \\frac{\\frac{7y}{10}}{x} = \\frac{7y}{10x} \\]
But \\( y = \\frac{2x}{5} \\), so:
\\[ \\frac{z}{x} = \\frac{7 \\cdot \\frac{2x}{5}}{10x} = \\frac{14x}{50x} = \\frac{14}{50} = \\frac{7}{25} \\]

Both approaches lead to the same result.

### Step 1: Abstract Plan

1. **Express \\( y \\) in terms of \\( x \\)**:
   - From \\( 2x = 5y \\), we get \\( y = \\frac{2x}{5} \\).

2. **Express \\( z \\) in terms of \\( y \\)**:
   - From \\( 7y = 10z \\), we get \\( z = \\frac{7y}{10} \\).

3. **Substitute \\( y \\) into the expression for \\( z \\)**:
   - \\( z = \\frac{7 \\cdot \\frac{2x}{5}}{10} = \\frac{14x}{50} = \\frac{7x}{25} \\).

4. **Compute \\( \\frac{z}{x} \\)**:
   - \\( \\frac{z}{x} = \\frac{\\frac{7x}{25}}{x} = \\frac{7}{25} \\), since \\( x \\neq 0 \\).

### Step 2: Lean 4 `have` Statements

```lean4
theorem mathd_algebra_33
(x y z : ℝ)
(h₀ : x ≠ 0)
(h₁ : 2 * x = 5 * y)
(h₂ : 7 * y = 10 * z) :
z / x = 7 / 25 := by
  have h_y : y = (2 * x) / 5 := by sorry
  have h_z : z = (7 * x) / 25 := by sorry
  have h_main : z / x = 7 / 25 := by sorry
  sorry
```

### Explanation:
1. `h_y`: We derive \\( y = \\frac{2x}{5} \\) from \\( 2x = 5y \\) by dividing both sides by 5.
2. `h_z`: We derive \\( z = \\frac{7x}{25} \\) by substituting \\( y = \\frac{2x}{5} \\) into \\( 7y = 10z \\) and simplifying.
3. `h_main`: We compute \\( \\frac{z}{x} = \\frac{7}{25} \\) using the expression for \\( z \\).

"""

proof = """
theorem mathd_numbertheory_299 :
(1 * 3 * 5 * 7 * 9 * 11 * 13) % 10 = 5 := by
  have h_main : (1 * 3 * 5 * 7 * 9 * 11 * 13) % 10 = 5 := by
    norm_num [Nat.mul_mod, Nat.mod_eq_of_lt, Nat.mod_eq_of_lt, Nat.mod_eq_of_lt,
      Nat.mod_eq_of_lt, Nat.mod_eq_of_lt, Nat.mod_eq_of_lt, Nat.mod_eq_of_lt]
    <;> rfl
    <;> norm_num
    <;> rfl
  exact h_main
"""
outline = """
### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, we need to find the last digit of the product \\(1 \\times 3 \\times 5 \\times 7 \\times 9 \\times 11 \\times 13\\). This is equivalent to finding \\((1 \\times 3 \\times 5 \\times 7 \\times 9 \\times 11 \\times 13) \\mod 10\\).

#### Step 1: Understand the Problem
We are working modulo 10, so we can ignore all factors that are multiples of 10 (none here) and focus on the last digit of each factor. The last digit of a product is the product of the last digits of the factors modulo 10.

#### Step 2: List the Last Digits
The last digits of the numbers in the product are:
- 1: last digit is 1
- 3: last digit is 3
- 5: last digit is 5
- 7: last digit is 7
- 9: last digit is 9
- 11: last digit is 1
- 13: last digit is 3

#### Step 3: Compute the Product of Last Digits Modulo 10
We need to compute:
\\[ 1 \\times 3 \\times 5 \\times 7 \\times 9 \\times 1 \\times 3 \\mod 10 \\]

Let's compute step by step:
1. \\(1 \\times 3 = 3\\)
2. \\(3 \\times 5 = 15 \\equiv 5 \\mod 10\\)
3. \\(5 \\times 7 = 35 \\equiv 5 \\mod 10\\)
4. \\(5 \\times 9 = 45 \\equiv 5 \\mod 10\\)
5. \\(5 \\times 1 = 5 \\mod 10\\)
6. \\(5 \\times 3 = 15 \\equiv 5 \\mod 10\\)

Thus, the last digit is 5.

#### Step 4: Verification
Alternatively, we can write the product as:
\\[ (1 \\times 3 \\times 5 \\times 7 \\times 9 \\times 11 \\times 13) \\mod 10 = (1 \\times 3 \\times 5 \\times 7 \\times 9 \\times 1 \\times 3) \\mod 10 \\]

Since \\(11 \\equiv 1 \\mod 10\\) and \\(13 \\equiv 3 \\mod 10\\), the last digit of the product is the same as the last digit of the product of the last digits of each factor.

#### Step 5: Conclusion
The last digit is 5.

### Step-by-Step Abstract Plan

1. **Understand the Problem**: We need to find the last digit of the product \\(1 \\times 3 \\times 5 \\times 7 \\times 9 \\times 11 \\times 13\\) modulo 10.
2. **List Last Digits**: The last digits of the numbers in the product are 1, 3, 5, 7, 9, 1, 3.
3. **Compute the Product of Last Digits Modulo 10**:
   - Multiply the last digits step by step modulo 10.
   - \\(1 \\times 3 = 3\\)
   - \\(3 \\times 5 = 15 \\equiv 5\\)
   - \\(5 \\times 7 = 35 \\equiv 5\\)
   - \\(5 \\times 9 = 45 \\equiv 5\\)
   - \\(5 \\times 1 = 5\\)
   - \\(5 \\times 3 = 15 \\equiv 5\\)
4. **Final Result**: The last digit is 5.

### Lean 4 Proof Sketch with `have` Statements

```lean4

(1 * 3 * 5 * 7 * 9 * 11 * 13) % 10 = 5 := by
  have h_main : (1 * 3 * 5 * 7 * 9 * 11 * 13) % 10 = 5 := by
    sorry
  sorry
```

<｜begin▁of▁sentence｜><｜User｜>Complete the following Lean 4 code:

  The plan should highlight key ideas, intermediate lemmas, and proof structures that will guide the construction of the final formal proof.<｜Assistant｜>### Detailed Proof

**Problem Analysis:**
We are given real numbers `a` and `b` such that `a² + b² = 1`. We need to prove that `a * b + |a - b| ≤ 1`.

**Key Observations:**
1. The expression `a * b + |a - b|` is symmetric in `a` and `b` in the sense that swapping `a` and `b` does not change the expression.
2. The condition `a² + b² = 1` is a circle in the plane, and we can think of `a` and `b` as coordinates on the unit circle.
3. The term `a * b` is the dot product of `(a, b)` with `(a, b)`, and `|a - b|` is the distance between `(a, b)` and `(b, a)`.
4. The maximum of `a * b` under `a² + b² = 1` is `1/2` (achieved when `a = b = ±√2/2`).
5. The maximum of `|a - b|` under `a² + b² = 1` is `√2` (achieved when `a = 1, b = 0` or `a = 0, b = 1`).
6. However, we need to find the maximum of `a * b + |a - b|` under `a² + b² = 1`.

**Approach:**
We can consider cases based on the sign of `a - b` to handle the absolute value. Alternatively, we can use the identity `(a - b)² = a² + b² - 2ab = 1 - 2ab` to relate `a * b` and `|a - b|`.

But a better approach is to consider the expression `(a + b)² = a² + b² + 2ab = 1 + 2ab` and `(a - b)² = 1 - 2ab`. This gives us:
`(a + b)² + (a - b)² = 2(a² + b²) = 2`.

But we can also write `a * b + |a - b|` in terms of `(a + b)²` and `(a - b)²`.

However, a more straightforward approach is to consider the cases based on the sign of `a - b`.

**Case 1: `a ≥ b`**

`a * b + a - b ≤ 1`.

Rearrange:
`a * b + a - b - 1 ≤ 0`
`a * b + a - b ≤ 1`.

But this seems complicated. Alternatively, we can use the fact that `a * b ≤ 1/2` (since `a² + b² = 1` implies `a * b ≤ (a² + b²)/2 = 1/2` by the AM-GM inequality).

`(a - b)² ≥ 0` implies `1 - 2ab ≥ 0` or `ab ≤ 1/2`.

But we need to find an upper bound for `a * b + |a - b|`.

Alternatively, we can use the following:
`(a + b)² ≤ 2(a² + b²) = 2` (by the inequality `(a + b)² ≤ 2(a² + b²)`).

But this is not directly helpful.

**Better Approach:**
Consider the expression `(a - b)² = 1 - 2ab`.

We can write `a * b + |a - b|` as `a * b + |a - b|`.

This seems not directly helpful.

But this is not obviously helpful.

### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, we are given:
1. \\( a = -1 \\)
2. \\( b = 5 \\)

We need to prove that:
\\[ -a - b^2 + 3 \\cdot (a \\cdot b) = -39 \\]

#### Step 1: Substitute the Given Values
Substitute \\( a = -1 \\) and \\( b = 5 \\) into the expression:
\\[ -a - b^2 + 3 \\cdot (a \\cdot b) = -(-1) - (5)^2 + 3 \\cdot ((-1) \\cdot 5) \\]

#### Step 2: Simplify Each Term
1. \\( -a = -(-1) = 1 \\)
2. \\( b^2 = 5^2 = 25 \\)
3. \\( a \\cdot b = (-1) \\cdot 5 = -5 \\)
4. \\( 3 \\cdot (a \\cdot b) = 3 \\cdot (-5) = -15 \\)

#### Step 3: Combine the Simplified Terms
Now, the expression becomes:
\\[ 1 - 25 - 15 = 1 - 40 = -39 \\]

Thus, the result is:
\\[ -a - b^2 + 3 \\cdot (a \\cdot b) = -39 \\]

### Step 4: Abstract Plan
1. Substitute \\( a = -1 \\) and \\( b = 5 \\) into the expression.
2. Calculate each term:
   - \\(-a = 1\\)
   - \\(b^2 = 25\\)
   - \\(a \\cdot b = -5\\)
   - \\(3 \\cdot (a \\cdot b) = -15\\)
3. Combine the terms:
   - \\(-a - b^2 + 3 \\cdot (a \\cdot b) = 1 - 25 - 15 = -39\\)

### Lean 4 `have` Statements

theorem mathd_algebra_419

<｜begin▁of▁sentence｜><｜User｜>Complete the following Lean 4 code:

  ```lean4

(Nat.digits 10 n).sum = 13 :=

  ```

  Before producing the Lean 4 code to formally prove the given theorem, provide a detailed proof plan outlining the main proof steps and strategies.
  The plan should highlight key ideas, intermediate lemmas, and proof structures that will guide the construction of the final formal proof.<｜Assistant｜>### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, recall that the logarithm base `b` of `x` is defined as `logb b x = log x / log b`. 

#### Step 1: Simplify the given equation
The given equation is:
\\[ \\log_2 (\\log_{16} n) = \\log_4 (\\log_4 n) \\]

We can simplify the bases using logarithm identities.

1. **Simplify \\(\\log_{16} n\\)**:
   \\[ \\log_{16} n = \\frac{\\log n}{\\log 16} = \\frac{\\log n}{4 \\log 2} \\]
   because \\(16 = 2^4\\) and \\(\\log 16 = 4 \\log 2\\).

2. **Simplify \\(\\log_2 (\\log_{16} n)\\)**:
   \\[ \\log_2 (\\log_{16} n) = \\log_2 \\left( \\frac{\\log n}{4 \\log 2} \\right) = \\log_2 (\\log n) - \\log_2 (4 \\log 2) \\]
   because \\(\\log_2 \\left( \\frac{x}{y} \\right) = \\log_2 x - \\log_2 y\\) and \\(\\log_2 (4 \\log 2) = \\log_2 4 + \\log_2 (\\log 2) = 2 \\log_2 2 + \\log_2 (\\log 2) = 2 + \\log_2 (\\log 2)\\).

3. **Simplify \\(\\log_4 (\\log_4 n)\\)**:
   \\[ \\log_4 n = \\frac{\\log n}{\\log 4} = \\frac{\\log n}{2 \\log 2} \\]
   because \\(4 = 2^2\\) and \\(\\log 4 = 2 \\log 2\\).
   \\[ \\log_4 (\\log_4 n) = \\log_4 \\left( \\frac{\\log n}{2 \\log 2} \\right) = \\log_4 (\\log n) - \\log_4 (2 \\log 2) \\]
   because \\(\\log_4 \\left( \\frac{x}{y} \\right) = \\log_4 x - \\log_4 y\\) and \\(\\log_4 (2 \\log 2) = \\frac{\\log (2 \\log 2)}{\\log 4} = \\frac{\\log 2 + \\log (\\log 2)}{2 \\log 2}\\).

4. **Set the two expressions equal**:
   \\[ \\log_2 (\\log n) - \\log_2 (4 \\log 2) = \\log_4 (\\log n) - \\log_4 (2 \\log 2) \\]

#### Step 2: Find a relationship between \\(\\log_2 (\\log n)\\) and \\(\\log_4 (\\log n)\\)
Recall that:
\\[ \\log_4 x = \\frac{\\log_2 x}{2} \\]
Thus:
\\[ \\log_4 (\\log n) = \\frac{\\log_2 (\\log n)}{2} \\]

Substitute this into the equation:
\\[ \\log_2 (\\log n) - \\log_2 (4 \\log 2) = \\frac{\\log_2 (\\log n)}{2} - \\log_4 (2 \\log 2) \\]

This seems complicated, but we can simplify further. Notice that:
\\[ \\log_2 (4 \\log 2) = \\log_2 4 + \\log_2 (\\log 2) = 2 + \\log_2 (\\log 2) \\]
and:
\\[ \\log_4 (2 \\log 2) = \\frac{\\log (2 \\log 2)}{2 \\log 2} = \\frac{\\log 2 + \\log (\\log 2)}{2 \\log 2} \\]

But we can also consider the case where \\(\\log n = 16\\). Let's test this:
If \\(\\log n = 16\\), then:
\\[ \\log_2 (\\log n) = \\log_2 16 = 4 \\]
\\[ \\log_2 (4 \\log 2) = 2 + \\log_2 (\\log 2) \\]
\\[ \\log_4 (\\log n) = \\log_4 16 = 2 \\]
\\[ \\log_4 (2 \\log 2) = \\frac{\\log 2 + \\log (\\log 2)}{2 \\log 2} \\]

But we need to find a general solution. Alternatively, we can consider that the original equation is satisfied when \\(\\log n = 16\\). 

But we need to ensure that \\(\\log n = 16\\) is the only solution. 

#### Step 3: Solve for \\(n\\)
Assume \\(\\log n = k\\). Then:
\\[ \\log_2 k - \\log_2 (4 \\log 2) = \\frac{\\log_2 k}{2} - \\log_4 (2 \\log 2) \\]

This seems too involved. Instead, let's consider that the original equation is satisfied when \\(n = 16^4 = (2^4)^4 = 2^{16}\\). 

But we need to verify:
\\[ \\log_{16} n = \\log_{16} (2^{16}) = 1 \\]
\\[ \\log_2 (\\log_{16} n) = \\log_2 1 = 0 \\]
\\[ \\log_4 n = \\log_4 (2^{16}) = 4 \\log_4 2 = 4 \\cdot \\frac{1}{2} = 2 \\]
\\[ \\log_4 (\\log_4 n) = \\log_4 2 = \\frac{1}{2} \\]
But \\(0 \\neq \\frac{1}{2}\\), so this is incorrect. 

This suggests that our initial assumption is incorrect. 

#### Step 4: Re-evaluate the approach
Instead, let's consider that the original equation is:
\\[ \\log_2 (\\log_{16} n) = \\log_4 (\\log_4 n) \\]

We can rewrite everything in terms of \\(\\log_2 n\\):
\\[ \\log_{16} n = \\frac{\\log_2 n}{4} \\]
\\[ \\log_2 (\\log_{16} n) = \\log_2 \\left( \\frac{\\log_2 n}{4} \\right) = \\log_2 (\\log_2 n) - \\log_2 4 = \\log_2 (\\log_2 n) - 2 \\]

Similarly:
\\[ \\log_4 n = \\frac{\\log_2 n}{2} \\]
\\[ \\log_4 (\\log_4 n) = \\log_4 \\left( \\frac{\\log_2 n}{2} \\right) = \\frac{\\log_2 (\\log_2 n) - \\log_2 2}{2} = \\frac{\\log_2 (\\log_2 n) - 1}{2} \\]

Thus, the equation becomes:
\\[ \\log_2 (\\log_2 n) - 2 = \\frac{\\log_2 (\\log_2 n) - 1}{2} \\]
Multiply both sides by 2:
\\[ 2 \\log_2 (\\log_2 n) - 4 = \\log_2 (\\log_2 n) - 1 \\]
Subtract \\(\\log_2 (\\log_2 n)\\) from both sides:
\\[ \\log_2 (\\log_2 n) - 4 = -1 \\]
Add 4:
\\[ \\log_2 (\\log_2 n) = 3 \\]
Exponentiate both sides with base 2:
\\[ \\log_2 n = 2^3 = 8 \\]
Exponentiate both sides with base 2 again:
\\[ n = 2^8 = 256 \\]

But wait, this is incorrect because \\(256\\) is not a solution. 

#### Step 5: Correct the mistake
The mistake was in the simplification of \\(\\log_4 (\\log_4 n)\\). 

Recall that:
\\[ \\log_4 (\\log_4 n) = \\frac{\\log_2 (\\log_4 n)}{2} \\]
But \\(\\log_4 n = \\frac{\\log_2 n}{2}\\), so:
\\[ \\log_4 (\\log_4 n) = \\frac{\\log_2 \\left( \\frac{\\log_2 n}{2} \\right)}{2} = \\frac{\\log_2 (\\log_2 n) - \\log_2 2}{2} = \\frac{\\log_2 (\\log_2 n) - 1}{2} \\]

This is correct. 

But earlier, we had:
\\[ \\log_2 (\\log_{16} n) = \\log_2 \\left( \\frac{\\log_2 n}{4} \\right) = \\log_2 (\\log_2 n) - \\log_2 4 = \\log_2 (\\log_2 n) - 2 \\]

Thus, the equation becomes:
\\[ \\log_2 (\\log_2 n) - 2 = \\frac{\\log_2 (\\log_2 n) - 1}{2} \\]
Multiply by 2:
\\[ 2 \\log_2 (\\log_2 n) - 4 = \\log_2 (\\log_2 n) - 1 \\]
\\[ \\log_2 (\\log_2 n) = 3 \\]
\\[ \\log_2 n = 8 \\]
\\[ n = 2^8 = 256 \\]

But \\(256\\) is a solution:
\\[ \\log_{16} 256 = \\log_{16} 16^2 = 2 \\]
\\[ \\log_2 (\\log_{16} 256) = \\log_2 2 = 1 \\]
\\[ \\log_4 256 = \\log_4 16^2 = 4 \\]
\\[ \\log_4 (\\log_4 256) = \\log_4 4 = 1 \\]
But \\(1 \\neq 1\\) is correct. 

But the problem states that the sum of the digits of \\(n\\) is \\(13\\). 

But \\(256\\) has digits \\(2, 5, 6\\) and sum \\(13\\). 

Thus, the solution is correct. 

#### Step 6: Verify uniqueness
The solution is unique because the equation is satisfied only when \\(\\log_2 n = 8\\), i.e., \\(n = 256\\).

#### Step 7: Conclusion
The only natural number \\(n > 0\\) satisfying the given condition is \\(n = 256\\), whose digit sum is \\(2 + 5 + 6 = 13\\).

### Step-by-Step Abstract Plan

1. **Simplify the equation**:
   - Express all logarithms in terms of base 2.
   - Use the change of base formula and logarithm identities to simplify the equation.

2. **Solve for \\(n\\)**:
   - Derive a simpler equation in terms of \\(\\log_2 n\\).
   - Solve the resulting equation to find \\(\\log_2 n = 8\\).
   - Conclude that \\(n = 256\\) is the only solution.

3. **Verify the solution**:
   - Check that \\(n = 256\\) satisfies the original equation.
   - Calculate the digit sum of \\(256\\) and confirm it is \\(13\\).

### Lean 4 Proof Sketch with `have` Statements

```lean4
theorem amc12a_2020_p10
(n : ℕ)
(h₀ : 0 < n)
(h₁ : Real.logb 2 (Real.logb 16 n) = Real.logb 4 (Real.logb 4 n)) :
(Nat.digits 10 n).sum = 13 := by
  have h_main : n = 256 := by
    sorry
  have h_final : (Nat.digits 10 n).sum = 13 := by
    sorry
  exact h_final
```

### Explanation:
1. `h_main`: We first prove that `n = 256` is the only solution under the given conditions. This is the core of the proof.
2. `h_final`: Once we have `n = 256`, we can directly compute the digit sum of `256` to be `13`.

```lean4

(n : ℕ)

(h₁ : Real.logb 2 (Real.logb 16 n) = Real.logb 4 (Real.logb 4 n)) :
(Nat.digits 10 n).sum = 13 := by
  have h_main : n = 256 := by
    have h₂ : Real.logb 2 (Real.logb 16 n) = Real.logb 4 (Real.logb 4 n) := h₁
    have h₃ : (n : ℝ) > 0 := by exact_mod_cast h₀
    have h₄ : Real.logb 16 n = Real.log n / Real.log 16 := by
      rw [Real.logb]
      <;> simp [h₃]

      rw [Real.logb]

      rw [Real.log_pow] <;> norm_num

<｜begin▁of▁sentence｜><｜User｜>Complete the following Lean 4 code:

  ```lean4
  import Mathlib
  import Aesop

  set_option maxHeartbeats 0

  open BigOperators Real Nat Topology Rat

  theorem imo_1960_p2
(x : ℝ)
(h₀ : 0 ≤ 1 + 2 * x)
(h₁ : (1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x))^2 ≠ 0)
(h₂ : (4 * x^2) / (1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2*x))^2 < 2*x + 9) :
-(1 / 2) ≤ x ∧ x < 45 / 8 :=
sorry
  ```

  Before producing the Lean 4 code to formally prove the given theorem, provide a detailed proof plan outlining the main proof steps and strategies.
  The plan should highlight key ideas, intermediate lemmas, and proof structures that will guide the construction of the final formal proof.<｜Assistant｜>### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, let's carefully analyze the problem and the hypotheses.

**Problem Statement:**
Given a real number \\( x \\geq 0 \\) (since \\( 1 + 2x \\geq 0 \\)), and the condition that \\((1 - \\sqrt{1 + 2x})^2 \\neq 0\\), we are to prove that \\(-1/2 \\leq x < 45/8\\).

**Key Observations:**
1. The term \\((1 - \\sqrt{1 + 2x})^2 \\neq 0\\) implies that \\(1 - \\sqrt{1 + 2x} \\neq 0\\), i.e., \\(\\sqrt{1 + 2x} \\neq 1\\). This further implies that \\(1 + 2x \\neq 1\\), i.e., \\(2x \\neq 0\\) or \\(x \\neq 0\\). However, this is not directly relevant to the bounds we need to prove.
2. The inequality \\((4x^2) / (1 - \\sqrt{1 + 2x})^2 < 2x + 9\\) is the main condition. We need to use this to derive bounds on \\(x\\).
3. The denominator \\((1 - \\sqrt{1 + 2x})^2\\) is always non-negative, and it is zero only when \\(1 - \\sqrt{1 + 2x} = 0\\), i.e., \\(\\sqrt{1 + 2x} = 1\\). But this would imply \\(1 + 2x = 1\\) or \\(x = 0\\). However, if \\(x = 0\\), the denominator becomes \\((1 - \\sqrt{1})^2 = 0\\), which is excluded by the hypothesis. Thus, the denominator is always positive, and the inequality is well-defined.

But wait, is \\((1 - \\sqrt{1 + 2x})^2\\) always positive? 

- The square root \\(\\sqrt{1 + 2x}\\) is well-defined because \\(1 + 2x \\geq 0\\) (given).
- The term \\(1 - \\sqrt{1 + 2x}\\) is negative if \\(1 + 2x > 1\\) (i.e., \\(x > 0\\)), zero if \\(1 + 2x = 1\\) (i.e., \\(x = 0\\)), and positive if \\(1 + 2x < 1\\) (i.e., \\(x < 0\\)). But since \\(x \\geq 0\\), \\(1 + 2x \\geq 1\\), so \\(1 + 2x \\geq 1\\) and \\(\\sqrt{1 + 2x} \\geq 1\\). Thus, \\(1 - \\sqrt{1 + 2x} \\leq 0\\) and \\((1 - \\sqrt{1 + 2x})^2 \\geq 0\\).

But the hypothesis is \\((1 - \\sqrt{1 + 2x})^2 \\neq 0\\), which means \\(1 - \\sqrt{1 + 2x} \\neq 0\\) or equivalently \\(\\sqrt{1 + 2x} \\neq 1\\). This further means \\(1 + 2x \\neq 1\\) or \\(x \\neq 0\\).

But the denominator \\((1 - \\sqrt{1 + 2x})^2\\) is zero only when \\(x = 0\\). So the hypothesis \\((1 - \\sqrt{1 + 2x})^2 \\neq 0\\) is equivalent to \\(x \\neq 0\\).

But the Lean statement has \\((1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x))^2 \\neq 0\\) as a hypothesis, which is equivalent to \\(x \\neq 0\\) (since \\(x \\geq 0\\)).

But the Lean statement also has \\(0 \\leq 1 + 2x\\) as a hypothesis, which is redundant because \\(x \\geq 0\\) is already given.

**Approach to the Proof:**

We need to prove \\(-1/2 \\leq x < 45/8\\) under the given hypotheses.

proof = """
theorem mathd_numbertheory_427
(a : ℕ)
(h₀ : a = (∑ k ∈ (Nat.divisors 500), k)) :
∑ k ∈ Finset.filter (fun x => Nat.Prime x) (Nat.divisors a), k = 25 := by
  have h₁ : a = 1092 := by
    rw [h₀]
    rw [show (500 : ℕ) = 2 ^ 2 * 5 ^ 3 by norm_num]
    -- Use the divisor sum formula to find the sum of the divisors of 500
    rw [Nat.divisors_mul, Nat.divisors_prime_pow (by decide : Nat.Prime 2), Nat.divisors_prime_pow (by decide : Nat.Prime 5)]
    <;> norm_num
    <;> rfl
    <;> decide
    <;> rfl
  
  have h₂ : ∑ k ∈ Finset.filter (fun x => Nat.Prime x) (Nat.divisors a), k = 25 := by
    rw [h₁]
    -- We need to find the sum of the prime divisors of 1092
    have h₃ : ∑ k ∈ Finset.filter (fun x => Nat.Prime x) (Nat.divisors 1092), k = 25 := by
      -- Factorize 1092 and find the sum of its prime factors
      rw [show (1092 : ℕ) = 2 ^ 2 * 3 * 7 * 13 by norm_num]
      -- Use the fact that the sum of the prime factors is 25
      rw [Nat.divisors_mul, Nat.divisors_mul, Nat.divisors_mul]
      <;> norm_num
      <;>
        (try decide) <;>
        (try
          {
            rfl
          }) <;>
        (try
          {
            simp [Finset.sum_filter, Nat.divisors]
            <;> norm_num
            <;> rfl
          }) <;>
        (try
          {
            decide
          }) <;>
        (try
          {
            rfl
          })
      <;>
        (try
          {
            norm_num
            <;> rfl
          })
      <;>
        (try
          {
            decide
          })
      <;>
        (try
          {
            rfl
          })
    exact h₃
  exact h₂
"""
outline = """
### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, we need to find the sum of all prime divisors of the sum of the divisors of 500. 

1. **Find the sum of the divisors of 500:**
   - The prime factorization of 500 is \\(500 = 2^2 \\times 5^3\\).
   - The sum of the divisors of \\(n = p_1^{a_1} \\dots p_k^{a_k}\\) is:
     \\[
     \\sigma(n) = (1 + p_1 + \\dots + p_1^{a_1}) \\dots (1 + p_k + \\dots + p_k^{a_k})
     \\]
   - For \\(500 = 2^2 \\times 5^3\\), the sum of the divisors is:
     \\[
     (1 + 2 + 2^2)(1 + 5 + 5^2 + 5^3) = (1 + 2 + 4)(1 + 5 + 25 + 125) = 7 \\times 156 = 1092.
     \\]
   - Therefore, \\(a = 1092\\).

2. **Find the prime divisors of \\(a = 1092\\) and their sum:**
   - The prime factorization of 1092 is \\(1092 = 2^2 \\times 3 \\times 7 \\times 13\\).
   - The prime divisors are 2, 3, 7, and 13.
   - The sum of these primes is \\(2 + 3 + 7 + 13 = 25\\).

3. **Verification:**
   - The sum of the divisors of 500 is correct:
     \\[
     \\sigma(500) = \\sigma(2^2 \\times 5^3) = (1 + 2 + 4)(1 + 5 + 25 + 125) = 7 \\times 156 = 1092.
     \\]
   - The prime factorization of 1092 is correct:
     \\[
     1092 = 2^2 \\times 3 \\times 7 \\times 13.
     \\]
   - The sum of the primes is correct:
     \\[
     2 + 3 + 7 + 13 = 25.
     \\]

### Step 1: Abstract Plan

1. **Compute the sum of the divisors of 500:**
   - Factorize 500 as \\(2^2 \\times 5^3\\).
   - Use the divisor sum formula to get \\(1092\\).

2. **Factorize 1092:**
   - \\(1092 = 2^2 \\times 3 \\times 7 \\times 13\\).

3. **List the prime divisors of 1092:**
   - The primes are 2, 3, 7, and 13.

4. **Sum the prime divisors:**
   - \\(2 + 3 + 7 + 13 = 25\\).

### Step 2: Lean 4 `have` Statements

```lean4
theorem mathd_numbertheory_427
(a : ℕ)
(h₀ : a = (∑ k ∈ (Nat.divisors 500), k)) :
∑ k ∈ Finset.filter (fun x => Nat.Prime x) (Nat.divisors a), k = 25 := by
  have h₁ : a = 1092 := by sorry
  have h₂ : ∑ k ∈ Finset.filter (fun x => Nat.Prime x) (Nat.divisors a), k = 25 := by sorry
  sorry
```

### Explanation:
1. `h₁`: We first prove that `a = 1092` by computing the sum of the divisors of 500.
2. `h₂`: We then use `h₁` to substitute `a` and find the sum of the prime divisors of `a` (which is 1092). The sum of the prime divisors of 1092 is 25.

theorem numbertheory_x5neqy2p4
(x y : ℤ) :
x^5 ≠ y^2 + 4 := by
  have h_main : x^5 ≠ y^2 + 4 := by
    intro h
    have h₁ := h
    rw [← Int.emod_add_ediv x 11] at h₁
    rw [← Int.emod_add_ediv y 11] at h₁
    ring_nf at h₁
    have h₂ : x % 11 = 0 ∨ x % 11 = 1 ∨ x % 11 = 2 ∨ x % 11 = 3 ∨ x % 11 = 4 ∨ x % 11 = 5 ∨ x % 11 = 6 ∨ x % 11 = 7 ∨ x % 11 = 8 ∨ x % 11 = 9 ∨ x % 11 = 10 := by
      omega

      omega
    rcases h₂ with (h₂ | h₂ | h₂ | h₂ | h₂ | h₂ | h₂ | h₂ | h₂ | h₂ | h₂) <;>
    rcases h₃ with (h₃ | h₃ | h₃ | h₃ | h₃ | h₃ | h₃ | h₃ | h₃ | h₃ | h₃) <;>
    simp [h₂, h₃, pow_succ, Int.mul_emod, Int.add_emod, Int.sub_emod, Int.emod_emod] at h₁ ⊢ <;>
    (try omega) <;>
    (try {
      have h₄ : x % 11 = 0 ∨ x % 11 = 1 ∨ x % 11 = 2 ∨ x % 11 = 3 ∨ x % 11 = 4 ∨ x % 11 = 5 ∨ x % 11 = 6 ∨ x % 11 = 7 ∨ x % 11 = 8 ∨ x % 11 = 9 ∨ x % 11 = 10 := by omega
      have h₅ : y % 11 = 0 ∨ y % 11 = 1 ∨ y % 11 = 2 ∨ y % 11 = 3 ∨ y % 11 = 4 ∨ y % 11 = 5 ∨ y % 11 = 6 ∨ y % 11 = 7 ∨ y % 11 = 8 ∨ y % 11 = 9 ∨ y % 11 = 10 := by omega
      rcases h₄ with (h₄ | h₄ | h₄ | h₄ | h₄ | h₄ | h₄ | h₄ | h₄ | h₄ | h₄) <;>
      rcases h₅ with (h₅ | h₅ | h₅ | h₅ | h₅ | h₅ | h₅ | h₅ | h₅ | h₅ | h₅) <;>
      simp [h₄, h₅, pow_succ, Int.mul_emod, Int.add_emod, Int.sub_emod, Int.emod_emod] at h₁ ⊢ <;>

outline = """
### Detailed Proof and Analysis

We need to prove that for all integers \\( x, y \\), \\( x^5 \\neq y^2 + 4 \\).

#### Key Observations:
1. The equation \\( x^5 = y^2 + 4 \\) is a Diophantine equation, and we need to show that it has no integer solutions.
2. We can consider the possible residues of \\( x \\) modulo 11 to find contradictions. This is because \\( x^5 \\mod 11 \\) cycles through a small set of residues, and \\( y^2 + 4 \\mod 11 \\) can take on a limited number of values.
3. The possible residues of \\( x^5 \\mod 11 \\) are \\( 0, 1, 10 \\) (since \\( x \\mod 11 \\) cycles through \\( 0, 1, \\dots, 10 \\), and \\( x^5 \\mod 11 \\) cycles through \\( 0, 1, 10, 1, 10, 0, 10, 1, 0, 10, 0 \\)).
4. The possible residues of \\( y^2 \\mod 11 \\) are \\( 0, 1, 3, 4, 5, 9 \\), because:
   - If \\( y \\equiv 0 \\mod 11 \\), then \\( y^2 \\equiv 0 \\mod 11 \\).
   - If \\( y \\equiv \\pm 1 \\mod 11 \\), then \\( y^2 \\equiv 1 \\mod 11 \\).
   - If \\( y \\equiv \\pm 2 \\mod 11 \\), then \\( y^2 \\equiv 4 \\mod 11 \\).
   - If \\( y \\equiv \\pm 3 \\mod 11 \\), then \\( y^2 \\equiv 9 \\mod 11 \\).
   - If \\( y \\equiv \\pm 4 \\mod 11 \\), then \\( y^2 \\equiv 5 \\mod 11 \\).
   - If \\( y \\equiv \\pm 5 \\mod 11 \\), then \\( y^2 \\equiv 3 \\mod 11 \\).
   - No other residues are possible.
5. The possible residues of \\( y^2 + 4 \\mod 11 \\) are \\( 4, 5, 7, 8, 9, 0 \\), because:
   - \\( 0 + 4 = 4 \\),
   - \\( 1 + 4 = 5 \\),
   - \\( 3 + 4 = 7 \\),
   - \\( 4 + 4 = 8 \\),
   - \\( 5 + 4 = 9 \\),
   - \\( 9 + 4 = 0 \\).
6. Comparing the two sets of residues, we see that \\( x^5 \\mod 11 \\) can be \\( 0, 1, 10 \\), while \\( y^2 + 4 \\mod 11 \\) can be \\( 4, 5, 7, 8, 9, 0 \\). The only overlap is \\( 0 \\), but \\( x^5 \\equiv 0 \\mod 11 \\) implies \\( x \\equiv 0 \\mod 11 \\), and \\( y^2 + 4 \\equiv 0 \\mod 11 \\) implies \\( y^2 \\equiv 7 \\mod 11 \\). But \\( 7 \\) is not a quadratic residue modulo \\( 11 \\), so this is a contradiction.

#### Detailed Steps:
1. Assume for contradiction that \\( x^5 = y^2 + 4 \\).
2. Consider \\( x^5 \\mod 11 \\). The possible values of \\( x^5 \\mod 11 \\) are \\( 0, 1, 10 \\).
3. Consider \\( y^2 + 4 \\mod 11 \\). The possible values of \\( y^2 + 4 \\mod 11 \\) are \\( 4, 5, 7, 8, 9, 0 \\).
4. The only overlap is \\( 0 \\), which occurs when \\( x \\equiv 0 \\mod 11 \\) and \\( y^2 \\equiv 7 \\mod 11 \\).
5. Check if \\( 7 \\) is a quadratic residue modulo \\( 11 \\):
   - The squares modulo \\( 11 \\) are \\( 0, 1, 3, 4, 5, 9 \\).
   - \\( 7 \\equiv 7 \\mod 11 \\) is not in this list, so \\( 7 \\) is not a quadratic residue modulo \\( 11 \\).
6. Thus, \\( y^2 \\equiv 7 \\mod 11 \\) is impossible, and the only case where \\( x^5 \\equiv 0 \\mod 11 \\) is when \\( x \\equiv 0 \\mod 11 \\), but this leads to a contradiction.
7. Therefore, no such \\( x, y \\) exist.

#### Abstract Plan:
1. **Modular Analysis**:
   - Consider \\( x^5 \\mod 11 \\) and \\( y^2 + 4 \\mod 11 \\).
   - Enumerate all possible residues of \\( x^5 \\mod 11 \\).
   - Enumerate all possible residues of \\( y^2 + 4 \\mod 11 \\).
2. **Check Overlap**:
   - The only overlap is \\( 0 \\), which requires \\( x \\equiv 0 \\mod 11 \\) and \\( y^2 \\equiv 7 \\mod 11 \\).
3. **Check Quadratic Residue**:
   - \\( 7 \\) is not a quadratic residue modulo \\( 11 \\), so this is impossible.
4. **Conclusion**:
   - The original equation has no solutions.

### Lean 4 Proof Sketch with `have` Statements

x^5 ≠ y^2 + 4 := by
  have h_main : x^5 ≠ y^2 + 4 := by
    sorry
  sorry

**Problem Statement:**
We have a sequence of non-negative real numbers `a_n` (indexed by natural numbers) such that:
\\[ \\sum_{x=0}^{99} (a_{x+1})^2 = 1. \\]
We need to prove that:
\\[ \\sum_{x=0}^{98} (a_{x+1})^2 \\cdot a_{x+2} + (a_{100})^2 \\cdot a_1 < \\frac{12}{25}. \\]

3. The term `(a 100)^2 * a 1` is `(a_{100})^2 * a_1`.
4. The goal is to bound the sum of these terms by `12/25`.

**Approach:**
To bound the sum, we can use the Cauchy-Schwarz inequality or other inequalities to relate the terms. However, since we are dealing with squares and products, we can consider the worst-case scenario where the terms are as large as possible under the given constraint.

<｜begin▁of▁sentence｜><｜User｜>Complete the following Lean 4 code:

  ```lean4
  import Mathlib
  import Aesop

  set_option maxHeartbeats 0

  open BigOperators Real Nat Topology Rat

  theorem imo_1963_p5 :
Real.cos (π / 7) - Real.cos (2 * π / 7) + Real.cos (3 * π / 7) = 1 / 2 :=
sorry
  ```

  Before producing the Lean 4 code to formally prove the given theorem, provide a detailed proof plan outlining the main proof steps and strategies.
  The plan should highlight key ideas, intermediate lemmas, and proof structures that will guide the construction of the final formal proof.<｜Assistant｜>### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, we need to prove that:
\\[ \\cos\\left(\\frac{\\pi}{7}\\right) - \\cos\\left(\\frac{2\\pi}{7}\\right) + \\cos\\left(\\frac{3\\pi}{7}\\right) = \\frac{1}{2}. \\]

#### Key Observations:
1. The angles are related to multiples of \\(\\frac{\\pi}{7}\\).
2. The sum of cosines can be evaluated using trigonometric identities or by exploiting symmetry.
3. The angles \\(\\frac{\\pi}{7}\\), \\(\\frac{2\\pi}{7}\\), and \\(\\frac{3\\pi}{7}\\) are roots of a certain polynomial related to the Chebyshev polynomials or can be derived from known identities.

#### Step 1: Use the Sum-to-Product Identity
We can use the identity for the sum of cosines and differences:
\\[ \\cos A - \\cos B = -2 \\sin\\left(\\frac{A + B}{2}\\right) \\sin\\left(\\frac{A - B}{2}\\right). \\]

However, this might not directly help here. Instead, we can use the identity for the sum of cosines in terms of a product of cosines.

But a better approach is to use the identity for the sum of cosines of angles in arithmetic progression.

#### Step 2: Use the Sum of Cosines Identity
Consider the sum:
\\[ S = \\cos\\left(\\frac{\\pi}{7}\\right) + \\cos\\left(\\frac{3\\pi}{7}\\right) + \\cos\\left(\\frac{5\\pi}{7}\\right). \\]

Notice that \\(\\frac{5\\pi}{7} = \\pi - \\frac{2\\pi}{7}\\), so:
\\[ \\cos\\left(\\frac{5\\pi}{7}\\right) = \\cos\\left(\\pi - \\frac{2\\pi}{7}\\right) = -\\cos\\left(\\frac{2\\pi}{7}\\right). \\]

Thus, the sum becomes:
\\[ S = \\cos\\left(\\frac{\\pi}{7}\\right) + \\cos\\left(\\frac{3\\pi}{7}\\right) - \\cos\\left(\\frac{2\\pi}{7}\\right). \\]

But we need to relate this to the original expression. 

#### Step 3: Use the Exact Value of \\(S\\)
It is known that:
\\[ \\cos\\left(\\frac{\\pi}{7}\\right) + \\cos\\left(\\frac{3\\pi}{7}\\right) + \\cos\\left(\\frac{5\\pi}{7}\\right) = \\frac{1}{2}. \\]

This can be derived using complex exponentials or by multiplying by \\(2 \\sin \\theta\\) and simplifying.

But we can also use the identity for the sum of cosines of angles in arithmetic progression.

#### Step 4: Use the General Sum of Cosines
The sum of cosines of angles in arithmetic progression can be evaluated using the formula:
\\[ \\sum_{k=0}^n \\cos(a + k d) = \\frac{\\sin\\left(\\frac{(n+1)d}{2}\\right) \\cos\\left(a + \\frac{n d}{2}\\right)}{\\sin\\left(\\frac{d}{2}\\right)}. \\]

For our case, let \\(a = \\frac{\\pi}{7}\\), \\(d = \\frac{2\\pi}{7}\\), and \\(n = 2\\). Then:
\\[ \\sum_{k=0}^2 \\cos\\left(\\frac{\\pi}{7} + k \\cdot \\frac{2\\pi}{7}\\right) = \\sum_{k=0}^2 \\cos\\left(\\frac{(1 + 2k)\\pi}{7}\\right). \\]

This is:
\\[ \\cos\\left(\\frac{\\pi}{7}\\right) + \\cos\\left(\\frac{3\\pi}{7}\\right) + \\cos\\left(\\frac{5\\pi}{7}\\right). \\]

But \\(\\cos\\left(\\frac{5\\pi}{7}\\right) = \\cos\\left(\\pi - \\frac{2\\pi}{7}\\right) = -\\cos\\left(\\frac{2\\pi}{7}\\right)\\).

Thus, the sum becomes:
\\[ \\cos\\left(\\frac{\\pi}{7}\\right) + \\cos\\left(\\frac{3\\pi}{7}\\right) - \\cos\\left(\\frac{2\\pi}{7}\\right). \\]

But we know that:
\\[ \\cos\\left(\\frac{\\pi}{7}\\right) + \\cos\\left(\\frac{3\\pi}{7}\\right) + \\cos\\left(\\frac{5\\pi}{7}\\right) = \\frac{1}{2}. \\]

This is a known identity, and we can prove it as follows:

#### Step 5: Prove the Known Identity
Multiply both sides by \\(2 \\sin \\frac{\\pi}{7}\\):
\\[ 2 \\sin \\frac{\\pi}{7} \\left( \\cos \\frac{\\pi}{7} + \\cos \\frac{3\\pi}{7} + \\cos \\frac{5\\pi}{7} \\right) = \\sin \\frac{\\pi}{7}. \\]

Using the product-to-sum identity:
\\[ 2 \\sin \\frac{\\pi}{7} \\cos \\frac{\\pi}{7} = \\sin \\frac{2\\pi}{7}, \\]
\\[ 2 \\sin \\frac{\\pi}{7} \\cos \\frac{3\\pi}{7} = \\sin \\left( \\frac{4\\pi}{7} \\right) - \\sin \\left( \\frac{2\\pi}{7} \\right), \\]
\\[ 2 \\sin \\frac{\\pi}{7} \\cos \\frac{5\\pi}{7} = \\sin \\left( \\frac{6\\pi}{7} \\right) - \\sin \\left( \\frac{4\\pi}{7} \\right). \\]

Thus, the LHS becomes:
\\[ \\sin \\frac{2\\pi}{7} + \\left( \\sin \\frac{4\\pi}{7} - \\sin \\frac{2\\pi}{7} \\right) + \\left( \\sin \\frac{6\\pi}{7} - \\sin \\frac{4\\pi}{7} \\right) = \\sin \\frac{6\\pi}{7}. \\]

But \\(\\sin \\frac{6\\pi}{7} = \\sin \\left( \\pi - \\frac{\\pi}{7} \\right) = \\sin \\frac{\\pi}{7}\\), so the LHS is:
\\[ \\sin \\frac{\\pi}{7}, \\]
which is exactly the RHS.

This proves the identity:
\\[ \\cos \\frac{\\pi}{7} + \\cos \\frac{3\\pi}{7} + \\cos \\frac{5\\pi}{7} = \\frac{1}{2}. \\]

#### Step 6: Relate to the Original Problem
The original expression is:
\\[ \\cos \\frac{\\pi}{7} - \\cos \\frac{2\\pi}{7} + \\cos \\frac{3\\pi}{7}. \\]

Notice that:
\\[ \\cos \\frac{5\\pi}{7} = \\cos \\left( \\pi - \\frac{2\\pi}{7} \\right) = -\\cos \\frac{2\\pi}{7}. \\]

Thus, the original expression is:
\\[ \\cos \\frac{\\pi}{7} + \\cos \\frac{3\\pi}{7} - \\cos \\frac{2\\pi}{7}, \\]
which is exactly the sum \\(\\cos \\frac{\\pi}{7} + \\cos \\frac{3\\pi}{7} + \\cos \\frac{5\\pi}{7}\\) minus \\(\\cos \\frac{2\\pi}{7}\\).

But we know that:
\\[ \\cos \\frac{\\pi}{7} + \\cos \\frac{3\\pi}{7} + \\cos \\frac{5\\pi}{7} = \\frac{1}{2}, \\]
so:
\\[ \\cos \\frac{\\pi}{7} - \\cos \\frac{2\\pi}{7} + \\cos \\frac{3\\pi}{7} = \\frac{1}{2}. \\]

This completes the proof.

### Step-by-Step Abstract Plan

1. **Understand the Angles**:
   - The angles are \\(\\frac{\\pi}{7}\\), \\(\\frac{2\\pi}{7}\\), and \\(\\frac{3\\pi}{7}\\).
   - The angles \\(\\frac{5\\pi}{7}\\) and \\(\\frac{6\\pi}{7}\\) are also present, but we don't need them directly.

2. **Key Identity**:
   - The sum of cosines of angles in arithmetic progression can be evaluated using the identity:
     \\[ \\cos \\frac{\\pi}{7} + \\cos \\frac{3\\pi}{7} + \\cos \\frac{5\\pi}{7} = \\frac{1}{2}. \\]

3. **Relate to the Original Expression**:
   - The original expression is:
     \\[ \\cos \\frac{\\pi}{7} - \\cos \\frac{2\\pi}{7} + \\cos \\frac{3\\pi}{7}. \\]
   - Notice that \\(\\cos \\frac{5\\pi}{7} = -\\cos \\frac{2\\pi}{7}\\).
   - Thus, the original expression is:
     \\[ \\cos \\frac{\\pi}{7} + \\cos \\frac{3\\pi}{7} - \\cos \\frac{2\\pi}{7}, \\]
     which is the sum of cosines minus \\(2 \\cos \\frac{2\\pi}{7}\\).

4. **Use the Known Identity**:
   - The sum of cosines is \\(\\frac{1}{2}\\), so the original expression is \\(\\frac{1}{2}\\).
