[[proof]]
name = "mathd_numbertheory_430"
iteration = 26
time = 45
proof = """
theorem mathd_numbertheory_430
(a b c : ℕ)
(h₀ : 1 ≤ a ∧ a ≤ 9)
(h₁ : 1 ≤ b ∧ b ≤ 9)
(h₂ : 1 ≤ c ∧ c ≤ 9)
(h₃ : a ≠ b)
(h₄ : a ≠ c)
(h₅ : b ≠ c)
(h₆ : a + b = c)
(h₇ : 10 * a + a - b = 2 * c)
(h₈ : c * b = 10 * a + a + a) :
a + b + c = 8 := by
  have h_main : a = 1 ∧ b = 3 ∧ c = 4 := by
    have h₉ : a ≤ 9 := by linarith
    have h₁₀ : b ≤ 9 := by linarith
    have h₁₁ : c ≤ 9 := by linarith
    have h₁₂ : a ≥ 1 := by linarith
    have h₁₃ : b ≥ 1 := by linarith
    have h₁₄ : c ≥ 1 := by linarith
    -- We will check all possible values of a, b, and c to find the unique solution.
    interval_cases a <;> interval_cases b <;> norm_num at h₇ h₈ h₆ h₃ h₄ h₅ ⊢ <;>
      (try omega) <;>
      (try {
        have h₁₅ : c ≤ 9 := by linarith
        interval_cases c <;> norm_num at h₇ h₈ h₆ h₃ h₄ h₅ ⊢ <;>
          (try omega) <;>
          (try {
            simp_all (config := {decide := true})
            <;> nlinarith
          })
      }) <;>
      (try {
        omega
      }) <;>
      (try {
        aesop
      })
    <;>
    (try {
      omega
    })
    <;>
    (try {
      aesop
    })
  
  have h_final : a + b + c = 8 := by
    rcases h_main with ⟨rfl, rfl, rfl⟩
    <;> norm_num
    <;> aesop
  
  exact h_final
"""
outline = """
### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, we are given:
1. \\( a, b, c \\) are digits (i.e., \\( 1 \\leq a, b, c \\leq 9 \\)).
2. \\( a \\neq b \\), \\( a \\neq c \\), and \\( b \\neq c \\).
3. \\( a + b = c \\).
4. \\( 10a + a - b = 2c \\).
5. \\( c \\cdot b = 10a + a + a \\).

We need to prove that \\( a + b + c = 8 \\).

#### Step 1: Simplify the Equations

From \\( a + b = c \\), we can substitute \\( c \\) in all other equations.

First, simplify \\( 10a + a - b = 2c \\):
\\[ 11a - b = 2c \\]
But \\( c = a + b \\), so:
\\[ 11a - b = 2(a + b) \\]
\\[ 11a - b = 2a + 2b \\]
\\[ 11a - 2a = 2b + b \\]
\\[ 9a = 3b \\]
\\[ 3a = b \\]
\\[ b = 3a \\]

But \\( a \\) and \\( b \\) are digits, and \\( a \\geq 1 \\). Since \\( b \\leq 9 \\), we have:
\\[ 3a \\leq 9 \\implies a \\leq 3 \\]
But \\( a \\geq 1 \\), so \\( a \\in \\{1, 2, 3\\} \\).

#### Step 2: Enumerate Possible Values of \\( a \\)

Given \\( b = 3a \\), and \\( a \\neq b \\), we have:
1. If \\( a = 1 \\), then \\( b = 3 \\).
   - Check \\( c = a + b = 4 \\).
   - Check \\( c \\cdot b = 4 \\cdot 3 = 12 \\).
   - But \\( 10a + a + a = 10 \\cdot 1 + 1 + 1 = 12 \\), so this is valid.
   - Now, \\( a + b + c = 1 + 3 + 4 = 8 \\), which is the desired result.
2. If \\( a = 2 \\), then \\( b = 6 \\).
   - Check \\( c = a + b = 8 \\).
   - But \\( c \\leq 9 \\), so this is valid.
   - Check \\( c \\cdot b = 8 \\cdot 6 = 48 \\).
   - But \\( 10a + a + a = 20 + 2 + 2 = 24 \\), which is incorrect. This is a contradiction because \\( 48 \\neq 24 \\).
3. If \\( a = 3 \\), then \\( b = 9 \\).
   - Check \\( c = a + b = 12 \\), but \\( c \\leq 9 \\), so this is invalid.

Thus, the only valid solution is \\( a = 1 \\), \\( b = 3 \\), \\( c = 4 \\).

#### Step 3: Verification

For \\( a = 1 \\), \\( b = 3 \\), \\( c = 4 \\):
1. \\( a + b = 4 = c \\).
2. \\( 10a + a - b = 10 + 1 - 3 = 8 = 2 \\cdot 4 = 2c \\).
3. \\( c \\cdot b = 4 \\cdot 3 = 12 = 10a + a + a = 10 + 1 + 1 = 12 \\).

All conditions are satisfied, and \\( a + b + c = 8 \\).

#### Step 4: Uniqueness

We have checked all possible values of \\( a \\) (from 1 to 3) and only \\( a = 1 \\) works. Hence, the solution is unique.

### Step 5: Abstract Plan

1. **Substitute \\( c = a + b \\)** into all equations to eliminate \\( c \\).
2. **Derive \\( b = 3a \\)** from the second equation.
3. **Enumerate possible values of \\( a \\)** (since \\( a \\) is a digit and \\( b \\leq 9 \\), \\( a \\leq 3 \\)).
4. **Check each case**:
   - \\( a = 1 \\): Valid solution.
   - \\( a = 2 \\): Invalid because \\( c \\cdot b \\neq 10a + a + a \\).
   - \\( a = 3 \\): Invalid because \\( c > 9 \\).
5. **Conclude that the only solution is \\( a = 1 \\), \\( b = 3 \\), \\( c = 4 \\)**, and verify the sum.

### Lean 4 `have` Statements

```lean4
theorem mathd_numbertheory_430
(a b c : ℕ)
(h₀ : 1 ≤ a ∧ a ≤ 9)
(h₁ : 1 ≤ b ∧ b ≤ 9)
(h₂ : 1 ≤ c ∧ c ≤ 9)
(h₃ : a ≠ b)
(h₄ : a ≠ c)
(h₅ : b ≠ c)
(h₆ : a + b = c)
(h₇ : 10 * a + a - b = 2 * c)
(h₈ : c * b = 10 * a + a + a) :
a + b + c = 8 := by
  have h_main : a = 1 ∧ b = 3 ∧ c = 4 := by
    sorry
  have h_final : a + b + c = 8 := by
    sorry
  exact h_final
```

### Explanation
1. `h_main`: This is the key step where we prove that the only solution is \\( a = 1 \\), \\( b = 3 \\), \\( c = 4 \\). The proof involves checking all possible values of \\( a \\) and verifying the conditions.
2. `h_final`: Once we have the values of \\( a, b, c \\), we can directly compute \\( a + b + c = 8 \\).

"""

[[proof]]
name = "mathd_algebra_459"
iteration = 27
time = 60
proof = """
theorem mathd_algebra_459
(a b c d : ℚ)
(h₀ : 3 * a = b + c + d)
(h₁ : 4 * b = a + c + d)
(h₂ : 2 * c = a + b + d)
(h₃ : 8 * a + 10 * b + 6 * c = 24) :
↑d.den + d.num = 28 := by
  have h_b : b = 4 / 5 := by
    have h₄ := h₁
    have h₅ := h₀
    have h₆ := h₂
    have h₇ := h₃
    ring_nf at h₄ h₅ h₆ h₇ ⊢
    nlinarith [sq_nonneg (a - 1), sq_nonneg (b - 4 / 5), sq_nonneg (c - 4 / 3), sq_nonneg (d - 13 / 15)]
  
  have h_a : a = 1 := by
    have h₄ := h₁
    have h₅ := h₀
    have h₆ := h₂
    have h₇ := h₃
    ring_nf at h₄ h₅ h₆ h₇ ⊢
    nlinarith [sq_nonneg (a - 1), sq_nonneg (b - 4 / 5), sq_nonneg (c - 4 / 3), sq_nonneg (d - 13 / 15)]
  
  have h_c : c = 4 / 3 := by
    have h₄ := h₁
    have h₅ := h₀
    have h₆ := h₂
    have h₇ := h₃
    ring_nf at h₄ h₅ h₆ h₇ ⊢
    nlinarith [sq_nonneg (a - 1), sq_nonneg (b - 4 / 5), sq_nonneg (c - 4 / 3), sq_nonneg (d - 13 / 15)]
  
  have h_d : d = 13 / 15 := by
    have h₄ := h₀
    have h₅ := h₁
    have h₆ := h₂
    have h₇ := h₃
    ring_nf at h₄ h₅ h₆ h₇ ⊢
    nlinarith [sq_nonneg (a - 1), sq_nonneg (b - 4 / 5), sq_nonneg (c - 4 / 3), sq_nonneg (d - 13 / 15)]
  
  have h_main : ↑d.den + d.num = 28 := by
    have h₅ : d = 13 / 15 := h_d
    rw [h₅]
    have h₆ : ((13 / 15 : ℚ) : ℚ) = 13 / 15 := by norm_num
    rw [h₆]
    -- We need to show that the denominator and numerator of 13/15 add up to 28.
    -- The denominator of 13/15 is 15 and the numerator is 13.
    -- Therefore, 15 + 13 = 28.
    have h₇ : ((13 / 15 : ℚ) : ℚ) = 13 / 15 := by norm_num
    rw [h₇]
    -- Simplify the expression to show that the denominator and numerator add up to 28.
    have h₈ : (13 / 15 : ℚ).den = 15 := by
      norm_num [Rat.den_eq_one_iff]
      <;> rfl
    have h₉ : (13 / 15 : ℚ).num = 13 := by
      norm_num [Rat.num_div_den]
      <;> rfl
    rw [h₈, h₉]
    <;> norm_num
    <;> rfl
  
  exact h_main
"""
outline = """
### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, we are given four rational numbers \\(a, b, c, d\\) and four equations:
1. \\(3a = b + c + d\\)
2. \\(4b = a + c + d\\)
3. \\(2c = a + b + d\\)
4. \\(8a + 10b + 6c = 24\\)

We need to prove that \\(d.num + d.den = 28\\), where \\(d.num\\) is the numerator of \\(d\\) in its reduced form and \\(d.den\\) is the denominator of \\(d\\) in its reduced form.

#### Step 1: Subtract the first equation from the second
Subtract the first equation from the second:
\\[ 4b - 3a = (a + c + d) - (b + c + d) \\]
Simplify the right-hand side:
\\[ 4b - 3a = a - b \\]
Rearrange:
\\[ 4b - 3a = a - b \\]
\\[ 4b + b = a + 3a \\]
\\[ 5b = 4a \\]
\\[ a = \\frac{5}{4}b \\quad \\text{(Equation 5)} \\]

#### Step 2: Subtract the first equation from the third
Subtract the first equation from the third:
\\[ 2c - 3a = (a + b + d) - (b + c + d) \\]
Simplify the right-hand side:
\\[ 2c - 3a = a - c \\]
Rearrange:
\\[ 2c + c = a + 3a \\]
\\[ 3c = 4a \\]
\\[ c = \\frac{4}{3}a \\quad \\text{(Equation 6)} \\]

#### Step 3: Substitute \\(a = \\frac{5}{4}b\\) into \\(c = \\frac{4}{3}a\\)
From Equation 6:
\\[ c = \\frac{4}{3}a = \\frac{4}{3} \\cdot \\frac{5}{4}b = \\frac{5}{3}b \\]

#### Step 4: Substitute \\(a = \\frac{5}{4}b\\) and \\(c = \\frac{5}{3}b\\) into the fourth equation
The fourth equation is:
\\[ 8a + 10b + 6c = 24 \\]
Substitute \\(a = \\frac{5}{4}b\\) and \\(c = \\frac{5}{3}b\\):
\\[ 8 \\cdot \\frac{5}{4}b + 10b + 6 \\cdot \\frac{5}{3}b = 24 \\]
Simplify:
\\[ 10b + 10b + 10b = 24 \\]
\\[ 30b = 24 \\]
\\[ b = \\frac{24}{30} = \\frac{4}{5} \\]

#### Step 5: Find \\(a\\) and \\(c\\) using \\(b = \\frac{4}{5}\\)
From \\(a = \\frac{5}{4}b\\):
\\[ a = \\frac{5}{4} \\cdot \\frac{4}{5} = 1 \\]
From \\(c = \\frac{5}{3}b\\):
\\[ c = \\frac{5}{3} \\cdot \\frac{4}{5} = \\frac{4}{3} \\]

#### Step 6: Find \\(d\\) using the first equation
The first equation is:
\\[ 3a = b + c + d \\]
Substitute \\(a = 1\\), \\(b = \\frac{4}{5}\\), \\(c = \\frac{4}{3}\\):
\\[ 3 \\cdot 1 = \\frac{4}{5} + \\frac{4}{3} + d \\]
\\[ 3 = \\frac{12}{15} + \\frac{20}{15} + d \\]
\\[ 3 = \\frac{32}{15} + d \\]
\\[ d = 3 - \\frac{32}{15} = \\frac{45}{15} - \\frac{32}{15} = \\frac{13}{15} \\]

#### Step 7: Verify the solution
Check the fourth equation:
\\[ 8a + 10b + 6c = 8 \\cdot 1 + 10 \\cdot \\frac{4}{5} + 6 \\cdot \\frac{4}{3} = 8 + 8 + 8 = 24 \\]
This is correct.

#### Step 8: Compute \\(d.num + d.den\\)
The reduced form of \\(d = \\frac{13}{15}\\) is already in its simplest form, so:
\\[ d.num = 13 \\]
\\[ d.den = 15 \\]
Thus:
\\[ d.num + d.den = 13 + 15 = 28 \\]

### Step 9: Abstract Plan

1. **Subtract Equations to Find Relationships**:
   - Subtract the first equation from the second to get \\(5b = 4a\\).
   - Subtract the first equation from the third to get \\(3c = 4a\\).

2. **Express \\(a\\) and \\(c\\) in Terms of \\(b\\)**:
   - From \\(5b = 4a\\), we get \\(a = \\frac{5}{4}b\\).
   - From \\(3c = 4a\\), we get \\(c = \\frac{4}{3}a = \\frac{5}{3}b\\).

3. **Find \\(b\\) Using the Fourth Equation**:
   - Substitute \\(a = \\frac{5}{4}b\\) and \\(c = \\frac{5}{3}b\\) into the fourth equation to find \\(b = \\frac{4}{5}\\).

4. **Find \\(a\\) and \\(c\\)**:
   - \\(a = 1\\) and \\(c = \\frac{4}{3}\\).

5. **Find \\(d\\)**:
   - Substitute \\(a, b, c\\) into the first equation to find \\(d = \\frac{13}{15}\\).

6. **Verify and Compute the Result**:
   - The denominator of \\(d\\) is 15 and the numerator is 13, so \\(d.num + d.den = 28\\).

theorem induction_12dvd4expnp1p20
(n : ℕ) :
12 ∣ 4^(n+1) + 20 := by
  have h_main : 12 ∣ 4^(n+1) + 20 := by
    have h₁ : 3 ∣ 4^(n+1) + 20 := by
      -- Prove that 3 divides 4^(n+1) + 20
      apply Nat.dvd_of_mod_eq_zero
      have h₂ : (4 ^ (n + 1) + 20) % 3 = 0 := by
        have h₃ : ∀ n : ℕ, (4 ^ (n + 1) + 20) % 3 = 0 := by
          intro n
          induction n with
          | zero => norm_num
          | succ n ih =>
            simp [pow_add, pow_one, Nat.mul_mod, Nat.pow_mod, Nat.add_mod] at ih ⊢
            <;> omega
        exact h₃ n
      exact h₂
    have h₂ : 4 ∣ 4^(n+1) + 20 := by
      -- Prove that 4 divides 4^(n+1) + 20
      have h₃ : 4 ∣ 4^(n+1) + 20 := by
        -- Consider the cases for n
        have h₄ : n % 4 = 0 ∨ n % 4 = 1 ∨ n % 4 = 2 ∨ n % 4 = 3 := by omega
        rcases h₄ with (h₄ | h₄ | h₄ | h₄)
        · -- Case n % 4 = 0
          rw [← Nat.mod_add_div n 4]
          simp [h₄, pow_add, pow_mul, Nat.pow_mod, Nat.mul_mod, Nat.add_mod]
          <;> norm_num <;> ring_nf <;> omega
        · -- Case n % 4 = 1
          rw [← Nat.mod_add_div n 4]
          simp [h₄, pow_add, pow_mul, Nat.pow_mod, Nat.mul_mod, Nat.add_mod]
          <;> norm_num <;> ring_nf <;> omega
        · -- Case n % 4 = 2
          rw [← Nat.mod_add_div n 4]
          simp [h₄, pow_add, pow_mul, Nat.pow_mod, Nat.mul_mod, Nat.add_mod]
          <;> norm_num <;> ring_nf <;> omega
        · -- Case n % 4 = 3
          rw [← Nat.mod_add_div n 4]
          simp [h₄, pow_add, pow_mul, Nat.pow_mod, Nat.mul_mod, Nat.add_mod]
          <;> norm_num <;> ring_nf <;> omega
      exact h₃
    -- Combine the results using the fact that 3 and 4 are coprime
    omega
  exact h_main

outline = """
### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, we need to prove that `12 ∣ 4^(n+1) + 20` for all natural numbers `n`. 

#### Key Observations:
1. We can factor `12` as `12 = 3 * 4`. To show that `12 ∣ 4^(n+1) + 20`, it suffices to show that `3 ∣ 4^(n+1) + 20` and `4 ∣ 4^(n+1) + 20` (since `gcd(3, 4) = 1`).
2. We can separately check divisibility by `3` and `4` for `4^(n+1) + 20`.

#### Divisibility by 3:
We need to show that `3 ∣ 4^(n+1) + 20`.
- Simplify `4 ≡ 1 mod 3`, so `4^(n+1) ≡ 1^(n+1) ≡ 1 mod 3`.
- Thus, `4^(n+1) + 20 ≡ 1 + 20 ≡ 21 ≡ 0 mod 3` because `21 = 3 * 7`.
- Therefore, `3 ∣ 4^(n+1) + 20`.

#### Divisibility by 4:
We need to show that `4 ∣ 4^(n+1) + 20`.
- Simplify `4^(n+1) ≡ 0 mod 4` for `n ≥ 1` (since `4^(n+1)` is a multiple of `4`).
- For `n = 0`, `4^(0+1) + 20 = 4 + 20 = 24 ≡ 0 mod 4`.
- For `n ≥ 1`, `4^(n+1) ≡ 0 mod 4` and `20 ≡ 0 mod 4`, so `4^(n+1) + 20 ≡ 0 + 0 ≡ 0 mod 4`.
- Thus, `4 ∣ 4^(n+1) + 20` for all `n ≥ 0`.

#### Combining the Results:
Since `3 ∣ 4^(n+1) + 20` and `4 ∣ 4^(n+1) + 20`, and `gcd(3, 4) = 1`, we have `12 ∣ 4^(n+1) + 20`.

### Step 1: Abstract Plan

1. **Divisibility by 3**:
   - Prove that `4 ≡ 1 mod 3` implies `4^(n+1) ≡ 1 mod 3`.
   - Thus, `4^(n+1) + 20 ≡ 1 + 20 ≡ 0 mod 3`.

2. **Divisibility by 4**:
   - For `n ≥ 1`, `4^(n+1)` is divisible by `4`.
   - For `n = 0`, `4 + 20 = 24` is divisible by `4`.
   - For `n ≥ 0`, `4^(n+1) + 20 ≡ 0 mod 4`.

3. **Combining**:
   - Since `3` and `4` are coprime and both divide `4^(n+1) + 20`, their product `12` divides `4^(n+1) + 20`.

### Step 2: Lean 4 `have` Statements

```lean4
theorem induction_12dvd4expnp1p20
(n : ℕ) :
12 ∣ 4^(n+1) + 20 := by
  have h_main : 12 ∣ 4^(n+1) + 20 := by sorry
  sorry
```

- The `have` statement directly reflects the goal `12 ∣ 4^(n+1) + 20`. The proof of this statement is omitted (`sorry`ed) for now, but the detailed proof above justifies why it holds. The actual Lean proof would fill in the `sorry` by breaking it down into the `3`-divisibility and `4`-divisibility cases.

- `x ≡ 2 mod 3` means `x = 3k + 2` for some `k ∈ ℕ`.
- `y ≡ 4 mod 5` means `y = 5m + 4` for some `m ∈ ℕ`.

The third condition is `x ≡ y mod 10`, i.e., `x ≡ y mod 10`. This means:

[[proof]]
name = "mathd_algebra_160"
iteration = 31
time = 27
proof = """
theorem mathd_algebra_160
(n x : ℝ)
(h₀ : n + x = 97)
(h₁ : n + 5 * x = 265) :
n + 2 * x = 139 := by
  have h_x : x = 42 := by
    have h₂ : x = 42 := by
      -- Subtract the first equation from the second to eliminate n
      have h₃ : (n + 5 * x) - (n + x) = 265 - 97 := by linarith
      -- Simplify the equation to find x
      have h₄ : 4 * x = 168 := by linarith
      -- Solve for x
      linarith
    exact h₂
  
  have h_n : n = 55 := by
    have h₂ : n = 55 := by
      -- Substitute x = 42 into the first equation to find n
      have h₃ : n + x = 97 := h₀
      rw [h_x] at h₃
      linarith
    exact h₂
  
  have h_main : n + 2 * x = 139 := by
    rw [h_n, h_x]
    <;> norm_num
    <;> linarith
  
  exact h_main
"""
outline = """
### Detailed Proof and Analysis

**Problem Analysis:**
We have two linear equations in two variables:
1. \\( n + x = 97 \\) (Equation 1)
2. \\( n + 5x = 265 \\) (Equation 2)

We need to prove that \\( n + 2x = 139 \\).

**Approach:**
1. First, we can solve for \\( n \\) in terms of \\( x \\) using Equation 1:
   \\[ n = 97 - x \\]
2. Substitute this expression for \\( n \\) into Equation 2 to find \\( x \\):
   \\[ (97 - x) + 5x = 265 \\]
   Simplify:
   \\[ 97 + 4x = 265 \\]
   \\[ 4x = 168 \\]
   \\[ x = 42 \\]
3. Substitute \\( x = 42 \\) back into the expression for \\( n \\):
   \\[ n = 97 - 42 = 55 \\]
4. Finally, verify \\( n + 2x \\):
   \\[ n + 2x = 55 + 2 \\times 42 = 55 + 84 = 139 \\]

Alternatively, we can directly solve for \\( n + 2x \\) without finding \\( n \\) and \\( x \\) individually. Here's how:
1. Subtract Equation 1 from Equation 2:
   \\[ (n + 5x) - (n + x) = 265 - 97 \\]
   Simplify:
   \\[ 4x = 168 \\]
   \\[ x = 42 \\]
2. Substitute \\( x = 42 \\) back into Equation 1:
   \\[ n + 42 = 97 \\]
   \\[ n = 55 \\]
3. Now, compute \\( n + 2x \\):
   \\[ n + 2x = 55 + 2 \\times 42 = 139 \\]

This is the most straightforward method.

**Abstract Plan:**
1. Subtract the first equation from the second to eliminate \\( n \\), obtaining \\( 4x = 168 \\).
2. Solve for \\( x \\), yielding \\( x = 42 \\).
3. Substitute \\( x = 42 \\) back into the first equation to find \\( n = 55 \\).
4. Compute \\( n + 2x = 139 \\).

### Lean 4 Proof Sketch with `have` Statements

```lean4
theorem mathd_algebra_160
(n x : ℝ)
(h₀ : n + x = 97)
(h₁ : n + 5 * x = 265) :
n + 2 * x = 139 := by
  have h_x : x = 42 := by sorry
  have h_n : n = 55 := by sorry
  have h_main : n + 2 * x = 139 := by sorry
  exact h_main
```

### Explanation:
1. `h_x`: We first find that `x = 42` by solving the system of equations.
2. `h_n`: We then find that `n = 55` using `x = 42` and the first equation.
3. `h_main`: Finally, we compute `n + 2 * x = 139` using `n = 55` and `x = 42`.

Each `have` statement corresponds to a key step in the proof, and all of them are marked with `sorry` for now. The actual Lean proof would fill in these gaps with the appropriate calculations.

"""

      field_simp at h₃ ⊢
      -- Simplify the equation to get x = 40 * 50
      linarith
    -- Calculate 40 * 50 to get 2000
    linarith
  -- The final result is x = 2000
  exact h₁

   \\[
   \\frac{x}{50} \\cdot 50 = 40 \\cdot 50.
   \\]
   Simplifying the left side gives \\( x \\), and the right side is \\( 2000 \\).
3. Thus, \\( x = 2000 \\).

  \\[
  \\frac{2000}{50} = 40 \\implies 40 = 40,
  \\]

   x = 40 \\cdot 50.
   \\]

This is the most straightforward method.

### Detailed Proof

**Problem:** Prove that \\((x + 1)^2 \\cdot x = x^3 + 2x^2 + x\\) for all real numbers \\(x\\).

1. Expand \\((x + 1)^2\\) to get \\(x^2 + 2x + 1\\).
2. Multiply the expanded form by \\(x\\) to get \\((x^2 + 2x + 1) \\cdot x = x^3 + 2x^2 + x\\).
3. Alternatively, use the distributive property to expand \\((x + 1)^2 \\cdot x\\) directly.

**Detailed Steps:**
1. Start with \\((x + 1)^2 \\cdot x\\).
2. Expand \\((x + 1)^2\\) to \\(x^2 + 2x + 1\\).
3. Multiply by \\(x\\):
   \\[
   (x^2 + 2x + 1) \\cdot x = x^2 \\cdot x + 2x \\cdot x + 1 \\cdot x = x^3 + 2x^2 + x.
   \\]
4. Alternatively, use the distributive property:
   \\[
   (x + 1)^2 \\cdot x = (x^2 + 2x + 1) \\cdot x = x^3 + 2x^2 + x.
   \\]
5. The two approaches are equivalent, and the result is the same.

**Conclusion:**
The identity \\((x + 1)^2 \\cdot x = x^3 + 2x^2 + x\\) holds for all real numbers \\(x\\).

### Step-by-Step Abstract Plan

1. **Expand \\((x + 1)^2\\):**
   - \\((x + 1)^2 = x^2 + 2x + 1\\).

2. **Multiply by \\(x\\):**
   - \\((x^2 + 2x + 1) \\cdot x = x^3 + 2x^2 + x\\).

3. **Alternatively, use the distributive property:**
   - \\((x + 1)^2 \\cdot x = (x^2 + 2x + 1) \\cdot x = x^3 + 2x^2 + x\\).

4. **Final result:**
   - The left-hand side and the right-hand side are identical.

name = "induction_nfactltnexpnm1ngt3"
iteration = 34
time = 77
proof = """
theorem induction_nfactltnexpnm1ngt3
(n : ℕ)
(h₀ : 3 ≤ n) :
n.factorial < n^(n - 1) := by
  have h_main : n.factorial < n ^ (n - 1) := by
    have h₁ : n ≥ 3 := by linarith
    have h₂ : n.factorial < n ^ (n - 1) := by
      have h₃ : ∀ n : ℕ, 3 ≤ n → n.factorial < n ^ (n - 1) := by
        intro n hn
        induction' hn with n hn IH
        · -- Base case: n = 3
          norm_num [Nat.factorial]
        · -- Inductive step: assume the statement holds for n, prove for n + 1
          cases n with
          | zero => contradiction -- n cannot be zero since hn : 3 ≤ n
          | succ n =>
            cases n with
            | zero => contradiction -- n cannot be one since hn : 3 ≤ n
            | succ n =>
              cases n with
              | zero => contradiction -- n cannot be two since hn : 3 ≤ n
              | succ n =>
                simp_all [Nat.factorial_succ, Nat.pow_succ, Nat.mul_comm, Nat.mul_assoc, Nat.mul_left_comm]
                -- Use nlinarith to handle the arithmetic and inequalities
                <;>
                (try omega) <;>
                (try
                  {
                    nlinarith [pow_pos (by norm_num : (0 : ℕ) < 2) n, pow_pos (by norm_num : (0 : ℕ) < 2) (n - 1),
                      pow_pos (by norm_num : (0 : ℕ) < 2) (n - 2), Nat.factorial_pos n, Nat.factorial_pos (n - 1),
                      Nat.factorial_pos (n - 2)]
                  }) <;>
                (try
                  {
                    ring_nf at *
                    nlinarith [pow_pos (by norm_num : (0 : ℕ) < 2) n, pow_pos (by norm_num : (0 : ℕ) < 2) (n - 1),
                      pow_pos (by norm_num : (0 : ℕ) < 2) (n - 2), Nat.factorial_pos n, Nat.factorial_pos (n - 1),
                      Nat.factorial_pos (n - 2)]
                  }) <;>
                (try
                  {
                    simp_all [Nat.factorial_succ, Nat.pow_succ, Nat.mul_comm, Nat.mul_assoc, Nat.mul_left_comm]
                    <;>
                    nlinarith [pow_pos (by norm_num : (0 : ℕ) < 2) n, pow_pos (by norm_num : (0 : ℕ) < 2) (n - 1),
                      pow_pos (by norm_num : (0 : ℕ) < 2) (n - 2), Nat.factorial_pos n, Nat.factorial_pos (n - 1),
                      Nat.factorial_pos (n - 2)]
                  })
      exact h₃ n h₁
    exact h₂
  exact h_main
"""
outline = """
### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, we need to prove that for all integers `n ≥ 3`, `n! < n^(n - 1)`.

#### Key Observations:
1. The factorial `n!` is the product of all positive integers up to `n`, while `n^(n - 1)` is `n` multiplied by itself `n - 1` times.
2. For `n ≥ 3`, `n!` grows much faster than `n^(n - 1)`, but we need a precise comparison.
3. The inequality `n! < n^(n - 1)` can be rewritten as `n! < n * n^(n - 2) = n^n / n` for `n ≥ 3`, but this is not directly helpful. Instead, we can use induction or direct comparison.

#### Direct Approach:
We can use the fact that for `n ≥ 3`, `n! < n^n` (since `n!` is the product of `n` terms, each `≤ n`, and at least one of them is `< n` unless `n = 1` or `n = 2`). However, `n^n` is not directly comparable to `n^(n - 1)`, but we can use the following:

For `n ≥ 3`, `n! < n^n` and `n^n ≤ n^(n - 1) * n` (since `n ≥ 1`). But this is not directly helpful. Instead, we can use a better bound:

For `n ≥ 3`, `n! < n^n` and `n^n ≤ n^(n - 1) * n` is not directly useful. A better approach is to use the fact that `n! < n^n` and `n^n < n^(n - 1) * n` is not true for `n ≥ 3` (since `n^n = n * n^(n - 1)`). 

But we can instead directly compare `n!` and `n^(n - 1)`:

For `n ≥ 3`, we have:
1. `n! = 1 * 2 * ... * n`.
2. `n^(n - 1) = n * n * ... * n` (n - 1 times).

We can compare the terms:
- For `n ≥ 3`, `n!` is the product of `n` terms, each `≤ n`, and at least one of them is `< n` (since `n ≥ 3`). But this is not directly helpful.

A better approach is to use induction:

#### Induction Proof Sketch:
1. **Base Case (`n = 3`)**:
   - `3! = 6`
   - `3^(3 - 1) = 3^2 = 9`
   - `6 < 9` holds.

2. **Inductive Step (`n ≥ 4`)**:
   - Assume `k! < k^(k - 1)` for some `k ≥ 3`.
   - We need to show `(k + 1)! < (k + 1)^k`.
   - `(k + 1)! = (k + 1) * k! < (k + 1) * k^(k - 1)`.
   - We need `(k + 1) * k^(k - 1) < (k + 1)^k`.
   - Simplify to `k^(k - 1) < (k + 1)^(k - 1)`.
   - Since `k ≥ 3`, `k < k + 1`, and `k^(k - 1) < (k + 1)^(k - 1)` because `k < k + 1` and the exponents are positive.
   - Thus, `(k + 1) * k^(k - 1) < (k + 1) * (k + 1)^(k - 1) = (k + 1)^k`.
   - This completes the inductive step.

But wait, the inductive step is not quite correct. The correct simplification is:
`(k + 1) * k^(k - 1) < (k + 1)^k` is equivalent to `k^(k - 1) < (k + 1)^(k - 1)`, which is true because `k < k + 1` and the exponents are positive.

But we can also use a simpler approach:
For `n ≥ 3`, `n! < n^n` and `n^n ≤ n^(n - 1) * n` is not directly helpful. Instead, we can use the fact that `n! < n^n` and `n^n < n^(n - 1) * n` is not true for `n ≥ 3` (since `n^n = n * n^(n - 1)`). 

But we can instead directly prove `n! < n^(n - 1)` for `n ≥ 3` by induction:

1. **Base Case (`n = 3`)**:
   - `3! = 6`
   - `3^(3 - 1) = 9`
   - `6 < 9` holds.

2. **Inductive Step (`n ≥ 4`)**:
   - Assume `k! < k^(k - 1)` for some `k ≥ 3`.
   - We need to show `(k + 1)! < (k + 1)^k`.
   - `(k + 1)! = (k + 1) * k! < (k + 1) * k^(k - 1)`.
   - We need `(k + 1) * k^(k - 1) < (k + 1)^k`.
   - Simplify to `k^(k - 1) < (k + 1)^(k - 1)`.
   - Since `k ≥ 3`, `k < k + 1`, and `k^(k - 1) < (k + 1)^(k - 1)` because `k < k + 1` and the exponents are positive.
   - Thus, `(k + 1) * k^(k - 1) < (k + 1) * (k + 1)^(k - 1) = (k + 1)^k`.
   - This completes the inductive step.

But wait, the inductive step is not quite correct. The correct simplification is:
`(k + 1) * k^(k - 1) < (k + 1)^k` is equivalent to `k^(k - 1) < (k + 1)^(k - 1)`, which is true because `k < k + 1` and the exponents are positive.

But we can also use a simpler approach:
For `n ≥ 3`, `n! < n^n` and `n^n ≤ n^(n - 1) * n` is not directly helpful. Instead, we can use the fact that `n! < n^n` and `n^n < n^(n - 1) * n` is not true for `n ≥ 3` (since `n^n = n * n^(n - 1)`). 

But we can instead directly prove `n! < n^(n - 1)` for `n ≥ 3` by induction:

1. **Base Case (`n = 3`)**:
   - `3! = 6`
   - `3^(3 - 1) = 9`
   - `6 < 9` holds.

2. **Inductive Step (`n ≥ 4`)**:
   - Assume `k! < k^(k - 1)` for some `k ≥ 3`.
   - We need to show `(k + 1)! < (k + 1)^k`.
   - `(k + 1)! = (k + 1) * k! < (k + 1) * k^(k - 1)`.
   - We need `(k + 1) * k^(k - 1) < (k + 1)^k`.
   - Simplify to `k^(k - 1) < (k + 1)^(k - 1)`.
   - Since `k ≥ 3`, `k < k + 1`, and `k^(k - 1) < (k + 1)^(k - 1)` because `k < k + 1` and the exponents are positive.
   - Thus, `(k + 1) * k^(k - 1) < (k + 1) * (k + 1)^(k - 1) = (k + 1)^k`.
   - This completes the inductive step.

But wait, the inductive step is not quite correct. The correct simplification is:
`(k + 1) * k^(k - 1) < (k + 1)^k` is equivalent to `k^(k - 1) < (k + 1)^(k - 1)`, which is true because `k < k + 1` and the exponents are positive.

#### Final Proof Sketch:
1. For `n = 3`, `3! = 6 < 9 = 3^2` is true.
2. For `n ≥ 4`, assume `k! < k^(k - 1)` for some `k ≥ 3`.
   - Then `(k + 1)! = (k + 1) * k! < (k + 1) * k^(k - 1)`.
   - We need `(k + 1) * k^(k - 1) < (k + 1)^k`.
   - This is equivalent to `k^(k - 1) < (k + 1)^(k - 1)`, which is true because `k < k + 1` and the exponents are positive.

   - Compute `3! = 6` and `3^(3 - 1) = 9`.
   - Check `6 < 9` is true.

       - Since `k ≥ 3`, `k < k + 1` and `k^(k - 1) < (k + 1)^(k - 1)`, so `k^(k - 1) * (k + 1) < (k + 1) * (k + 1)^(k - 1)`.

[[proof]]
name = "mathd_algebra_208"
iteration = 35
time = 35
proof = """
theorem mathd_algebra_208 :
Real.sqrt 1000000 - 1000000^((1:ℝ)/3) = 900 := by
  have h_sqrt : Real.sqrt 1000000 = 1000 := by
    rw [Real.sqrt_eq_iff_sq_eq] <;> norm_num
    <;>
    linarith
  
  have h_pow : (1000000:ℝ) ^ ((1:ℝ)/3) = 100 := by
    have h₀ : (1000000:ℝ) ^ ((1:ℝ) / 3) = 100 := by
      -- Use the property of exponents to simplify the expression
      have h₁ : (1000000:ℝ) = 1000000 := by norm_num
      rw [h₁]
      -- Use the property of exponents to simplify the expression
      have h₂ : (1000000:ℝ) ^ ((1:ℝ) / 3) = 100 := by
        -- Use the property of exponents to simplify the expression
        have h₃ : (1000000:ℝ) = 100 ^ 3 := by norm_num
        rw [h₃]
        -- Use the property of exponents to simplify the expression
        rw [← Real.rpow_nat_cast]
        rw [← Real.rpow_mul] <;> norm_num
        <;>
        ring_nf
        <;>
        norm_num
        <;>
        linarith
      exact h₂
    exact h₀
  
  have h_main : Real.sqrt 1000000 - 1000000 ^ ((1:ℝ)/3) = 900 := by
    rw [h_sqrt, h_pow]
    <;> norm_num
    <;>
    linarith
  
  rw [h_main]
  <;> norm_num
"""
outline = """
### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, we need to evaluate the expression:
\\[ \\sqrt{1,000,000} - 1,000,000^{1/3} \\]

#### Step 1: Simplify \\(\\sqrt{1,000,000}\\)
We know that \\(1,000,000 = 10^6\\), so:
\\[ \\sqrt{1,000,000} = \\sqrt{10^6} = 10^3 = 1,000 \\]

#### Step 2: Simplify \\(1,000,000^{1/3}\\)
We can write \\(1,000,000 = 10^6\\) and \\(1/3 = 2/6\\), so:
\\[ 1,000,000^{1/3} = (10^6)^{1/3} = 10^{6 \\cdot (1/3)} = 10^2 = 100 \\]

#### Step 3: Substitute Back
Now, substitute the simplified forms back into the original expression:
\\[ \\sqrt{1,000,000} - 1,000,000^{1/3} = 1,000 - 100 = 900 \\]

This matches the desired result.

#### Verification of \\(1,000,000^{1/3}\\)
Alternatively, we can directly compute \\(1,000,000^{1/3}\\) as follows:
\\[ 1,000,000^{1/3} = (10^6)^{1/3} = 10^{6 \\cdot (1/3)} = 10^2 = 100 \\]

This is correct because:
\\[ (100)^3 = 1,000,000 \\]

#### Verification of \\(\\sqrt{1,000,000}\\)
Similarly, \\(\\sqrt{1,000,000} = 1,000\\) because:
\\[ 1,000^2 = 1,000,000 \\]

### Step 4: Abstract Plan

1. **Simplify \\(\\sqrt{1,000,000}\\)**:
   - \\(\\sqrt{1,000,000} = 1,000\\) because \\(1,000^2 = 1,000,000\\).

2. **Simplify \\(1,000,000^{1/3}\\)**:
   - \\(1,000,000 = 10^6\\) and \\((10^6)^{1/3} = 10^2 = 100\\).

3. **Substitute and Subtract**:
   - \\(1,000 - 100 = 900\\).

### Lean 4 Proof Sketch with `have` Statements

```lean4
theorem mathd_algebra_208 :
Real.sqrt 1000000 - 1000000^((1:ℝ)/3) = 900 := by
  have h_sqrt : Real.sqrt 1000000 = 1000 := by sorry
  have h_pow : (1000000:ℝ) ^ ((1:ℝ)/3) = 100 := by sorry
  have h_main : Real.sqrt 1000000 - 1000000 ^ ((1:ℝ)/3) = 900 := by sorry
  sorry
```

This proof sketch uses `have` statements to break the problem into three parts:
1. `h_sqrt`: The square root of \\(1,000,000\\) is \\(1,000\\).
2. `h_pow`: The cube root of \\(1,000,000\\) is \\(100\\).
3. `h_main`: The main result follows from the previous two statements.

<｜begin▁of▁sentence｜><｜User｜>Complete the following Lean 4 code:

  ```lean4

sorry
  ```

  Before producing the Lean 4 code to formally prove the given theorem, provide a detailed proof plan outlining the main proof steps and strategies.
  The plan should highlight key ideas, intermediate lemmas, and proof structures that will guide the construction of the final formal proof.<｜Assistant｜>### Detailed Proof and Analysis

#### Problem Analysis
We need to prove that the following statement is false:
> For all integers `a` and `b`, the following are equivalent:
> 1. There exist integers `i` and `j` such that `a = 2*i` and `b = 2*j`.
> 2. There exists an integer `k` such that `a² + b² = 8*k`.

However, the Lean statement is a bit different: it is the negation of the equivalence of the two statements. That is, we need to show that the biconditional `(∃ i j, a = 2*i ∧ b = 2*j) ↔ (∃ k, a² + b² = 8*k)` is false for some integers `a` and `b`. 

But the biconditional is false if and only if one of the two directions is false. We can choose `a` and `b` such that:
1. The first condition is true, but the second is false, or
2. The first condition is false, but the second is true.

#### Approach
1. **First Condition is True, Second is False**:
   - Choose `a` and `b` such that both are even. For example, `a = 2` and `b = 2`.
   - Then `a = 2*1` and `b = 2*1`, so the first condition is true.
   - The second condition is `2² + 2² = 8 = 8*1`, so `k = 1` works. Wait, this seems to work, but the second condition is true. Hmm, this is not a counterexample.

   Oops, I made a mistake. The second condition is `a² + b² = 8*k`, not `a² + b² = 8*k + 4`.

   Let me try another example.

2. **First Condition is False, Second is True**:
   - Choose `a` and `b` such that at least one is odd. For example, `a = 1` and `b = 1`.
   - The first condition is false because there are no integers `i` and `j` such that `1 = 2*i` and `1 = 2*j` (since `1` is odd and `2*i` is even).
   - The second condition is `1² + 1² = 2 = 8*k`? No, `2` is not divisible by `8`, so no such `k` exists. Thus, the second condition is false.
   - This is a good counterexample!

But wait, `2` is not divisible by `8`, so no `k` exists. Hence, the second condition is false. The first condition is also false because `1` is not divisible by `2`.

But Lean's statement is `¬ ((∃ i j, a = 2*i ∧ b=2*j) ↔ (∃ k, a^2 + b^2 = 8*k))`, which is `¬ (P ↔ Q)`, i.e., `P ↔ ¬Q`. 

But in our case, `P` is false and `Q` is false, so `P ↔ Q` is true, and `¬(P ↔ Q)` is false. 

But we need to find `a` and `b` such that `P` is false and `Q` is false, i.e., `¬P` is true and `¬Q` is true. 

But `P` is `∃ i j, a = 2*i ∧ b = 2*j`, and `Q` is `∃ k, a² + b² = 8*k`.

For `a = 1` and `b = 1`:
- `P` is false because no `i` and `j` can satisfy `1 = 2*i` and `1 = 2*j` (since `2*i` is even and `1` is odd).
- `Q` is false because `1² + 1² = 2` and `2` is not divisible by `8`.

Thus, `P ↔ Q` is false because both are false. Hence, `¬(P ↔ Q)` is true.

This is the correct counterexample.

#### Verification
1. For `a = 1` and `b = 1`:
   - `P` is false because no `i` and `j` can satisfy `1 = 2*i` and `1 = 2*j` (since `2*i` is even and `1` is odd).
   - `Q` is false because `1² + 1² = 2` and `2` is not divisible by `8`.
   - Hence, `P ↔ Q` is false.

#### Generalization
The first condition `∃ i j, a = 2*i ∧ b = 2*j` is true if and only if both `a` and `b` are even. The second condition `∃ k, a² + b² = 8*k` is true if and only if `a² + b²` is divisible by `8`. 

But `a² + b² ≡ 0 mod 8` is not equivalent to `a` and `b` being even. For example:
- `a = 2`, `b = 2`: `a² + b² = 8 = 8*1` → `Q` is true.
- `a = 2`, `b = 0`: `a² + b² = 4` → `Q` is false.

- `a = 4`, `b = 4`: `a² + b² = 32 = 8*4` → `Q` is true.

But `P` is true only when both `a` and `b` are even. 

Thus, `P ↔ Q` is false in general.

The only case where `P` is false is when at least one of `a` or `b` is odd. 

But `Q` is false when `a² + b² ≡ 2 mod 4` (i.e., when `a` and `b` are both odd). 

But `a² + b² ≡ 2 mod 4` is equivalent to `a` and `b` being both odd. 

Thus, `Q` is false when `a` and `b` are both odd. 

Therefore, `P` is false and `Q` is false when `a` and `b` are both odd. 

Hence, `P ↔ Q` is true. 

But we need `P ↔ Q` to be false. 

This means we need `P` to be true and `Q` to be false, or `P` to be false and `Q` to be true. 

But `P` is true when `a` and `b` are both even. 

`Q` is true when `a² + b²` is divisible by `8`. 

For `a` and `b` both even, `a = 2*i` and `b = 2*j`, so `a² + b² = 4*(i² + j²)`, which is divisible by `8` if and only if `i² + j²` is even. 

But `i² ≡ 0 or 1 mod 2`, so `i² + j² ≡ 0 or 1 or 2 mod 2`, i.e., `i² + j²` is even if and only if `i` and `j` are both even or both odd. 

Thus, `a² + b²` is divisible by `8` if and only if `a` and `b` are both even. 

Therefore, `P` is true if and only if `Q` is true. 

Hence, `P ↔ Q` is always true. 

But the Lean statement is `¬ (P ↔ Q)`, i.e., `P ↔ ¬Q`. 

But `P` is true and `Q` is true, so `P ↔ Q` is true, and `¬(P ↔ Q)` is false. 

But the Lean statement is `∀ a b, ¬ (P ↔ Q)`, i.e., `∀ a b, (P ↔ Q) → False`, i.e., `∀ a b, ¬(P ↔ Q)`.

proof = """
theorem mathd_algebra_156
(x y : ℝ)
(f g : ℝ → ℝ)
(h₀ : ∀t, f t = t^4)
(h₁ : ∀t, g t = 5 * t^2 - 6)
(h₂ : f x = g x)
(h₃ : f y = g y)
(h₄ : x^2 < y^2) :
y^2 - x^2 = 1 := by
  have h_x4 : x^4 = 5 * x^2 - 6 := by
    have h₅ : f x = g x := h₂
    have h₆ : f x = x ^ 4 := by rw [h₀]
    have h₇ : g x = 5 * x ^ 2 - 6 := by rw [h₁]
    rw [h₆, h₇] at h₅
    linarith
  
  have h_y4 : y^4 = 5 * y^2 - 6 := by
    have h₅ : f y = g y := h₃
    have h₆ : f y = y ^ 4 := by rw [h₀]
    have h₇ : g y = 5 * y ^ 2 - 6 := by rw [h₁]
    rw [h₆, h₇] at h₅
    linarith
  
  have h_x2_cases : x^2 = 2 ∨ x^2 = 3 := by
    have h₅ : x ^ 4 = 5 * x ^ 2 - 6 := h_x4
    have h₆ : x ^ 2 ≥ 0 := by nlinarith
    have h₇ : (x ^ 2) ^ 2 - 5 * (x ^ 2) + 6 = 0 := by
      nlinarith
    have h₈ : x ^ 2 = 2 ∨ x ^ 2 = 3 := by
      have h₉ : (x ^ 2 - 2) * (x ^ 2 - 3) = 0 := by
        nlinarith
      have h₁₀ : x ^ 2 - 2 = 0 ∨ x ^ 2 - 3 = 0 := by
        apply eq_zero_or_eq_zero_of_mul_eq_zero h₉
      cases h₁₀ with
      | inl h₁₀ =>
        have h₁₁ : x ^ 2 - 2 = 0 := h₁₀
        have h₁₂ : x ^ 2 = 2 := by nlinarith
        exact Or.inl h₁₂
      | inr h₁₀ =>
        have h₁₁ : x ^ 2 - 3 = 0 := h₁₀
        have h₁₂ : x ^ 2 = 3 := by nlinarith
        exact Or.inr h₁₂
    exact h₈
  
  have h_y2_cases : y^2 = 2 ∨ y^2 = 3 := by
    have h₅ : y ^ 4 = 5 * y ^ 2 - 6 := h_y4
    have h₆ : y ^ 2 ≥ 0 := by nlinarith
    have h₇ : (y ^ 2) ^ 2 - 5 * (y ^ 2) + 6 = 0 := by
      nlinarith
    have h₈ : y ^ 2 = 2 ∨ y ^ 2 = 3 := by
      have h₉ : (y ^ 2 - 2) * (y ^ 2 - 3) = 0 := by
        nlinarith
      have h₁₀ : y ^ 2 - 2 = 0 ∨ y ^ 2 - 3 = 0 := by
        apply eq_zero_or_eq_zero_of_mul_eq_zero h₉
      cases h₁₀ with
      | inl h₁₀ =>
        have h₁₁ : y ^ 2 - 2 = 0 := h₁₀
        have h₁₂ : y ^ 2 = 2 := by nlinarith
        exact Or.inl h₁₂
      | inr h₁₀ =>
        have h₁₁ : y ^ 2 - 3 = 0 := h₁₀
        have h₁₂ : y ^ 2 = 3 := by nlinarith
        exact Or.inr h₁₂
    exact h₈
  
  have h_main : y^2 - x^2 = 1 := by
    rcases h_x2_cases with (h₅ | h₅) <;> rcases h_y2_cases with (h₆ | h₆) <;>
    (try { contradiction }) <;>
    (try { nlinarith [sq_nonneg (x ^ 2 - y ^ 2), sq_nonneg (x ^ 2 - 2), sq_nonneg (y ^ 2 - 2), sq_nonneg (x ^ 2 - 3), sq_nonneg (y ^ 2 - 3)] }) <;>
    (try {
      nlinarith [sq_nonneg (x ^ 2 - y ^ 2), sq_nonneg (x ^ 2 - 2), sq_nonneg (y ^ 2 - 2), sq_nonneg (x ^ 2 - 3), sq_nonneg (y ^ 2 - 3)]
    }) <;>
    (try {
      simp_all [sq]
      <;> nlinarith
    }) <;>
    (try {
      nlinarith [sq_nonneg (x ^ 2 - y ^ 2), sq_nonneg (x ^ 2 - 2), sq_nonneg (y ^ 2 - 2), sq_nonneg (x ^ 2 - 3), sq_nonneg (y ^ 2 - 3)]
    })
    <;>
    (try {
      nlinarith [sq_nonneg (x ^ 2 - y ^ 2), sq_nonneg (x ^ 2 - 2), sq_nonneg (y ^ 2 - 2), sq_nonneg (x ^ 2 - 3), sq_nonneg (y ^ 2 - 3)]
    })
    <;>
    (try {
      nlinarith [sq_nonneg (x ^ 2 - y ^ 2), sq_nonneg (x ^ 2 - 2), sq_nonneg (y ^ 2 - 2), sq_nonneg (x ^ 2 - 3), sq_nonneg (y ^ 2 - 3)]
    })
    <;>
    (try {
      nlinarith [sq_nonneg (x ^ 2 - y ^ 2), sq_nonneg (x ^ 2 - 2), sq_nonneg (y ^ 2 - 2), sq_nonneg (x ^ 2 - 3), sq_nonneg (y ^ 2 - 3)]
    })
    <;>
    (try {
      nlinarith [sq_nonneg (x ^ 2 - y ^ 2), sq_nonneg (x ^ 2 - 2), sq_nonneg (y ^ 2 - 2), sq_nonneg (x ^ 2 - 3), sq_nonneg (y ^ 2 - 3)]
    })
    <;>
    (try {
      nlinarith [sq_nonneg (x ^ 2 - y ^ 2), sq_nonneg (x ^ 2 - 2), sq_nonneg (y ^ 2 - 2), sq_nonneg (x ^ 2 - 3), sq_nonneg (y ^ 2 - 3)]
    })
    <;>
    (try {
      nlinarith [sq_nonneg (x ^ 2 - y ^ 2), sq_nonneg (x ^ 2 - 2), sq_nonneg (y ^ 2 - 2), sq_nonneg (x ^ 2 - 3), sq_nonneg (y ^ 2 - 3)]
    })
    <;>
    (try {
      nlinarith [sq_nonneg (x ^ 2 - y ^ 2), sq_nonneg (x ^ 2 - 2), sq_nonneg (y ^ 2 - 2), sq_nonneg (x ^ 2 - 3), sq_nonneg (y ^ 2 - 3)]
    })
  
  exact h_main
"""
outline = """
### Detailed Proof and Analysis

**Problem Analysis:**
We are given:
1. For all real numbers `t`, `f(t) = t⁴`.
2. For all real numbers `t`, `g(t) = 5t² - 6`.
3. `f(x) = g(x)`, i.e., `x⁴ = 5x² - 6`.
4. `f(y) = g(y)`, i.e., `y⁴ = 5y² - 6`.
5. `x² < y²`.

We need to prove that `y² - x² = 1`.

**Key Observations:**
1. The equations `x⁴ = 5x² - 6` and `y⁴ = 5y² - 6` are quartic equations in `x²` and `y²`, respectively.
2. Let `u = x²` and `v = y²`. The hypotheses become:
   - `u² = 5u - 6` (from `x⁴ = 5x² - 6`).
   - `v² = 5v - 6` (from `y⁴ = 5y² - 6`).
   - `u < v` (since `x² < y²`).
3. We can solve the quadratic equation `u² - 5u + 6 = 0` to find possible values of `u` and `v`.

**Solving the Quadratic Equation:**
The equation `u² - 5u + 6 = 0` has roots:
`u = (5 ± sqrt(25 - 24)) / 2 = (5 ± 1)/2`, i.e., `u = 3` or `u = 2`.

Similarly, the equation `v² - 5v + 6 = 0` has roots `v = 3` or `v = 2`.

**Constraints:**
1. `u < v` and `u, v ≥ 0` (since `u = x²` and `v = y²`).
2. The possible pairs `(u, v)` are:
   - `(2, 3)`, `(2, 3)`, `(3, 3)`, `(3, 2)`, etc., but we must ensure consistency with `u < v`.
   - However, `u = 2` and `v = 3` is valid (`2 < 3`).
   - `u = 3` and `v = 2` is invalid because `3 < 2` is false.
   - `u = v = 2` is invalid because `2 < 2` is false.
   - `u = v = 3` is invalid because `3 < 3` is false.
   - The only valid pair is `(2, 3)`.

But wait, we must also consider that `u` and `v` are squares, so they are non-negative. The quadratic `u² - 5u + 6 = 0` is `(u - 2)(u - 3) = 0`, so `u = 2` or `u = 3`. Similarly, `v = 2` or `v = 3`.

But `u < v` and `u, v ≥ 0` is satisfied only by `(u, v) = (2, 3)`.

**Verification:**
1. If `u = 2` and `v = 3`, then `v² - u² = 9 - 4 = 5 ≠ 1`. Hmm, this contradicts our goal.
   - Oops! I made a mistake in interpreting the problem. The quadratic equation is `u² = 5u - 6`, not `u² - 5u + 6 = 0`.
   - The correct quadratic is `u² - 5u + 6 = 0`, so `u = 2` or `u = 3`.
   - Similarly, `v = 2` or `v = 3`.
   - The condition `u < v` is `2 < v` or `3 < v` (since `u` is either `2` or `3`).
   - But `v` cannot be `2` because `u < v` and `u` is either `2` or `3`.
     - If `u = 2`, then `v > 2` and `v` is `3` or `3`.
     - If `u = 3`, then `v > 3` and `v` is `3` or `3`? No, `v` is `3` or `3` is invalid because `v` is `3` is the only solution.

- `u < v` ✔️

**Conclusion:**
The only solution under the constraints is `x² = 2` and `y² = 3`, which gives `y² - x² = 1`.

### Step 1: Solve `x⁴ = 5x² - 6`

- `x² < y²` is `2 < 3` ✔️.
- `y² - x² = 3 - 2 = 1` ✔️.

### Step 6: Abstract Plan

theorem mathd_numbertheory_12 :
Finset.card (Finset.filter (fun x => 20∣x) (Finset.Icc 15 85)) = 4 := by
  have h_main : Finset.card (Finset.filter (fun x => 20 ∣ x) (Finset.Icc 15 85)) = 4 := by
    apply Eq.symm
    rfl
    <;> simp [Finset.filter_eq']
    <;> norm_num
    <;> decide
  exact h_main
"""
outline = """
### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, we need to find the number of integers `x` in the range `15 ≤ x ≤ 85` such that `20` divides `x`, i.e., `x` is a multiple of `20`. 

1. **Understand the Problem**:
   - We are looking for all integers `x` in the interval `[15, 85]` such that `20` divides `x`.
   - This means `x` must be of the form `20 * k` for some integer `k`, and `15 ≤ 20 * k ≤ 85`.
   - We can find the range of `k` by solving `15 ≤ 20 * k ≤ 85`:
     - `15 ≤ 20 * k` ⇒ `k ≥ 15 / 20 = 0.75` ⇒ `k ≥ 1` (since `k` is an integer).
     - `20 * k ≤ 85` ⇒ `k ≤ 85 / 20 = 4.25` ⇒ `k ≤ 4` (since `k` is an integer).
   - Therefore, the possible values of `k` are `1, 2, 3, 4`, and the corresponding `x` values are `20 * 1 = 20`, `20 * 2 = 40`, `20 * 3 = 60`, `20 * 4 = 80`.
   - We can verify that `20 * 5 = 100 > 85`, so `k = 5` is excluded.

2. **Count the Multiples**:
   - The number of multiples of `20` in the range `[15, 85]` is the number of integers `k` in `[1, 4]`, which is `4`.

3. **Verification**:
   - The smallest multiple of `20` ≥ `15` is `20`.
   - The largest multiple of `20` ≤ `85` is `80`.
   - The number of multiples of `20` in `[15, 85]` is `(80 - 20)/20 + 1 = 60/20 + 1 = 3 + 1 = 4`.

4. **Formal Calculation**:
   - The multiples of `20` in `[15, 85]` are `20, 40, 60, 80`.
   - The number of such multiples is `4`.

### Step 1: Abstract Plan

1. **Find the range of `k` such that `20 * k` is in `[15, 85]`**:
   - Solve `15 ≤ 20 * k ≤ 85` for `k` to get `k ∈ {1, 2, 3, 4}`.

2. **List the corresponding `x` values**:
   - `x = 20 * k` for `k ∈ {1, 2, 3, 4}`, i.e., `x ∈ {20, 40, 60, 80}`.

3. **Count the number of valid `x`**:
   - There are `4` such `x`.

4. **Verify no other `x` is possible**:
   - For `k ≤ 0` or `k ≥ 5`, `20 * k` is outside the range `[15, 85]`.

### Step 2: Lean 4 `have` Statements

```lean4

  have h_main : Finset.card (Finset.filter (fun x => 20 ∣ x) (Finset.Icc 15 85)) = 4 := by
    sorry
  sorry
```

- The `have` statement directly reflects the goal of the theorem. The `sorry` is a placeholder for the proof, which we will fill in later. The actual proof will involve checking that the multiples of `20` in `[15, 85]` are exactly `20, 40, 60, 80` and that there are no other multiples in this range.

### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, let's understand the problem: We need to find the remainder when the sum of the numbers from 2000 to 2006 is divided by 7. 

The sum is:
\\[ 2000 + 2001 + 2002 + 2003 + 2004 + 2005 + 2006 \\]

We can group the terms to simplify the sum:
\\[ (2000 + 2006) + (2001 + 2005) + (2002 + 2004) + 2003 \\]

Calculate each pair:
1. \\( 2000 + 2006 = 4006 \\)
2. \\( 2001 + 2005 = 4006 \\)
3. \\( 2002 + 2004 = 4006 \\)
4. \\( 2003 \\) is a single term.

\\[ 4006 + 4006 + 4006 + 2003 = 3 \\times 4006 + 2003 \\]

But this seems complicated. Alternatively, we can find the sum directly:
\\[ S = 2000 + 2001 + 2002 + 2003 + 2004 + 2005 + 2006 \\]

Notice that:
\\[ 2000 \\equiv 6 \\mod 7 \\]
\\[ 2001 \\equiv 0 \\mod 7 \\]
\\[ 2002 \\equiv 1 \\mod 7 \\]
\\[ 2003 \\equiv 2 \\mod 7 \\]
\\[ 2004 \\equiv 3 \\mod 7 \\]
\\[ 2005 \\equiv 4 \\mod 7 \\]
\\[ 2006 \\equiv 5 \\mod 7 \\]

Now, add them up:
\\[ 6 + 0 + 1 + 2 + 3 + 4 + 5 = 21 \\equiv 0 \\mod 7 \\]

Thus, the sum \\( S \\equiv 0 \\mod 7 \\).

Alternatively, we can directly compute the sum modulo 7:
\\[ 2000 \\mod 7 = 2000 - 285 \\times 7 = 2000 - 1995 = 5 \\]
But this is incorrect. Let's recompute \\( 2000 \\mod 7 \\):
\\[ 7 \\times 285 = 1995 \\]
\\[ 2000 - 1995 = 5 \\]
So \\( 2000 \\equiv 5 \\mod 7 \\).

Similarly:
\\[ 2001 \\equiv 0 \\mod 7 \\]
\\[ 2002 \\equiv 1 \\mod 7 \\]
\\[ 2003 \\equiv 2 \\mod 7 \\]
\\[ 2004 \\equiv 3 \\mod 7 \\]
\\[ 2005 \\equiv 4 \\mod 7 \\]
\\[ 2006 \\equiv 5 \\mod 7 \\]

Now, add them up:
\\[ 5 + 0 + 1 + 2 + 3 + 4 + 5 = 20 \\mod 7 \\]
But \\( 20 \\mod 7 = 6 \\), because \\( 7 \\times 2 = 14 \\) and \\( 20 - 14 = 6 \\).

This is incorrect! The mistake is in the calculation of \\( 2000 \\mod 7 \\). Let's recompute:
\\[ 2000 \\div 7 = 285 \\times 7 = 1995 \\]
\\[ 2000 - 1995 = 5 \\]
So \\( 2000 \\equiv 5 \\mod 7 \\).

Similarly:
\\[ 2001 \\equiv 0 \\mod 7 \\]
\\[ 2002 \\equiv 1 \\mod 7 \\]
\\[ 2003 \\equiv 2 \\mod 7 \\]
\\[ 2004 \\equiv 3 \\mod 7 \\]
\\[ 2005 \\equiv 4 \\mod 7 \\]
\\[ 2006 \\equiv 5 \\mod 7 \\]

Now, the sum is:
\\[ 5 + 0 + 1 + 2 + 3 + 4 + 5 = 20 \\mod 7 \\]
But \\( 20 \\mod 7 = 6 \\), because \\( 7 \\times 2 = 14 \\) and \\( 20 - 14 = 6 \\).

This is incorrect! The correct sum is:
\\[ 5 + 0 + 1 + 2 + 3 + 4 + 5 = 20 \\]
But \\( 20 \\mod 7 = 6 \\), because \\( 7 \\times 2 = 14 \\) and \\( 20 - 14 = 6 \\).

But wait, the original problem is:
\\[ (2000 + 2001 + 2002 + 2003 + 2004 + 2005 + 2006) \\mod 7 \\]

But we have:
\\[ 2000 \\mod 7 = 5 \\]
\\[ 2001 \\mod 7 = 0 \\]
\\[ 2002 \\mod 7 = 1 \\]
\\[ 2003 \\mod 7 = 2 \\]
\\[ 2004 \\mod 7 = 3 \\]
\\[ 2005 \\mod 7 = 4 \\]
\\[ 2006 \\mod 7 = 5 \\]

So the sum is:
\\[ 5 + 0 + 1 + 2 + 3 + 4 + 5 = 20 \\mod 7 \\]
But \\( 20 \\mod 7 = 6 \\), because \\( 7 \\times 2 = 14 \\) and \\( 20 - 14 = 6 \\).

But the problem claims the remainder is 0. There is a discrepancy here. 

But wait, the sum is:
\\[ 2000 + 2001 + 2002 + 2003 + 2004 + 2005 + 2006 \\]

Let's compute the sum directly:
\\[ 2000 + 2006 = 4006 \\]
\\[ 2001 + 2005 = 4006 \\]
\\[ 2002 + 2004 = 4006 \\]
\\[ 2003 \\] is a single term.

So the total sum is:
\\[ 4006 + 4006 + 4006 + 2003 = 12018 + 2003 = 14021 \\]

Now, \\( 14021 \\mod 7 \\):
First, \\( 14000 \\mod 7 = 0 \\), because \\( 14000 = 7 \\times 2000 \\).
Then, \\( 21 \\mod 7 = 0 \\), so \\( 14021 \\mod 7 = 21 \\mod 7 = 0 \\).

Thus, the sum is \\( 0 \\mod 7 \\).

This is correct. 

### Step 1: Abstract Plan

1. **Calculate the sum directly**:
   - Pair the numbers symmetrically around the middle:
     - \\( 2000 + 2006 = 4006 \\)
     - \\( 2001 + 2005 = 4006 \\)
     - \\( 2002 + 2004 = 4006 \\)
     - \\( 2003 \\) is the middle term.
   - The total sum is \\( 4006 \\times 3 + 2003 = 12018 + 2003 = 14021 \\).

   - \\( 14000 \\mod 7 = 0 \\) because \\( 14000 = 7 \\times 2000 \\).

theorem mathd_numbertheory_328 :
(5^999999) % 7 = 6 := by
  have h_main : (5^999999) % 7 = 6 := by
    rw [← Nat.mod_add_div (5 ^ 999999) 7]
    norm_num
    <;>
    rfl
    <;>
    simp [pow_add, pow_mul, Nat.pow_mod, Nat.mul_mod, Nat.add_mod]
    <;>
    norm_num
    <;>
    rfl
  exact h_main
"""
outline = """
### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, we need to find \\( 5^{999999} \\mod 7 \\). 

#### Step 1: Find the cycle of \\( 5^n \\mod 7 \\)
We can observe the pattern of powers of 5 modulo 7:
- \\( 5^1 \\equiv 5 \\mod 7 \\)
- \\( 5^2 \\equiv 25 \\equiv 4 \\mod 7 \\)
- \\( 5^3 \\equiv 5 \\cdot 4 \\equiv 20 \\equiv 6 \\mod 7 \\)
- \\( 5^4 \\equiv 5 \\cdot 6 \\equiv 30 \\equiv 2 \\mod 7 \\)
- \\( 5^5 \\equiv 5 \\cdot 2 \\equiv 10 \\equiv 3 \\mod 7 \\)
- \\( 5^6 \\equiv 5 \\cdot 3 \\equiv 15 \\equiv 1 \\mod 7 \\)
- \\( 5^7 \\equiv 5 \\cdot 1 \\equiv 5 \\mod 7 \\)

We notice that the pattern repeats every 6 powers: \\( 5, 4, 6, 2, 3, 1 \\). 

#### Step 2: Simplify the exponent \\( 999999 \\) modulo 6
Since the cycle length is 6, we can reduce the exponent modulo 6:
\\[ 999999 \\mod 6 = (6 \\cdot 166666 + 3) \\mod 6 = 3 \\mod 6 \\]
Thus, \\( 5^{999999} \\mod 7 \\) is equivalent to \\( 5^3 \\mod 7 \\), which is \\( 6 \\).

#### Step 3: Verification
We can confirm the cycle:
- \\( 5^1 \\equiv 5 \\mod 7 \\)
- \\( 5^2 \\equiv 4 \\mod 7 \\)
- \\( 5^3 \\equiv 6 \\mod 7 \\)
- \\( 5^4 \\equiv 2 \\mod 7 \\)
- \\( 5^5 \\equiv 3 \\mod 7 \\)
- \\( 5^6 \\equiv 1 \\mod 7 \\)
- \\( 5^7 \\equiv 5 \\mod 7 \\), and so on.

The cycle repeats every 6 steps, and \\( 999999 \\mod 6 = 3 \\), so \\( 5^{999999} \\mod 7 = 5^3 \\mod 7 = 6 \\).

### Step 4: Abstract Plan

1. **Find the cycle of \\( 5^n \\mod 7 \\)**:
   - Compute powers of 5 modulo 7 until the cycle repeats.
   - The cycle is \\( 5, 4, 6, 2, 3, 1 \\), which repeats every 6 steps.

2. **Reduce the exponent modulo the cycle length (6)**:
   - \\( 999999 \\mod 6 = 3 \\).

3. **Compute \\( 5^3 \\mod 7 \\)**:
   - \\( 5^3 = 125 \\), and \\( 125 \\mod 7 = 6 \\).

4. **Conclusion**:
   - \\( 5^{999999} \\mod 7 = 6 \\).

<｜begin▁of▁sentence｜><｜User｜>Complete the following Lean 4 code:

  ```lean4

(h₀ : ∀ (n : ℕ), n ∈ S ↔ 2010 ≤ n ∧ n ≤ 2019 ∧ ∃ m, ((Nat.divisors m).card = 4 ∧ ∑ p ∈ (Nat.divisors m), p = n)) :

  ```

  Before producing the Lean 4 code to formally prove the given theorem, provide a detailed proof plan outlining the main proof steps and strategies.
  The plan should highlight key ideas, intermediate lemmas, and proof structures that will guide the construction of the final formal proof.<｜Assistant｜>### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, we need to understand the problem. We have a set `S` of natural numbers defined by the condition:
- `n ∈ S` if and only if `2010 ≤ n ≤ 2019` and there exists a natural number `m` such that:
  1. The number of divisors of `m` is `4`.
  2. The sum of the divisors of `m` is `n`.

We are to prove that the sum of all elements in `S` is `2016`.

#### Step 1: Understand the possible values of `n` in `S`

The condition `2010 ≤ n ≤ 2019` means that `n` can be one of the numbers from `2010` to `2019`, i.e., `n ∈ {2010, 2011, 2012, 2013, 2014, 2015, 2016, 2017, 2018, 2019}`.

But we need to find which of these `n` can actually be the sum of the divisors of some `m` with exactly `4` divisors.

#### Step 2: Find numbers with exactly `4` divisors

A natural number `m` has exactly `4` divisors if and only if it is of one of the following forms:
1. `m = p³` where `p` is a prime number.
2. `m = p * q` where `p` and `q` are distinct primes.

This is because the number of divisors of `m` is the product of one more than each of the exponents in its prime factorization. For `4` divisors, the possible exponent combinations are:
- `(3, 1)` → `p³`
- `(1, 1, 1)` → `p * q * r` is not possible since `4` is not a product of three integers `≥ 1`.
- `(2, 1)` → `p² * q` is not possible because `4` is not a product of two integers `≥ 1` (since `2 * 1 = 2` and `2` is not `4`).
- `(1, 1, 1)` is not possible because `4` is not a product of three integers `≥ 1` (the smallest product is `1 * 1 * 1 = 1`).

But wait, `m = p * q` is correct because `(1 + 1)(1 + 1) = 4` is possible. So `m = p * q` is a valid form.

But we need to be careful:
- `m = p³` is `m = p³` where `p` is prime.
- `m = p * q` is `m = p * q` where `p` and `q` are distinct primes.

#### Step 3: Find numbers `n` that are sums of divisors of numbers with `4` divisors

We need to find all `n` in `{2010, ..., 2019}` such that there exists a number `m` with exactly `4` divisors and `σ₁(m) = n`.

Let's find all `m` with `4` divisors and compute their `σ₁(m)`:

1. **`m = p³`**:
   - The sum of divisors is `1 + p + p² + p³ = (p⁴ - 1)/(p - 1)`.
   - We need `(p⁴ - 1)/(p - 1) ∈ {2010, ..., 2019}`.
   - For `p = 2`, `(2⁴ - 1)/(2 - 1) = 15`.

   - For `p = 23`, `(23⁴ - 1)/(23 - 1) = (279841 - 1)/22 = 12720`.

   - For `p = 71`, `(71⁴ - 1)/(71 - 1) = (254121841 - 1)/70 = 3630312`.
   - This is getting too large, and we can stop here.

2. **`m = p * q`**:

#### Observations:
1. Since `2 < a² < 3`, we can deduce that `a` is in the interval `(√2, √3)`, because `a > 0` and `a² > 2` implies `a > √2`, and similarly `a² < 3` implies `a < √3`.
2. The condition `1 / a - floor(1 / a) = a² - floor(a²)` is unusual. Let's denote `f(x) = x - floor(x)`. The condition becomes `f(1 / a) = f(a²)`.
3. The function `f(x)` is periodic with period `1` and `f(x) ∈ [0, 1)` for all real `x`.
4. Given `2 < a² < 3`, we can compute `floor(a²)`:
   - Since `a² ∈ (2, 3)`, `floor(a²) = 2`.
5. Similarly, `1 / a ∈ (1/√3, 1/√2) ≈ (0.577, 0.707)`, so `floor(1 / a) = 0` (since `1 / a > 0.577` and `1 / a < 0.707`).
   - To verify `floor(1 / a) = 0`:
     - `a² < 3` implies `1 / a > 1 / √3 ≈ 0.577` (since `a < √3` and `1 / a` is decreasing).
     - `a² > 2` implies `1 / a < 1 / √2 ≈ 0.707` (since `a > √2` and `1 / a` is decreasing).
     - Thus, `0.577 < 1 / a < 0.707`, so `floor(1 / a) = 0`.
6. Substitute `floor(a²) = 2` and `floor(1 / a) = 0` into the original condition:
   - `1 / a - 0 = a² - 2` ⇒ `1 / a = a² - 2`.

[[proof]]
name = "algebra_sqineq_at2malt1"
iteration = 45
time = 27
proof = """
theorem algebra_sqineq_at2malt1
(a : ℝ) :
a * (2 - a) ≤ 1 := by
  have h_main : a * (2 - a) ≤ 1 := by
    have h₁ : a * (2 - a) = 2 * a - a ^ 2 := by
      ring
    rw [h₁]
    -- We need to show that 2 * a - a ^ 2 ≤ 1
    -- This is equivalent to showing that a ^ 2 - 2 * a + 1 ≥ 0
    -- Which is equivalent to showing that (a - 1) ^ 2 ≥ 0
    -- And since (a - 1) ^ 2 ≥ 0 is always true, the inequality holds.
    nlinarith [sq_nonneg (a - 1), sq_nonneg (a - 1 / 2), sq_nonneg (a + 1 / 2),
      sq_nonneg (a - 2), sq_nonneg (a + 2), sq_nonneg (a - 1 / 2 - 1),
      sq_nonneg (a + 1 / 2 - 1), sq_nonneg (a - 1 / 2 + 1), sq_nonneg (a + 1 / 2 + 1)]
  exact h_main
"""
outline = """
### Detailed Proof

**Problem:** Prove that for any real number \\( a \\), \\( a(2 - a) \\leq 1 \\).

**Approach:**
We can rewrite the inequality \\( a(2 - a) \\leq 1 \\) as \\( 2a - a^2 \\leq 1 \\), or equivalently, \\( a^2 - 2a + 1 \\geq 0 \\). Notice that \\( a^2 - 2a + 1 = (a - 1)^2 \\geq 0 \\) for all real \\( a \\). This is always true because the square of any real number is non-negative.

Alternatively, we can complete the square:
\\[ a(2 - a) = 2a - a^2 = - (a^2 - 2a) = - (a^2 - 2a + 1 - 1) = - (a^2 - 2a + 1) + 1 = - (a - 1)^2 + 1. \\]
Since \\( (a - 1)^2 \\geq 0 \\), we have \\( - (a - 1)^2 \\leq 0 \\), and thus \\( - (a - 1)^2 + 1 \\leq 1 \\). This directly gives \\( a(2 - a) \\leq 1 \\).

**Proof:**
1. Start with the expression \\( a(2 - a) \\).
2. Expand it to get \\( 2a - a^2 \\).
3. Rearrange the inequality to \\( 2a - a^2 \\leq 1 \\), or equivalently, \\( 1 - 2a + a^2 \\geq 0 \\).
4. Notice that \\( 1 - 2a + a^2 = (a - 1)^2 \\geq 0 \\), which is always true.
5. Therefore, the original inequality holds.

Alternatively, we can directly complete the square:
1. \\( a(2 - a) = 2a - a^2 \\).
2. \\( 2a - a^2 - 1 = - (a^2 - 2a + 1) = - (a - 1)^2 \\leq 0 \\).
3. Thus, \\( 2a - a^2 \\leq 1 \\), i.e., \\( a(2 - a) \\leq 1 \\).

**Conclusion:** The inequality \\( a(2 - a) \\leq 1 \\) is always true for all real \\( a \\).

### Step 1: Abstract Plan

1. **Expand the expression:**
   - \\( a(2 - a) = 2a - a^2 \\).

2. **Rearrange the inequality:**
   - The goal is to prove \\( 2a - a^2 \\leq 1 \\), or equivalently, \\( 1 - 2a + a^2 \\geq 0 \\).

3. **Complete the square:**
   - \\( 1 - 2a + a^2 = (a - 1)^2 \\geq 0 \\), which is always true.

4. **Conclude the proof:**
   - Since \\( (a - 1)^2 \\geq 0 \\), we have \\( 1 - 2a + a^2 \\geq 0 \\), and thus \\( 2a - a^2 \\leq 1 \\).

### Step 2: Lean 4 `have` Statements

```lean4
theorem algebra_sqineq_at2malt1
(a : ℝ) :
a * (2 - a) ≤ 1 := by
  have h_main : a * (2 - a) ≤ 1 := by
    sorry
  sorry
```

### Explanation:
- The `have` statement `h_main` directly corresponds to the goal `a * (2 - a) ≤ 1`. The proof of this statement is straightforward by completing the square, as explained above. The `sorry` is a placeholder for the actual proof, which can be filled in by expanding and simplifying the expression.

theorem algebra_apbmpcneq0_aeq0anbeq0anceq0
(a b c : ℚ)
(m n : ℝ)
(h₀ : 0 < m ∧ 0 < n)
(h₁ : m^3 = 2)
(h₂ : n^3 = 4)
(h₃ : (a:ℝ) + b * m + c * n = 0) :
a = 0 ∧ b = 0 ∧ c = 0 := by
  have h_main : a = 0 ∧ b = 0 ∧ c = 0 := by
    have h₄ : (a : ℝ) = -b * m - c * n := by
      have h₄₁ : (a : ℝ) + b * m + c * n = 0 := by exact_mod_cast h₃
      linarith
    have h₅ : a = 0 := by
      have h₅₁ : (a : ℝ) = -b * m - c * n := h₄
      have h₅₂ : (a : ℝ) = 0 := by
        -- Use the fact that m and n are positive to show that a must be 0
        have h₅₃ : 0 < m := h₀.1
        have h₅₄ : 0 < n := h₀.2
        have h₅₅ : 0 < m * n := mul_pos h₅₃ h₅₄
        have h₅₆ : 0 < m * n * m := by positivity
        have h₅₇ : 0 < m * n * n := by positivity
        have h₅₈ : 0 < m * n * m * n := by positivity
        have h₅₉ : 0 < m * n * m * n * m := by positivity
        have h₅₁₀ : 0 < m * n * m * n * n := by positivity
        -- Use the fact that m and n are positive to show that a must be 0
        have h₅₁₁ : (a : ℝ) = -b * m - c * n := h₄₁
        have h₅₁₂ : (a : ℝ) = 0 := by
          -- Use the fact that m and n are positive to show that a must be 0
          field_simp [h₁, h₂] at h₅₁₁ ⊢
          <;>
            (try norm_num at h₅₁₁ ⊢) <;>
            (try nlinarith [sq_nonneg (b * m + c * n), sq_nonneg (b * m - c * n),
              sq_nonneg (b * n - c * m), sq_nonneg (b * n + c * m),
              sq_nonneg (b * m + c * n - 2 * b), sq_nonneg (b * m + c * n + 2 * b),
              sq_nonneg (b * m + c * n - 2 * c), sq_nonneg (b * m + c * n + 2 * c)]) <;>
            (try
              nlinarith [sq_nonneg (b * m + c * n), sq_nonneg (b * m - c * n),
                sq_nonneg (b * n - c * m), sq_nonneg (b * n + c * m),
                sq_nonneg (b * m + c * n - 2 * b), sq_nonneg (b * m + c * n + 2 * b),
                sq_nonneg (b * m + c * n - 2 * c), sq_nonneg (b * m + c * n + 2 * c)])
        exact_mod_cast h₅₁₂
      exact_mod_cast h₅₂
    have h₆ : b = 0 := by
      have h₆₁ : (a : ℝ) = -b * m - c * n := h₄
      have h₆₂ : (a : ℝ) = 0 := by exact_mod_cast h₅
      have h₆₃ : (b : ℝ) * m + (c : ℝ) * n = 0 := by linarith
      have h₆₄ : (b : ℝ) = 0 := by
        -- Use the fact that m and n are positive to show that b must be 0
        have h₆₅ : 0 < m := h₀.1
        have h₆₆ : 0 < n := h₀.2
        have h₆₇ : 0 < m * n := mul_pos h₆₅ h₆₆
        have h₆₈ : 0 < m * n * m := by positivity
        have h₆₉ : 0 < m * n * n := by positivity
        have h₆₁₀ : 0 < m * n * m * n := by positivity
        have h₆₁₁ : 0 < m * n * m * n * m := by positivity
        have h₆₁₂ : 0 < m * n * m * n * n := by positivity
        -- Use the fact that m and n are positive to show that b must be 0
        nlinarith [sq_pos_of_pos h₆₅, sq_pos_of_pos h₆₆, sq_nonneg (b * m + c * n),
          sq_nonneg (b * m - c * n), sq_nonneg (b * n - c * m), sq_nonneg (b * n + c * m)]
      exact_mod_cast h₆₄
    have h₇ : c = 0 := by
      have h₇₁ : (a : ℝ) = -b * m - c * n := h₄
      have h₇₂ : (a : ℝ) = 0 := by exact_mod_cast h₅
      have h₇₃ : (b : ℝ) = 0 := by exact_mod_cast h₆
      have h₇₄ : (b : ℝ) * m + (c : ℝ) * n = 0 := by linarith
      have h₇₅ : (c : ℝ) * n = 0 := by linarith
      have h₇₆ : (c : ℝ) = 0 := by
        -- Use the fact that n is positive to show that c must be 0
        have h₇₇ : 0 < n := h₀.2
        have h₇₈ : (c : ℝ) * n = 0 := by linarith
        have h₇₉ : (c : ℝ) = 0 := by
          apply mul_left_cancel₀ (show (n : ℝ) ≠ 0 by linarith)
          linarith
        exact_mod_cast h₇₉
      exact_mod_cast h₇₆
    exact ⟨h₅, h₆, h₇⟩
  exact h_main
"""
outline = """
### Detailed Proof and Analysis

**Problem Analysis:**
We are given:
1. `m` and `n` are positive real numbers.
2. `m³ = 2` and `n³ = 4`.
3. The equation `a + b * m + c * n = 0` holds in the reals.

We need to prove that `a = 0`, `b = 0`, and `c = 0`.

**Key Observations:**
1. The equation `a + b * m + c * n = 0` is linear in `a`, `b`, and `c` with coefficients `1`, `m`, and `n` respectively.
2. The values of `m` and `n` are constrained by their cubes:
   - `m³ = 2` implies `m = 2^(1/3) = 2^(1/3)`.
   - `n³ = 4` implies `n = 4^(1/3) = (2²)^(1/3) = 2^(2/3)`.
   However, we don't need the exact values of `m` and `n` to solve the problem.
3. The condition `a + b * m + c * n = 0` can be rewritten as `a = -b * m - c * n`.
4. We can substitute this into the other equations to eliminate `a` and derive conditions on `b` and `c`.

**Approach:**
1. Assume for contradiction that `a ≠ 0`, `b ≠ 0`, or `c ≠ 0` and derive a contradiction.
2. Alternatively, we can directly prove that `a = 0`, `b = 0`, and `c = 0` by showing that the only solution to the linear equation is the trivial one.

**Detailed Proof:**

1. From `a + b * m + c * n = 0`, we get `a = -b * m - c * n`.
2. Substitute `a = -b * m - c * n` into the original equation:
   - `a + b * m + c * n = (-b * m - c * n) + b * m + c * n = 0` (which is trivially true).
   - This doesn't give us new information, so we need another approach.
3. Alternatively, consider the equation `a + b * m + c * n = 0` as a vector equation in `ℝ³` with coefficients `(1, m, n)`.
   - The condition `m > 0` and `n > 0` ensures that `(1, m, n)` is a linearly independent set in `ℝ³` (since `m` and `n` are distinct and positive).
   - The only solution to `a + b * m + c * n = 0` is `a = b = c = 0`.
4. To make this precise, we can use the fact that `m` and `n` are positive and distinct (if `m = n`, then `m³ = n³` would imply `m = n`, but `m³ = 2` and `n³ = 4` are not equal, so `m ≠ n`).
   - However, we don't need this here because we can directly use the linear independence of `(1, m, n)`.
5. Alternatively, we can use the fact that `m` and `n` are positive and distinct to derive a contradiction if `a`, `b`, or `c` is non-zero.
   - But we can avoid this by directly proving that `a = b = c = 0` is the only solution.

But a simpler approach is to use the given equations to derive `a = 0`, `b = 0`, and `c = 0` directly.

**Simpler Approach:**
1. Assume `a ≠ 0` for contradiction. Then, from `a + b * m + c * n = 0`, we get `b * m + c * n = -a`.
   - But `m > 0` and `n > 0`, so `b * m + c * n > 0` if `b ≠ 0` or `c ≠ 0`, and `b * m + c * n ≤ 0` if `b = 0` and `c = 0` (but this is impossible because `a = 0` is not allowed).
   - This is not directly helpful, so we need a better approach.

**Better Approach:**
1. The equation `a + b * m + c * n = 0` can be rewritten as `a = -b * m - c * n`.
2. Multiply both sides by `m²` to get `a * m² = -b * m³ - c * m * n²`.
   - But `m³ = 2`, so this becomes `a * m² = -2 * b - c * m * n²`.
3. Similarly, multiply the original equation by `n²` to get `a * n² = -b * m * n² - c * n³`.
   - But `n³ = 4`, so this becomes `a * n² = -b * m * n² - 4 * c`.
4. Multiply the original equation by `m * n` to get `a * m * n = -b * m² * n - c * m * n²`.
5. We now have three equations:
   - `a * m² = -2 * b - c * m * n²`,
   - `a * n² = -b * m * n² - 4 * c`,
   - `a * m * n = -b * m² * n - c * m * n²`.
6. Subtract the second equation from the first:
   `a * m² - a * n² = (-2 * b - c * m * n²) - (-b * m * n² - 4 * c)`.
   Simplifying the right-hand side:
   `-2 * b - c * m * n² + b * m * n² + 4 * c = -2 * b + b * m * n² - c * m * n² + 4 * c`.
   So:
   `a * (m² - n²) = -2 * b + b * m * n² - c * m * n² + 4 * c`.
   Factor `b` and `c`:
   `a * (m² - n²) = b * (m * n² - 2) + c * (-m * n² + 4)`.
   But `m² - n² = (m - n)(m + n)`, and `m * n² - 2` and `-m * n² + 4` are not obviously related.
   This seems complicated, so perhaps we can find a better path.

**Alternative Simpler Approach:**
1. Assume `a ≠ 0` for contradiction.
2. From `a + b * m + c * n = 0`, we get `b * m + c * n = -a`.
   - Since `m, n > 0`, `b * m + c * n > 0` if `b ≠ 0` or `c ≠ 0`, and `b * m + c * n ≤ 0` if `b = 0` and `c = 0`.
   - But `-a` could be positive or negative, so this is not directly helpful.
3. Alternatively, consider the case `b ≠ 0` or `c ≠ 0` and derive a contradiction.
   - Suppose `b ≠ 0`. Then `b * m + c * n = -a` implies `|b * m + c * n| = |a|`.
   - But `m³ = 2` and `n³ = 4` imply `m = 2^(1/3)` and `n = 4^(1/3) = 2^(2/3)`.
   - The exact values are not needed, but we can use inequalities.
   - Since `m > 0` and `n > 0`, we have `b * m + c * n ≥ 0` if `b ≥ 0` and `c ≥ 0`, and `b * m + c * n ≤ 0` if `b ≤ 0` and `c ≤ 0`.
   - But `a = -b * m - c * n`, so `|a| = |b * m + c * n|`.
   - However, we don't have any direct bounds on `a`, `b`, or `c` to derive a contradiction.

**Simpler Correct Approach:**
1. The equation `a + b * m + c * n = 0` can be rewritten as `a = -b * m - c * n`.
2. Substitute into the original equation:
   - `a + b * m + c * n = (-b * m - c * n) + b * m + c * n = 0` (which is trivially true).
   - This doesn't help.
3. Alternatively, consider the case `b ≠ 0` or `c ≠ 0` and derive a contradiction.
   - Suppose `b ≠ 0`. Then `a = -b * m - c * n`.
   - But `m³ = 2` and `n³ = 4` imply `m > 1` and `n > 1` (since `1³ = 1 < 2` and `1³ = 1 < 4`).
   - We can use the fact that `m > 1` and `n > 1` to derive a contradiction.
   - However, we don't have `m > 1` or `n > 1` directly from `m³ = 2` and `n³ = 4`, so this is not straightforward.

**Correct Approach:**
1. The key is to realize that the only solution to `a + b * m + c * n = 0` is `a = b = c = 0` because `m` and `n` are linearly independent in `ℝ³` (since `m > 0` and `n > 0`).
   - Alternatively, we can use the fact that `m` and `n` are distinct (since `m³ ≠ n³` unless `m = n`, but `m³ = 2` and `n³ = 4` are not equal).
   - But we don't need this.
2. A better approach is to use the fact that `m` and `n` are positive and distinct to derive a contradiction if `a`, `b`, or `c` is non-zero.
   - But we can avoid this by directly proving that `a = b = c = 0` is the only solution.

**Final Correct Proof:**
1. Assume `a ≠ 0` for contradiction.
   - Then `a + b * m + c * n = 0` implies `b * m + c * n = -a`.
   - But `m > 0` and `n > 0`, so `b * m + c * n > 0` if `b ≠ 0` or `c ≠ 0`, and `b * m + c * n ≤ 0` if `b = 0` and `c = 0` (but `-a ≤ 0` is not directly helpful).
   - Alternatively, consider the case `b ≠ 0` or `c ≠ 0` and derive a contradiction.
   - Suppose `b ≠ 0`. Then `b * m + c * n = -a`.
   - But `m > 0` and `n > 0`, so `b * m + c * n > 0` if `b ≠ 0` or `c ≠ 0`, and `b * m + c * n ≤ 0` if `b = 0` and `c = 0`.
   - But `-a` could be positive or negative, so this is not directly helpful.
   - Instead, we can use the fact that `m` and `n` are distinct (since `m³ ≠ n³` unless `m = n`, but `2 ≠ 4`).
   - But we don't have `m ≠ n` directly from `m³ = 2` and `n³ = 4`, so this is not straightforward.

**Simpler Correct Approach:**
1. The equation `a + b * m + c * n = 0` can be rewritten as `a = -b * m - c * n`.
2. Substitute into the original equation:
   - `a + b * m + c * n = (-b * m - c * n) + b * m + c * n = 0` (which is trivially true).
   - This doesn't help.
3. Alternatively, consider the case `b ≠ 0` or `c ≠ 0` and derive a contradiction.
   - Suppose `b ≠ 0`. Then `a = -b * m - c * n`.
   - But `m > 0` and `n > 0`, so `a = -b * m - c * n` is a linear combination of `m` and `n` with coefficients `-b` and `-c`.
   - The only way this can hold is if `-b = 0` and `-c = 0`, i.e., `b = 0` and `c = 0`.
   - But if `b = 0` and `c = 0`, then `a = 0`.
   - Hence, the only solution is `a = b = c = 0`.

**Conclusion:**
The only solution to `a + b * m + c * n = 0` with `m > 0`, `n > 0`, and `m³ = 2`, `n³ = 4` is `a = b = c = 0`.

### Step 1: Abstract Plan

outline = """
### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, let's carefully restate the problem in a more familiar form. We have positive integers \\( x, y, n \\) (i.e., \\( x, y, n \\geq 1 \\)) and the equation:
\\[
\\frac{x}{4} + \\frac{y}{6} = \\frac{x + y}{n}
\\]
We need to prove that \\( n = 5 \\).

#### Step 1: Rewrite the Equation
Multiply both sides by \\( 12n \\) (the least common multiple of 4 and 6) to eliminate denominators:
\\[
3n x + 2n y = 12 (x + y)
\\]
This simplifies to:
\\[
3n x + 2n y = 12 x + 12 y
\\]
Rearrange all terms to one side:
\\[
3n x + 2n y - 12 x - 12 y = 0
\\]
Factor out common terms:
\\[
(3n - 12) x + (2n - 12) y = 0
\\]

#### Step 2: Analyze the Equation
Since \\( x, y \\geq 1 \\), the left-hand side is a linear combination of \\( x \\) and \\( y \\). The equation can be rewritten as:
\\[
(3n - 12) x = - (2n - 12) y
\\]
This implies that \\( 3n - 12 \\) divides \\( (2n - 12) y \\). However, this is not directly helpful. Instead, we can consider the equation modulo \\( x \\) or \\( y \\), but it's simpler to consider the possible values of \\( n \\).

#### Step 3: Find Possible Values of \\( n \\)
Since \\( x, y \\geq 1 \\), the left-hand side \\( (3n - 12) x + (2n - 12) y \\) must be non-negative. We can consider cases for \\( n \\geq 1 \\).

However, a better approach is to consider the original equation and find \\( n \\) such that it holds. 

Alternatively, we can treat the original equation as a Diophantine-like equation and solve for \\( n \\). 

But first, let's consider the original equation:
\\[
\\frac{x}{4} + \\frac{y}{6} = \\frac{x + y}{n}
\\]
Multiply through by \\( 12n \\):
\\[
3n x + 2n y = 12 x + 12 y
\\]
Rearrange:
\\[
(3n - 12) x + (2n - 12) y = 0
\\]

This can be rewritten as:
\\[
(3n - 12) x = - (2n - 12) y
\\]

Since \\( x, y \\geq 1 \\), the right-hand side is negative, and the left-hand side is positive (assuming \\( n \\geq 4 \\), because \\( 3n - 12 \\geq 0 \\) for \\( n \\geq 4 \\)). 

But this is not immediately helpful. Instead, let's consider the possible values of \\( n \\).

#### Step 4: Find \\( n \\) by Testing Small Values
We can test small positive integers for \\( n \\):

1. **Case \\( n = 1 \\)**:
   \\[
   \\frac{x}{4} + \\frac{y}{6} = \\frac{x + y}{1}
   \\]
   Simplify:
   \\[
   \\frac{x}{4} + \\frac{y}{6} = x + y
   \\]
   Multiply by 12:
   \\[
   3x + 2y = 12x + 12y
   \\]
   Rearrange:
   \\[
   -9x - 10y = 0 \\implies 9x = -10y
   \\]
   But \\( x, y \\geq 1 \\), so \\( 9x \\geq 9 \\), and \\( -10y \\leq -10 \\), which is a contradiction. Thus, \\( n \\neq 1 \\).

2. **Case \\( n = 2 \\)**:
   \\[
   \\frac{x}{4} + \\frac{y}{6} = \\frac{x + y}{2}
   \\]
   Multiply by 12:
   \\[
   3x + 2y = 6x + 6y
   \\]
   Rearrange:
   \\[
   -3x - 4y = 0 \\implies 3x = -4y
   \\]
   Again, \\( x, y \\geq 1 \\), so \\( 3x \\geq 3 \\), and \\( -4y \\leq -4 \\), which is a contradiction. Thus, \\( n \\neq 2 \\).

3. **Case \\( n = 3 \\)**:
   \\[
   \\frac{x}{4} + \\frac{y}{6} = \\frac{x + y}{3}
   \\]
   Multiply by 12:
   \\[
   3x + 2y = 4x + 4y
   \\]
   Rearrange:
   \\[
   -x - 2y = 0 \\implies x = -2y
   \\]
   But \\( x, y \\geq 1 \\), so \\( x \\geq 1 \\), and \\( -2y \\leq -2 \\), which is a contradiction. Thus, \\( n \\neq 3 \\).

4. **Case \\( n = 4 \\)**:
   \\[
   \\frac{x}{4} + \\frac{y}{6} = \\frac{x + y}{4}
   \\]
   Multiply by 12:
   \\[
   3x + 2y = 3x + 3y
   \\]
   Rearrange:
   \\[
   3x + 2y - 3x - 3y = 0 \\implies -y = 0 \\implies y = 0
   \\]
   But \\( y \\geq 1 \\), so \\( n \\neq 4 \\).

5. **Case \\( n = 5 \\)**:
   \\[
   \\frac{x}{4} + \\frac{y}{6} = \\frac{x + y}{5}
   \\]
   Multiply by 60:
   \\[
   15x + 10y = 12x + 12y
   \\]
   Rearrange:
   \\[
   15x + 10y - 12x - 12y = 0 \\implies 3x - 2y = 0 \\implies 3x = 2y
   \\]
   This implies \\( y = \\frac{3}{2} x \\). Since \\( x, y \\in \\mathbb{N}^+ \\), we can try \\( x = 2 \\), \\( y = 3 \\):
   \\[
   \\frac{2}{4} + \\frac{3}{6} = \\frac{2 + 3}{5} \\implies \\frac{1}{2} + \\frac{1}{2} = 1 \\implies 1 = 1
   \\]
   This works. 

   Alternatively, \\( x = 4 \\), \\( y = 6 \\):
   \\[
   \\frac{4}{4} + \\frac{6}{6} = \\frac{4 + 6}{5} \\implies 1 + 1 = 2 \\implies 2 = 2
   \\]
   This also works. 

   In fact, for any \\( x \\geq 1 \\), \\( y = \\frac{3}{2} x \\geq \\frac{3}{2} \\), but since \\( y \\in \\mathbb{N}^+ \\), we can take \\( x = 2k \\), \\( y = 3k \\), \\( k \\geq 1 \\). 

   Thus, \\( n = 5 \\) is a valid solution.

6. **Case \\( n \\geq 6 \\)**:
   We can check \\( n = 6 \\):
   \\[
   \\frac{x}{4} + \\frac{y}{6} = \\frac{x + y}{6}
   \\]
   Multiply by 12:
   \\[
   3x + 2y = 2x + 2y
   \\]
   Rearrange:
   \\[
   3x + 2y - 2x - 2y = 0 \\implies x = 0
   \\]
   But \\( x \\geq 1 \\), so \\( n \\neq 6 \\).

   Similarly, for \\( n \\geq 6 \\), the equation simplifies to \\( x = 0 \\), which is a contradiction. 

   Alternatively, we can consider the general form:
   \\[
   (3n - 12) x + (2n - 12) y = 0
   \\]
   Since \\( x, y \\geq 1 \\), and \\( 3n - 12 \\geq 6 \\) for \\( n \\geq 6 \\), the left-hand side is positive, which is a contradiction. 

   Therefore, the only possible solution is \\( n = 5 \\).

#### Step 5: Verification
For \\( n = 5 \\), the original equation becomes:
\\[
\\frac{x}{4} + \\frac{y}{6} = \\frac{x + y}{5}
\\]
Multiply by 60:
\\[
15x + 10y = 12x + 12y
\\]
Simplify:
\\[
3x - 2y = 0 \\implies 3x = 2y
\\]
This is satisfied if \\( x = 2k \\), \\( y = 3k \\), \\( k \\geq 1 \\). 

Thus, \\( n = 5 \\) is the unique solution.

### Step 6: Abstract Plan

1. **Multiply through by \\( 12n \\)** to eliminate denominators and obtain:
   \\[
   3n x + 2n y = 12 x + 12 y
   \\]
2. **Rearrange the equation** to:
   \\[
   (3n - 12) x + (2n - 12) y = 0
   \\]
3. **Test small values of \\( n \\)** to find that \\( n = 5 \\) is the only solution.
4. **Verify that \\( n = 5 \\) works** and that no other \\( n \\) is possible.

### Lean 4 `have` Statements
